الكيفيات النابعة من انفعالات النفس (قال: (ومنها الفرح) والغم (۲) والغضب والخوف والحزن والهم ونحو ذلك، ولا بحث فيها . قد تعرض للنفس كيفيات نابعة لانفعالات تحدث فيها لما يرتسم في بعض قواها من النافع والضار كالفرح وهو كيفية نفسانية تتبعها حركة الروح إلى خارج البدن، طلبا للوصول إلى الملذ والغم وهو ما يتبعها حركة الروح إلى الداخل خوفاً من مؤذ واقع والغضب وهو ما يتبعها حركة الروح إلى الخارج طلباً للانتقام، والفزع وهو ما يتبعها حركة الروح إلى الداخل هرباً من المؤذي واقعاً كان أو متخيلاً، والحزن وهو ما يتبعها حركة الروح إلى الداخل قليلاً قليلاً، والهم وهو ما يتبعها حركة الروح إلى الداخل والخارج لحدوث أمر يتصور منه يقع أو شر ينتظر ، فهو مركب من جاء وخوف (۳) فأيهما غلب على الفكر تحركت النفس إلى جهته فللخير المتوقع إلى الخارج والشر المنتظر إلى الداخل، فلذلك قيل : إنه جهاد فكري والخجل وهو ما يتبعها حركة الروح إلى الداخل والخارج، لأنه كالمركب من فزع وفرح حيث ينقبض الروح أولاً إلى الباطن ثم يخطر بباله أن ليس فيه كثير مضرة فينبسط ثانياً، وهذه كلها إشارة إلى ما لكل من الخواص واللوازم وإلا فمعانيها واضحة عند العقل وكثيراً ما يتسامح فيفسر بنفس الانفعالات كما يقال الفرح انبساط القلب، والغم انقباضه والغضب (۱) غليان الدم إلى غير ذلك .
The qualities arising from emotions of the soul. He said: Among them are joy, grief, anger, fear, sorrow, anxiety, and the like, and there is no investigation into them. There may occur to the soul qualities arising from emotions that happen in it due to what is depicted in some of its faculties of beneficial and harmful, such as joy, which is a psychic quality followed by movement of the spirit to the outside of the body, seeking to reach pleasure; and grief, which is what is followed by movement of the spirit inward, fearing an actual harm; and anger, which is followed by movement of the spirit outward seeking revenge; and fright, which is followed by movement of the spirit inward fleeing from a harmful thing, whether actual or imagined; and sorrow, which is followed by movement of the spirit inward little by little; and anxiety, which is followed by movement of the spirit inward and outward due to the occurrence of a matter from which one imagines good occurring or evil awaited, so it is composed of fear and hope. Whichever overcomes thought, the soul moves toward its direction: for anticipated good outward, awaited evil inward. Therefore it is said: it is an intellectual struggle. And shame, which is followed by movement of the spirit inward and outward, because it is like composed of fright and joy: the spirit first contracts inward, then it occurs to one’s mind that there is not much harm, so it expands secondarily. All these are indications of what belongs to each of properties and concomitants; otherwise their meanings are clear to the intellect, and often one is lenient and interprets them by the emotions themselves, as it is said: joy is expansion of the heart, grief is contraction, and anger is boiling of blood, etc.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage defines emotional states (الكيفيات النابعة من انفعالات النفس) by their associated movements of the spirit (الروح). The author begins by listing examples: joy, grief, anger, fear, sorrow, anxiety, and the like, noting that there is no need for further investigation into them. He then explains that these qualities arise from emotions that occur in the soul due to the perception of benefit or harm. Each emotion is defined by a specific movement of the spirit: joy (الفرح) involves movement outward seeking pleasure; grief (الغم) involves movement inward fearing actual harm; anger (الغضب) involves movement outward seeking revenge; fright (الفزع) involves movement inward fleeing from harm, real or imagined; sorrow (الحزن) involves movement inward slowly; anxiety (الهم) involves movement both inward and outward, as it combines fear and hope depending on whether good or evil is anticipated, and is described as an intellectual struggle (جهاد فكري); shame (الخجل) involves movement first inward (contraction) then outward (expansion) after realizing no serious harm. The author concludes that these are indications of the properties and concomitants of these states, while their essential meanings are clear to the intellect, and people often loosely describe them by the emotions themselves, such as joy being expansion of the heart, grief contraction, anger boiling of blood.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Emotions are merely physical sensations (e.g., anger is boiling blood).
Correction: The text defines emotions by the movement of the spirit (الروح) in response to perceptions of benefit or harm, not by physical symptoms.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Emotional states in kalam are analyzed through the direction of the spirit's movement, reflecting a sophisticated psychological theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Shame (الخجل) involves first contracting inward then expanding outward. Recall a time you felt shame. After the initial contraction, did you eventually expand in relief? How can this realization help you turn to Allah in repentance rather than remain stuck in shame?

Definitions of Fear, Shyness, and Anger
Defines three psychic qualities with physical manifestations and a hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars defined fear, shyness, and anger through their bodily and spiritual movements, and why these definitions are grounded in observable properties.

Core Idea
Emotions have physical signatures that reveal their spiritual reality.

This lesson explores how classical scholars defined three fundamental psychic qualities—fear, shyness, and anger—by their observable bodily manifestations. Rather than purely mental states, these emotions are understood through movements of the soul and body. The passage also includes a hadith that deepens the theological understanding of fear in worship.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic text and its English translation carefully. Notice the pattern of defining each emotion by a physical movement.
The passage uses technical terms like 'كيفيات نفسانية' (psychic qualities).
2
Identify the three definitions
Find the definitions for fear, shyness, and anger. Note how each involves a specific movement: fear moves the soul outward/inward, shyness contracts then expands, anger boils the blood.
These definitions are based on 'properties and concomitants' (الخواص واللوازم).
3
Understand the hadith
Read the hadith from Aisha (RA) about the verse 'those who give while their hearts are fearful'. The Prophet clarifies that this fear is about sincerity, not sin.
This hadith illustrates the spiritual dimension of fear.
4
Reflect on the footnote on anger
The footnote quotes Ibn Arafa distinguishing praiseworthy anger (for truth) and blameworthy anger (for other reasons).
Anger is not always negative; it can be justified.

Upon reflection, the soul moves toward it; for good expected is outward and evil anticipated is inward, so it is said: it is intellectual struggle. And shyness is what follows the soul's movement inward and outward, because it is composed of fear and joy, where the soul first contracts inward, then it occurs to the mind that there is no great harm, so it expands secondarily. From Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) said: O Messenger of Allah, 'those who give what they give while their hearts are fearful' – is it the one who steals, drinks wine, and commits fornication? He said: No, O daughter of As-Siddiq, but it is the man who fasts, prays, and gives charity, and fears that it may not be accepted from him. (1) Anger is the boiling of the blood of the heart desiring revenge. Allah Almighty said: 'So they returned with anger.' Ibn Arafa said: Anger in creatures is something inside their hearts, and it may be praiseworthy or blameworthy; the blameworthy is that which is not for the truth.

Commentary
This passage discusses psychic qualities, specifically fear, shyness, and anger. It explains their bodily and spiritual movements: fear drives the soul outward toward anticipated good or inward toward evil; shyness involves alternate contraction and expansion. It then mentions the hadith about fear of Allah, emphasizing that fear is not about committing sins but about sincerity of worship. Qualities Special to Quantity: Definition, Examples, and the Composite Quality of Form
The author defines the third division of quality and lists its subdivisions, including the composite quality of form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third division of quality—qualities special to quantity—including their examples from continuous and discrete quantity, and the composite quality of form (hilqah).

Core Idea
Some qualities exist only through quantity, whether continuous or discrete.

This lesson introduces the third category of quality: those that depend on quantity. The author defines them as accidents that cannot be conceived without a quantitative substrate, provides examples from continuous and discrete quantity, and discusses the composite quality of form (shape and color) that is also considered special to quantity.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of qualities special to quantity
Read the first part where the author defines what is meant by 'qualities special to quantity' and how they are divided into those that require continuous quantity and those that require discrete quantity.
The definition is: 'those whose occurrence in a thing is not conceivable except through continuous or discrete quantity.'
2
Identify examples for continuous quantity
Look for the examples given for continuous quantity: straightness, curvature, concavity, convexity, and angle. Understand that these apply to lines and surfaces.
These are all modes of lines or surfaces.
3
Identify examples for discrete quantity
Find the examples for discrete quantity: evenness and oddness. These pertain to numbers.
Discrete quantity refers to number and can be even or odd.
4
Study the composite quality of form (hilqah)
Read the part where the author discusses the composite quality of shape and color, called al-hilqah, and how it is considered a quality special to quantity due to the unity of shape and color that makes a person beautiful or ugly.
This composite is said to be special to quantity because shape is special to quantity and color is special to the surface.
5
Note the beginning of an objection
The passage ends by starting an objection: one part of this composite (shape) is already a quality special to quantity, so the composite might be redundant. The passage is cut off, so the resolution is not provided.
Only the first aspect of the objection is given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات . أعني التي لا يتصور عروضها لشيء إلا بواسطة الكمية المتصلة كالاستقامة والانحناء، وكالتقعير والتقبيب والزاوية، أو المنفصلة كالزوجية والفردية، وقد يعد منها الحلقة (1) أعني مجموع الشكل واللون باعتبار أن الشكل يختص بالكم لكونه هيئة إحاطة الحد أو الحدود بالجسم ، وكذا اللون فيمن يخصه بالسطح، واعتد بهذا المركب خاصة بما فيه من وحدة بحسبها يتصف الشخص بالحسن والقبح . وهي التي لا يكون عروضها بالذات إلا للكم المتصل كالاستقامة والانحناء للخط (۲) والتقعير (۳) والتقبيب للسطح، وكذا الزاوية على ما سيأتي أو للكم المنفصل كالزوجية والفردية للعدد حتى إن اتصاف الجسم بهذه العوارض لا يكون إلا باعتبار ما فيه من هذه (٤) الكميات وقد يعد من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات الخلقة التي هي عبارة عن مجموع الشكل واللون واستشكل من وجوه : الأول : أن أحد جزئيه أعني الشكل، وإن كان من الكيفيات المختصة بالكم
The third division: qualities special to quantities. That is, those whose occurrence in a thing is not conceivable except through continuous quantity, such as straightness and curvature, concavity and convexity, and angle; or discrete quantity, such as evenness and oddness. And among them may be counted form (al-hilqah), i.e., the combination of shape and color, considering that shape is special to quantity because it is the mode of surrounding of the boundary or boundaries of the body, and likewise color in so far as it is special to the surface. And this composite is considered a particular quality due to the unity therein, by which a person is described as beautiful or ugly. They are those which are not essentially attributed except to continuous quantity, such as straightness and curvature for the line, concavity and convexity for the surface, and likewise the angle as will come; or to discrete quantity, such as evenness and oddness for number, such that the body is described by these accidents only by virtue of the quantities in it. And among the qualities special to quantities may be counted complexion (al-khilqah), which is the combination of shape and color. And an objection is raised from several aspects: First: that one of its parts, namely shape, is already among the qualities special to quantity.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage defines the third division of quality: qualities that only occur through quantity. It divides quantity into continuous (line, surface) and discrete (number). Examples for continuous: straightness, curvature, concavity, convexity, angle. For discrete: evenness, oddness. Then it mentions that some count the combination of shape and color as a quality special to quantity, and that the body is described as beautiful or ugly based on that combination. It then begins an objection: shape itself is already a quality special to quantity, so the composite might be redundant. The passage prepares for further objections.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that shape is independent of quantity, or that color does not depend on surface.
Correction: The author clarifies that shape is the boundary of the body (so it depends on quantity) and color is special to the surface (a quantitative concept).

Key Takeaway

Qualities Specific to Quantities
Defines qualities specific to quantities with examples and the compound case of form and color.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what qualities specific to quantities are and why form and color are considered a compound in this category.

Core Idea
Some qualities only make sense through quantity.

Al-Taftazani continues his classification of qualities by turning to a special group: those that cannot exist without a quantity—either continuous or discrete. Understanding this category clarifies how logicians classify attributes like straightness, curvature, and even the combination of form and color. This lesson unpacks the definition and examples given in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two types of quantities
The passage mentions continuous quantity (al-kammiyya al-muttasila) and discrete quantity (al-kammiyya al-munfasila). Understand their difference.
Continuous: line, surface. Discrete: number.
3
Note the examples for each type
For continuous: straightness, curvature, concavity, convexity, angle. For discrete: evenness, oddness. Relate each to its quantity.
Straightness is for line; concavity for surface; evenness for number.
4
Consider the compound quality 'khilqah'
Al-Taftazani mentions that form and color together are sometimes counted as a quality specific to quantities because form involves quantity and color is said to be specific to surface. Note why they are treated as a single compound.
The compound is considered one type because it provides a unity for describing beauty/ugliness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات. (قال) : (القسم الثالث الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات . أعني التي لا يتصور عروضها لشيء إلا بواسطة الكمية المتصلة كالاستقامة والانحناء، وكالتقعير والتقبيب والزاوية، أو المنفصلة كالزوجية والفردية، وقد يعد منها الخلقة (1) أعني مجموع الشكل واللون باعتبار أن الشكل يختص بالكم لكونه هيئة إحاطة الحد أو الحدود بالجسم ، وكذا اللون فيمن يخصه بالسطح، واعتد بهذا المركب خاصة بما فيه من وحدة بحسبها يتصف الشخص بالحسن والقبح) . وهي التي لا يكون عروضها بالذات إلا للكم المتصل كالاستقامة والانحناء للخط (2) والتقعير (3) والتقبيب للسطح، وكذا الزاوية على ما سيأتي أو للكم المنفصل كالزوجية والفردية للعدد حتى إن اتصاف الجسم بهذه العوارض لا يكون إلا باعتبار ما فيه من هذه (4) الكميات وقد يعد من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات الخلقة التي هي عبارة عن مجموع الشكل واللون
The third division: qualities specific to quantities. (He said): (The third division: qualities specific to quantities. I mean those whose occurrence in something is not conceivable except through continuous quantity, like straightness and curvature, and like concavity and convexity and the angle, or discrete quantity like evenness and oddness. And among them is sometimes counted al-khilqah (1), meaning the combination of form and color, considering that form is specific to quantity because it is the encompassing configuration of the limit or limits of the body, and likewise color according to those who make it specific to the surface; and they considered this compound a special type because of the unity in it by which a person is described as beautiful or ugly). And these are those whose occurrence essentially is only for continuous quantity, like straightness and curvature for the line (2), and concavity (3) and convexity for the surface, and likewise the angle as will come, or for discrete quantity like evenness and oddness for number, such that the description of the body with these accidents is only by consideration of what is in it of these quantities. And sometimes al-khilqah, which is the combination of form and color, is counted among the qualities specific to quantities.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. Al-Taftazani defines the third division of qualities: those that are specific to quantities (al-kayfiyyāt al-mukhtaṣṣah bil-kammiyyāt). These qualities cannot inhere in something except through a quantity, either continuous (like line, surface) or discrete (like number). He gives examples: for continuous quantity: straightness (istiqāmah) and curvature (inḥinā') for a line; concavity (taq‘īr) and convexity (taqbīb) for a surface; and the angle (zāwiyah). For discrete quantity: evenness (zawjiyyah) and oddness (fardiyyah) for number. Then he discusses a special case: the combination of form (shakl) and color (lawn), called al-khilqah. Form is said to belong to quantity because it is the configuration of the boundary(ies) of a body. Color is said by some to be specific to the surface, which is a quantity. The compound is treated as one type because it gives unity of beauty/ugliness. He reiterates that these qualities inhere only through quantity, and that the body's description with these accidents is only via the quantities within it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That khilqah (form and color) is a simple quality
Correction: It is actually a compound of form and color, but counted as one type due to a specific unity.

Checkpoint

A) Straightness
B) Heat
C) Evenness
D) Concavity

Key Takeaway
Qualities specific to quantities depend on continuous quantity (like lines/surfaces) or discrete quantity (like number), and even the combination of form and color is included because of its relation to quantity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You judge people's beauty and ugliness based on their form and color, which this lesson says is a quality dependent on quantity. Are you aware that your perception of others is based on mere accidents? How does this affect your view of a person's true worth?
?
Consider that every physical quality you experience—from a straight line to even numbers—is only possible through quantity. Does this make you reflect on the contingency of the material world? What are you truly attached to: the physical or the immutable?
?
This lesson shows that even your own body's shape and color are accidental qualities. If they are only secondary, what does that say about your identity beyond the physical? How much of your self-worth is tied to these temporary accidents?

Objection: Color as Sensible vs. Quantity-Specific
Al-Taftazani raises and answers the objection that color is a sensible quality, not specific to quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that color is a sensible quality is answered by linking color to the surface as a quantity.

Core Idea
Color can be quantity-specific because it inheres in the surface, which is a quantity.

In this passage, al-Taftazani addresses a key objection to classifying the compound khilqah (form and color) as a quantity-specific quality. The objection argues that color is a sensible quality, not quantity-specific. Al-Taftazani responds by referencing the theory that color is a property of the surface, and the surface is a quantity. This lesson unpacks that objection and answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer.
The answer includes a nuance about color penetrating depth.
2
Identify the core problem
The objection states that form is quantity-specific but color is a sensible quality, which belongs to a different category. Understand why this is a problem for classifying khilqah as quantity-specific.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer uses the premise that color is a property of the surface. The surface is a quantity. Therefore, color is indirectly quantity-specific. Note the final remark that the more apparent view is that color may penetrate depth, but this does not undermine the argument.
This resolves the apparent contradiction between sensible and quantity-specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستشكل من وجوه : الأول : أن أحد جزئية أعني الشكل، وإن كان من الكيفيات المختصة بالكم بناء على كونه عبارة عن هيئة إحاطة حد أي نهاية بالجسم كما في الكرة المحيط بها سطح واحد أو حدود أي نهايات كما في نصف الدائرة والمثلث والمربع ، وغيرهما الأشكال الحاصلة من إحاطة خطين أو أكثر لكن لا خفاء في أن جزءه الآخر من أعني اللون من الكيفيات المحسوسة المقابلة للكيفيات المختصة بالكميات . والجواب أن مبني ذلك على ما قيل إن اللون من خواص السطح، ومعنى كون الجسم ملونا أن سطحه ملون، ولا تنافي بين كون الكيفية محسوسة، وكونها مخصوصة بالكم على ما سبقت الإشارة إليه هذا ولكن الأظهر أن اللون قد ينفذ في عمق الجسم .
And an objection is raised from several aspects: The first: that one of its two parts, meaning form, although it is among the qualities specific to quantity based on its being the configuration of the limit (i.e., extremity) of the body, as in the sphere surrounded by one surface, or limits (i.e., extremities) as in the semicircle, triangle, square, and other shapes resulting from the enclosure of two or more lines, yet there is no concealment that its other part, meaning color, is among the sensible qualities which are opposed to the qualities specific to quantities. And the answer: that this is based on what was said, that color is a property of the surface, and the meaning of a body being colored is that its surface is colored, and there is no contradiction between a quality being sensible and being specific to quantity, as was indicated previously. However, the more apparent view is that color may penetrate into the depth of the body.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer pair. The objection states that the compound khilqah consists of form and color. Form is indeed a quality specific to quantity because it is the configuration of the body's boundary(ies). But color is a sensible quality, which belongs to a different category: sensible qualities are opposed to quantity-specific qualities. Therefore, khilqah cannot be counted among quantity-specific qualities. The answer replies that this objection is based on a specific premise: that color is a property of the surface (sath). The meaning of a body being colored is that its surface is colored. The surface is a quantity (continuous quantity). Hence, color can be considered specific to quantity via the surface. There is no contradiction between a quality being sensible (mahṣūsah) and being specific to quantity. The author then adds a nuance: the more apparent view (al-aẓhar) is that color may also penetrate into the depth of the body, not just the surface, but this does not negate the previous argument.

The Special Status of Form and Color
Al-Taftazani answers the objection that composition yields infinite types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the combination of form and color is treated as a single class of quality due to its special property of producing beauty or ugliness in bodies.

Core Idea
Form and color combine for a special purpose: beauty and ugliness.

In the second objection, the opponent argues that if we allow composition of quantity-specific qualities, we would have infinite types, yet scholars do not count such composites. Al-Taftazani responds by pointing out a key exception: the combination of form and color is special because it produces beauty or ugliness in a body, which is why scholars count it as one type. Let's examine this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the Arabic passage which presents the second objection. The objection says that if we consider the composition of quantity-specific qualities, there would be infinite types, but scholars do not count such composites.
Note the term 'single quality' (الكيفية المفردة).
2
Understand the answer
Read the answer: scholars found a special property (خصوصية) in the combination of form and color – it makes bodies beautiful or ugly – so they counted the compound as one type, unlike other combinations.
This is an exception, not a rule.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Ask yourself why this exception is logical. Because the compound acquires a new attribute (beauty/ugliness) that is not present in the parts separately, it deserves its own classification.
This shows that classification in kalam is based on conceptual unity, not arbitrary enumeration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الكلام في الكيفية المفردة إذ لو اعتبر تركيب الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات بعضها مع البعض لكان هناك أقسام لا تتناهى مع أنهم لم يعتدوا بها ولم يعدوها من أنواعها . والجواب : أنهم لما وجدوا لاجتماع اللون والشكل خصوصية باعتبارها يتصف الجسم بالحسن والقبح (2) عدوا المركب منهما نوعاً واحداً بخلاف مثل اللون أو الضوء مع الاستقامة أو الانحناء، أو الزوجية أو الفردية إلى غير ذلك . (2) الحسن يطلق في عرف العلماء على ثلاثة معان وكذا ضد الحسن وهو القبح الأول : كون الشيء ملائماً للطبع حسن كالحلو، وما كان منافراً له قبيح كالمر وما ليس شئياً منهما فليس بحسن ولا قبيح كأفعال الله تعالى لتنزهه عن الغرض والثاني : كون الشيء صفة كمال وضده القبح وهو كونه صفة نقصان فما يكون صفة كمال كالعلم حسن وما يكون صفة نقصان كالجهل قبيح، والثالث كون الشيء متعلق المدح وضده القبح بمعنى كونه متعلق الذم فما تعلق به المدح يسمي حسناً وما تعلق به الذم يسمى قبيحاً وما لا يتعلق به شيء منهما فهو خارج عنهما .
The second: that the discussion is about the single quality, for if the composition of quantity-specific qualities with each other were considered, there would be infinite divisions, while they (the scholars) did not count them nor classify them as types. And the answer: that because they found for the combination of color and form a property by which the body is described as beautiful and ugly (2), they counted the compound as one type, unlike the combination of color or light with straightness or curvature, or evenness or oddness, and so on. (2) 'Beauty' (al-ḥusn) is used in the custom of the scholars in three senses, and likewise its opposite ugliness (al-qubḥ): first: a thing being agreeable to nature is beautiful like sweetness, and what is repugnant to it is ugly like bitterness, and what is neither is neither beautiful nor ugly, like the actions of God Almighty because He is transcendent above purpose; second: a thing being an attribute of perfection and its opposite is ugliness meaning being an attribute of deficiency, so whatever is an attribute of perfection like knowledge is beautiful, and whatever is an attribute of deficiency like ignorance is ugly; third: a thing being an object of praise and its opposite ugliness meaning being an object of blame, so whatever is praised is called beautiful, and whatever is blamed is called ugly, and what is neither is outside both.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its answer. The objection argues that if we allow composition of quantity-specific qualities, then we would have infinite types, but scholars do not count such composites as types. Hence, the compound of form and color should not be a type. The answer replies that the scholars found a special property (khuṣūṣiyyah) in the combination of form and color: it is the basis for describing a body as beautiful or ugly (ḥusn and qubḥ). Therefore, they made an exception and counted this particular compound as one type. They do not do this for other combinations, like color or light with straightness or curvature, etc., because those do not produce such a characterization. The passage includes a long footnote clarifying the three meanings of 'beauty' (al-ḥusn) and 'ugliness' (al-qubḥ) in scholarly usage: (1) natural agreeability/disagreeability, (2) perfection/deficiency, and (3) praiseworthiness/blameworthiness. This nuance shows that the term in the answer likely refers to the first or second sense, but the author provides the full taxonomy.

الخصوصية
الحسن
القبح

Correction: They count it because it has a special property (beauty/ugliness) that justifies treating it as one type.

Key Takeaway
The combination of form and color is treated as one type because it has a special property: it makes bodies beautiful or ugly.

The Three Meanings of Beauty and Ugliness
Footnote explaining the three scholarly meanings of beauty and ugliness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct senses in which scholars use the terms 'beauty' (al-ḥusn) and 'ugliness' (al-qubḥ).

Core Idea
Beauty and ugliness have three meanings: natural, perfectional, and moral.

In the answer to the second objection, the term 'beauty and ugliness' is used. But these terms have multiple meanings in scholarly discourse. The author provides a footnote clarifying three distinct senses: one based on natural agreeableness, one based on perfection versus deficiency, and one based on praise versus blame. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping the full force of the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote
Read the Arabic passage starting at '(2)' which explains the three meanings of beauty and ugliness.
2
Identify the first meaning
The first meaning is natural agreeableness (e.g., sweetness is beautiful, bitterness is ugly). Note that this does not apply to God's actions.
God is beyond purposes, so His actions are neither beautiful nor ugly in this sense.
3
Identify the second meaning
The second meaning is perfection versus deficiency: attributes like knowledge are beautiful (perfect), ignorance is ugly (deficient).
This is about intrinsic qualities.
4
Identify the third meaning
The third meaning is praiseworthiness versus blameworthiness: what is praised is beautiful, what is blamed is ugly.
This is related to moral judgment.
5
Connect to the answer
Consider which sense of beauty/ugliness applies to the combination of form and color in a body. Likely the first (natural agreeableness) or second (perfection) sense, as form and color produce aesthetic qualities.
This shows how the footnote supports the answer.

Third Objection: Khilqah Requires Natural Body
Al-Taftazani presents the third objection and begins the response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why khilqah (the compound of form and color) is argued to require a natural body in conception, unlike abstract mathematical qualities, and how al-Taftazani begins to respond.

Core Idea
Khilqah cannot be conceived without a natural body; abstract quantities can.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents the third objection against counting khilqah as a quantity-specific quality. The objector claims that khilqah depends on a natural body in both existence and conception, while mathematical qualities like straightness can be conceived without matter. The answer begins by distinguishing between two types of quantity accidents, but the text cuts off before completing the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the objector's claim: khilqah requires a natural body in conception, unlike qualities specific to quantity.
The objection relies on the division of wisdom into natural, mathematical, and divine.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice the contrast: khilqah needs a natural body for its very conception, while quantity qualities (like straightness) need matter only for existence, not for conception.
This distinction is the heart of the objection.
3
Examine the beginning of the answer
Read al-Taftazani's reply. He says that among the accidents of quantity, some are accidental because they are quantity (e.g., straightness, evenness). These are studied in the mathematical division.
The answer is incomplete; it ends with 'في قسم' (in the division…).

Reflect on the incomplete answer

This lesson only covers the objection and the start of the answer; the full answer appears later.

Objection and Answer on Color as a Sensible Quality
Objection and answer on color's classification as a sensible quality and its relation to quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological objection that color as a sensible quality seems to contradict its being specific to quantity, and how the answer resolves this by grounding color in the surface, while also considering the view that color can penetrate depth.

Core Idea
Color is a surface property, but it may also penetrate depth.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential conflict in the classification of qualities: sensible qualities like color appear to belong to a different category than qualities specific to quantities. The objection is raised and answered, clarifying that color is primarily a property of the surface, which is a quantitative aspect, and that this does not contradict its sensible nature. The author further notes a more apparent view that color can also exist in the depth of bodies, adding nuance to the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the objection that is being raised and the answer given.
The objection contrasts sensible qualities with qualities specific to quantities.
2
Understand the surface property explanation
The answer states that color is a property of the surface; a body's color is its surface color. This links color to quantity (surface area) without negating its sensible nature.
The author references a previous indication that there is no contradiction.
3
Note the additional clarification
The author adds that the more apparent view is that color can penetrate into the depth of the body, which is a point of discussion.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Think about why this distinction matters in kalam. It relates to how qualities are categorized and whether they are intrinsic or accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعني اللون من الكيفيات المحسوسة المقابلة للكيفيات المختصة بالكميات . والجواب أن مبني ذلك على ما قيل إن اللون من خواص السطح، ومعنى كون الجسم ملونا أن سطحه ملون، ولا تنافي بين كون الكيفية محسوسة، وكونها مخصوصة بالكم على ما سبقت الإشارة إليه هذا ولكن الأظهر أن اللون قد ينفذ في عمق الجسم .
I mean color from the sensible qualities contrasted with the qualities specific to quantities. And the answer: that is based on what was said: that color is from the properties of the surface, and the meaning of a body being colored is that its surface is colored, and there is no contradiction between the quality being sensible and its being specific to quantity, as previously indicated. This, but the more apparent is that color may penetrate into the depth of the body.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the classification of color. The objection implies that color, being a sensible quality, is contrasted with qualities specific to quantities (such as shape). The answer explains that color is a property of the surface; a body is said to be colored because its surface is colored. This does not conflict with the idea that color is a sensible quality while also being specific to quantity (as surface is a quantity). The author adds that the more apparent view is that color can penetrate into the depth of the body, suggesting it is not limited to the surface.

Second Objection: Composition of Qualities Leads to Infinite Divisions
Objection and answer about composition of qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that composition of qualities leads to infinite divisions and why scholars only count certain compositions as distinct types.

Core Idea
Not all combinations of qualities count as types—only those with special properties.

This passage presents a second objection: if we consider the composition of qualities, there would be infinite divisions, yet scholars did not enumerate all of them. The answer explains that only compositions with a special property, such as the combination of color and shape giving rise to beauty or ugliness, are counted as distinct types. This lesson unpacks this objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the objection's claim that composition leads to infinite divisions.
Notice the key term 'كيفية مفردة' (single quality).
2
Understand the problem
The objection argues that if all compositions were considered types, the number would be limitless. Why would that be a problem for scholars?
3
Read the answer
The answer restricts counting to compositions with a special property. Identify the example given.
Pay attention to 'خصوصية' (special property).
4
Reflect on the example
Why is color+shape counted but not color+straightness? The special property here is beauty/ugliness. Consider how this principle applies.
This shows that not all combinations are created equal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الكلام في الكيفية المفردة إذ لو اعتبر تركيب الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات بعضها مع البعض لكان هناك أقسام لا تتناهى مع أنهم لم يعتدوا بها ولم يعدوها من أنواعها . والجواب : أنهم لما وجدوا لاجتماع اللون والشكل خصوصية باعتبارها يتصف الجسم بالحسن والقبح عدوا المركب منهما نوعاً واحداً بخلاف مثل اللون أو الضوء مع الاستقامة أو الانحناء، أو الزوجية أو الفردية إلى غير ذلك .
The second: that the discussion is about the single quality, for if the composition of qualities specific to quantities with each other were considered, there would be infinite divisions, while they did not count them nor consider them among its types. And the answer: that when they found that the combination of color and shape has a special property by which the body is described as beautiful or ugly, they counted the composite of the two as one type, unlike such as color or light with straightness or curvature, or evenness or oddness, etc.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage contains the second objection and its answer. The objection argues that if we consider the composition of qualities specific to quantities (such as color, shape, etc.), there would be infinite combinations, yet scholars did not enumerate all of them as separate types. The answer explains that scholars only count as a distinct type those compositions that have a special property, such as the combination of color and shape that gives rise to beauty or ugliness. Other combinations, like color with straightness or evenness, do not form a distinct type.

Third Objection: Khilqa Requires a Natural Body
Presents the third objection and its answer regarding 'khilqa' and natural bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that 'khilqa' requires a natural body is answered by distinguishing two kinds of accidental properties of quantity.

Core Idea
Not all qualities of quantity depend on natural bodies — only those tied to specific things.

This passage presents a concise objection against the existence of certain qualities without natural bodies. The author responds by clarifying the nature of accidental properties of quantity, showing that some are universal to quantity itself while others are specific to particular quantities. This distinction resolves the confusion about whether 'khilqa' is purely mathematical.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: why does 'khilqa' require a natural body while other qualities of quantity do not? Notice the reference to the division of wisdom.
The objection assumes that 'khilqa' is like shape but tied to natural bodies.
2
Identify the answer's two parts
The answer first distinguishes two kinds of accidental properties of quantity: those accidental because it is quantity, and those accidental because it is the quantity of a particular thing.
The first kind includes straightness, curvature, evenness, oddness.
3
Understand the classification
Realize that the second kind (e.g., specific shape) does not contradict being specific to quantity—it just adds a relation to a particular thing.
4
Reflect on the concluding note
The author notes the uncertainty about 'halqa' and offers his opinion that it is a combination resulting in a separate type.
This shows the ongoing discussion in the tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن عروض الخلقة لا يتصور إلا حيث يكون هناك جسم طبيعي بخلاف الكيفيات المختصة بالكم، فإنها إنما تفتقر إلى المادة في الوجود دون التصور على ما تقرر في تقسيم الحكمة إلى الطبيعي والرياضي والإلهي . والجواب : أن الأمور العارضة للكمية منها ما هي عارضة لها بسبب أنها كمية كالاستقامة والانحناء والزوجية والفردية وهي المبحوث عنها في قسم الرياضيات، ومنها ما هي عارضة لها بسبب أنها كمية شيء مخصوص وهذا لا ينافي الاختصاص بالكم، واعلم أن كلامهم متردد في أن الحلقة الشكل واللون أو الشكل المنضم إلى اللون، أو كيفية حاصلة من اجتماعهما، وهذا أقرب إلى جعلهما نوعاً على حدة.
The third: that the occurrence of 'khilqa' (natural form) is inconceivable except where there is a natural body, unlike the qualities specific to quantity, for they only need matter in existence, not in conception, as established in the division of wisdom into natural, mathematical, and divine. And the answer: that among the accidental things of quantity, some are accidental to it because it is quantity, like straightness, curvature, evenness, and oddness—these are investigated in the mathematical division; and some are accidental to it because it is the quantity of a particular thing, and this does not contradict being specific to quantity. And know that their speech is undecided about whether 'halqa' (ring) is shape and color, or shape joined to color, or a quality resulting from their combination; and the latter is closer to making them a separate type.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage contains the third objection and its answer. The objection states that 'khilqa' (natural form or shape) can only occur in a natural body, while qualities specific to quantity (like shape) do not require a natural body for conception—they only need matter for existence. This is based on the division of wisdom into natural, mathematical, and divine. The answer distinguishes between two kinds of accidental properties of quantity: those that are accidental to quantity as such (e.g., straightness, curvature, evenness, oddness) and those that are accidental because they are the quantity of a particular thing (e.g., the specific shape of a certain body). The latter does not contradict being specific to quantity. The passage concludes with a note that scholars are undecided about whether 'halqa' (ring) is shape and color together, or shape alone, or a quality resulting from their combination; the author leans toward considering the combination as a separate type.

Correction: The objection assumes this, but the answer shows that shape as a quality of quantity does not always require a natural body for conception; only shape tied to a particular thing does.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The objection is resolved by distinguishing universal accidental properties of quantity from those tied to particular things, showing that 'khilqa' falls into the latter category.

Shape: Quality or Position? The Majority View
Sanusi presents two views on shape, defines the majority view, and starts refuting Thabit ibn Qurra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the majority of scholars classify shape as a quality rather than a quantity or position, based on the definition and examples provided.

Core Idea
Shape is a quality because it is the configuration from boundaries, not the surface itself.

This lesson tackles a key classification in Islamic ontology: is shape a quality, a quantity, or a position? The majority view, which al-Sanusi endorses, holds that shape belongs to the category of qualities. To understand why, we need to examine how shape is defined and how it differs from the surfaces it applies to. The passage also begins to refute an alternative view by Thabit ibn Qurra.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views
Read the first part of the passage to find the two competing opinions on the classification of shape.
One view says shape is from position; the majority says it is from qualities.
2
Understand the majority definition
Focus on the definition: shape is the configuration resulting from the enclosure of a boundary or boundaries by a body. Notice that it is not the specific surface itself, which would be quantity.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the definitions of circle and triangle. They describe surfaces, but the shape itself (roundness, triangularity) is a quality that occurs to those surfaces.
This shows that shape is not the surface but the quality of the surface.
4
Note the refutation
The passage begins to refute Thabit ibn Qurra's view that shape is position. Notice that the author says shape is not the configuration from relations of parts. The argument cuts off, but the direction is clear.
The refutation is incomplete in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل من الكيفيات (قال: (وبعضهم) على أن الشكل (۱) من الوضع . الجمهور على أن الشكل من الكيفيات بناء على أنه الهيئة الحاصلة من إحاطة الحد أو الحدود بالجسم لا نفس السطح المخصوص ليكون من الكم على ما يتوهم من تقسيمه إلى الدائرة والمثلث والمربع ،وغيرهما، ثم تفسير الدائرة بأنه سطح محيط به خط في وسطه يكون جميع الخطوط الخارجة منها إلى ذلك الخط متساوية. وتفسير المثلث بأنه سطح محيط به ثلاثة خطوط وهكذا، وذلك لأن الشكل ههنا بمعنى المشكل، وأما حقيقته فإنما تنقسم إلى الاستدارة والتثليث والتربيع وهي الكيفيات الحاصلة للسطوح المذكورة، وليس أيضاً عبارة عن الهيئة الحاصلة بسبب نسبة أجزاء الجسم بعضها إلى بعض أو إلى الأمور الخارجة ليكون من قبيل الوضع على ما زعم ثابت بن قرة (۳) ، ومال إليه الإمام، وذلك لأن
Shape is from the qualities. (He said: And some hold that shape is from position. The majority hold that shape is from the qualities, based on the fact that it is the configuration resulting from the enclosure of a boundary or boundaries by the body, not the specific surface itself so as to be from quantity, as might be imagined from its division into circle, triangle, square, etc. Then the definition of the circle: a surface enclosed by a line, in the middle of which all lines drawn from it to that line are equal. And the definition of the triangle: a surface enclosed by three lines, and so on. This is because shape here means that which is shaped; as for its reality, it divides into roundness, triangularity, and squareness—these are the qualities that occur to the mentioned surfaces. Also, it is not the configuration resulting from the relation of the parts of the body to each other or to external things, so as to be of the category of position, as claimed by Thabit ibn Qurra, and al-Imam inclined to it, and that is because
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of shape (shakl). The author presents two views: some say shape belongs to the category of position (wad'), but the majority (jumhur) hold that it belongs to qualities (kayfiyyat). The majority argue that shape is the configuration resulting from the enclosure of a boundary or boundaries by a body, not the specific surface itself (which would be quantity). They support this by defining geometric figures: a circle is a surface enclosed by a line with equal radii; a triangle is a surface enclosed by three lines; etc. The author explains that 'shape' here means 'the shaped' (al-mushakkal), and its reality (haqiqa) divides into roundness, triangularity, and squareness—these are the qualities that occur to those surfaces. The author refutes Thabit ibn Qurra's view that shape is the configuration from the relation of body parts to each other or to external things, thus being position. The text ends mid-sentence with 'because' (li-anna), indicating the refutation continues but is cut off.

Shape as Quality: Definition and Classification
Author defines shape and resolves its category among quality, position, or quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of shape in Islamic logic and why the majority classifies it as a quality rather than position or quantity.

Core Idea
Shape is a configuration, not a surface.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid tackles the definition of 'shape' (al-shakl), a key concept in logic and kalam. The author navigates competing views and clarifies that shape is a quality resulting from boundaries, not the surface itself. You will see how he defends the majority position and refines the definition through objections and answers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the flow from definition to objections.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the majority view and their argument
Find where the author states the majority opinion and their reasoning. Why do they say shape is a quality?
The key phrase: 'al-hay'a al-hasila min ihatat al-hadd'.
3
Examine the objections and answers
Look at the objections: one about relation, another about bodily vs. surface shapes. See how the author responds.
The answer to the second objection introduces 'al-jism al-ta'limi'.
4
Consider the final definition and hesitation
Read the final verification and note the hesitation about whether the inherent quantity is the limits, surface, or body.
This shows the depth of scholarly discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل من الكيفيات. وبعضهم على أن الشكل من الوضع. الجمهور على أن الشكل من الكيفيات بناء على أنه الهيئة الحاصلة من إحاطة الحد أو الحدود بالجسم لا نفس السطح المخصوص ليكون من الكم على ما يتوهم من تقسيمه إلى الدائرة والمثلث والمربع وغيرهما، ثم تفسير الدائرة بأنه سطح محيط به خط في وسطه يكون جميع الخطوط الخارجة منها إلى ذلك الخط متساوية. وتفسير المثلث بأنه سطح محيط به ثلاثة خطوط وهكذا، وذلك لأن الشكل ههنا بمعنى المشكل، وأما حقيقته فإنما تنقسم إلى الاستدارة والتثليث والتربيع وهي الكيفيات الحاصلة للسطوح المذكورة، وليس أيضاً عبارة عن الهيئة الحاصلة بسبب نسبة أجزاء الجسم بعضها إلى بعض أو إلى الأمور الخارجة ليكون من قبيل الوضع على ما زعم ثابت بن قرة، ومال إليه الإمام، وذلك لأن الحدود ليست أجزاء للجسم ولا للسطح. فإن قيل: النسبة مأخوذة في مفهومه ولا شيء من الكيف كذلك. أجيب بمنع الصغرى، وإنما يتم لو كان المذكور في تعريفه حداً حقيقياً له. واعترض على تعريفه بأنه إنما يتناول الأشكال الجسمية دون السطحية. وأجيب بأن المراد بالجسم ههنا هو التعليمي لأنه بالذات معروض الحدود السطحية كما أن السطح معروض الحدود الخطية، وإنما خص التعليمي بالذكر دون الخط والسطح لأنه الذي يمكن تخيله بشرط لا شيء بخلافهما. فالتحقيق أن الشكل هيئة إحاطة الحد أو الحدود بالسطح أو الجسم. والحدود على الأول خطوط، وعلى الثاني سطوح، والكمية المعروضة بالذات للشكل هو الحدود المحيطة أم السطح أم الجسم المحاط فيه تردد.
Shape belongs to qualities. And some hold that shape belongs to position. The majority hold that shape belongs to qualities based on the fact that it is the configuration resulting from the encompassing of the limit or limits around the body, not the specific surface itself so as to belong to quantity as might be imagined from its division into circle, triangle, square, etc., and then the interpretation of the circle as a surface surrounded by a line in its middle such that all lines extending from it to that line are equal, and the interpretation of the triangle as a surface surrounded by three lines, and so on. This is because shape here means 'shaped thing', but its reality is only divided into roundness, triangulation, and quadrature, which are the qualities produced in the mentioned surfaces. Nor is it an expression of the configuration resulting from the relation of the parts of the body to each other or to external matters so as to belong to the category of position, as was claimed by Thabit ibn Qurra and inclined to by the Imam, because limits are not parts of the body nor of the surface. If it is said: relation is included in its concept, and nothing from quality is like that. The answer is: we deny the minor premise, and it would only hold if what was mentioned in its definition was a real definition of it. And it was objected against his definition that it only covers bodily shapes, not surface shapes. And it was answered that what is meant by 'body' here is the mathematical body, because it is in itself the substrate of surface limits just as the surface is the substrate of linear limits, and only the mathematical body was specified for mention, not the line and the surface, because it can be imagined without condition, unlike them. So the verification is that shape is the configuration of the encompassing of the limit or limits around the surface or the body. And the limits in the first case are lines, and in the second case are surfaces. And whether the quantity inherent in shape is the encompassing limits or the surface or the body encompassed is subject to hesitation.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage defines 'shape' (al-shakl) and discusses its categorical classification. The author begins by noting a disagreement: some consider shape to belong to position (wad'), while the majority (al-jumhur) hold it belongs to qualities (kaifiyyat). The majority argue that shape is the configuration (hay'a) resulting from the enclosure of a limit or limits around a body, not the surface itself. If it were the surface, it would be a quantity. The author explains that shape's reality divides into roundness, triangulation, etc., which are qualities. He refutes the position view by noting that limits are not parts of the body or surface, so no relation there. An objector claims relation is part of shape's concept while no quality includes relation; the answer is that the definition is not a real definition. Another objection is that the definition only covers bodily (3-dimensional) shapes, not planar; the answer is that 'body' here means the mathematical body (al-jism al-ta'limi), which is the substrate of surfaces. The final verified definition is: shape is the configuration of the limit(s) encompassing the surface or body. Whether the inherent quantity is the limits, the surface, or the body is debated.

Key Terms
الشكل (shape)
الكيفيات (qualities)
الوضع (position)
الهيئة (configuration)
إحاطة (encompassing)
الحد (boundary)
الجسم (body)
السطح (surface)
التعليمي (mathematical body)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shape is the surface itself (e.g., a circle is a surface enclosed by a line).
Correction: Shape is the configuration (hay'a) produced by the enclosure of limits, not the surface. The reality of shape divides into qualities like roundness, not into surfaces.

Checkpoint
According to the majority, shape is classified under which category?
A) Quantity
B) Position
C) Quality
D) Substance

Key Takeaway
Shape in Islamic logic is defined as the configuration resulting from the encompassing of boundaries around a body or surface, and it is categorized as a quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you mistake the outer form of something for its true reality? This lesson shows that shape is not the surface itself but the configuration of boundaries. In your spiritual life, where might you be confusing outward appearances with inner truths? What practical step can you take today to look beyond surfaces?
?
The author refutes a mistaken view about shape's category. Think of a belief you hold about Islam that you have never questioned. Are you willing to examine your foundational definitions with the same logical rigor used here? What would it cost you to be wrong?
?
Precision in defining terms is crucial in Islamic theology. In your own understanding of Allah's attributes, have you been careful to define what you mean by terms like 'power' or 'mercy'? How might a sloppy definition lead to a distorted belief? Make a specific commitment to investigate one term you use frequently.

The Claim That Angle Is a Quantity
Author presents the claim that angle is quantity and its definition as a surface.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that angle belongs to quantity and how it is defined as a surface by its proponents.

Core Idea
Angle is considered quantity due to its divisibility, but that claim is vulnerable.

The author begins by presenting the view that angle belongs to the category of quantity because it can be divided. Proponents define it as a surface bounded by two lines meeting at a single point. This lesson unpacks that claim and its definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author presents the claim and definition.
The passage uses technical terms like 'الكم' and 'القسمة'.
2
Identify the claim and definition
Find the sentence that states the claim (angle is from quantity) and the definition (surface bounded by two lines). Note the condition 'without the lines coinciding'.
The definition includes the idea that the two lines meet at a single point.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Ask yourself why divisibility is used to classify angle as quantity. The author will later challenge this reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والزاوية من الكم لقبولها القسمة، ففسرت بسطح أحاط به خطان يلتقيان على نقطة واحدة من غير أن يتحدا. والمراد أنها ما يلي تلك النقطة من السطح على ما صرح به من قال هي من ذلك السطح. ورد بأنه يجوز أن يكون قبولها القسمة لا لذاتها. كيف وقد انتفى فيها لازم الكم، وهو عدم البطلان بالتضعيف. ولذا فسرت بهيئة إحاطة الخطين بالسطح عند الملتقى. يعني ذهب بعضهم إلى أن الزاوية من الكميات لكونها قابلة للقسمة بالذات، ففسروها بسطح يحيط به خطان يلتقيان على نقطة واحدة من غير أن يتحد الخطان. وهذا مراد من قال إنها سطح ينتهي إلى نقطة، ولا خفاء في أن هذا صادق على غير موضع تماس الخطين أيضاً من الشكل، وليس بزاوية فمرادهم أنها =.
And the angle belongs to quantity because of its acceptance of division. So it was interpreted as a surface encompassed by two lines meeting at a single point without the two lines coinciding. And what is meant is that which adjoins that point of the surface, according to what was stated by whoever said it is from that surface. And it was refuted that its acceptance of division may not be due to itself. How so? The necessary property of quantity is absent in it, namely, not being nullified by multiplication. Therefore, it was interpreted as the configuration of the two lines encompassing the surface at the meeting point. That is, some went to that the angle is among quantities because it is divisible in itself, so they interpreted it as a surface encompassed by two lines meeting at a single point without the two lines coinciding. And this is what is meant by whoever said it is a surface ending at a point. And there is no concealment that this applies to other than the place of contact of the two lines also in the shape, and it is not an angle, so what they mean is that it is = .
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage defines 'angle' (al-zawiya) and debates its classification as a quantity. Some argued that angle belongs to quantity because it is divisible, defining it as a surface bounded by two lines meeting at one point. However, the author refutes this: divisibility may be accidental, not essential to angle, and angle lacks a necessary property of quantity—specifically, it can be nullified by multiplication (perhaps meaning that multiplying an angle can produce a line or shape that is not an angle). Therefore, the author states that angle is better defined as the configuration (hay'a) of the two lines encompassing the surface at the meeting point. The author then elaborates on the view of those who consider angle a quantity: they define it as a surface bounded by two lines meeting at a point. But this definition also applies to other parts of the shape, not just the angle, so their intended meaning is the surface adjacent to the point. The passage ends with a break, but the main point is established: angle is not a quantity but a configuration.

Key Takeaway
Proponents of angle as quantity define it as a surface bounded by two lines meeting at a point, based on its divisibility.

?

How might focusing on the 'surface near the point'—the essence of action—transform your practice starting today?
?
The definition of angle is precise: it is the surface that adjoins the meeting point. Is your understanding of your relationship with Allah similarly precise, or have you allowed vague assumptions to define your faith? What is one area where you need to move from general concepts to specific, actionable closeness?

Refuting the Quantity Classification and Defining Angle as Configuration
Author refutes the quantity classification and provides a new definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author refutes the classification of angle as quantity and the correct definition as a configuration.

Core Idea
Angle is not a quantity but a configuration of lines at the meeting point.

The author now refutes the view that angle is a quantity, arguing that its divisibility is accidental and it lacks a necessary property of quantity. Instead, angle is defined as a configuration (hay'a) of two lines at the meeting point. This lesson explores the refutation and the new definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage. Notice the refutation begins with 'ورد بأنه' (and it was refuted). Then find the two reasons given: divisibility may be accidental, and angle lacks the necessary property of quantity.
The necessary property is 'عدم البطلان بالتضعيف' (not being nullified by multiplication).
2
Understand the new definition
After refuting, the author gives a new definition: 'فسرت بهيئة إحاطة الخطين بالسطح عند الملتقى' (interpreted as the configuration of the two lines encompassing the surface at the meeting point). This defines angle as a configuration, not a substance.
This shifts the focus from a measurable quantity to a relational property.
3
Reflect on the argument
Consider why the author thinks divisibility is not essential. Then note that the alternative definition avoids the problems of the quantity view.

Angle as a Quality: Definition and Proof
Definition of the plane angle and proof it is a quality, not a quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of an angle as a quality (kayfiyya) and the rational argument that it is not a quantity (kamm).

Core Idea
An angle is a state, not a quantity—it annuls by doubling.

This lesson examines a key distinction in classical kalam: an angle is not a quantity but a quality. The passage presents a sophisticated argument based on the effect of doubling and concludes that the angle is a state (hay'a) at the meeting of two lines. We will follow the logic and see why mathematicians sometimes speak of it as quantity only as a metonymy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Pay attention to the structure: objection, answer, major and minor premises.
2
Understand the objection and rebuttal
The objector claims that the convex point on a surface is divisible essentially. The rebuttal says divisibility is by substrate, and even if allowed, the angle is annulled by doubling—unlike quantities.
3
Trace the proof that doubling annuls an angle
Follow the minor premise: an acute angle becomes right or obtuse; the right angle becomes a straight line; the obtuse also becomes a straight line. Thus the angle is annulled—unlike a quantity.
Doubling here means adding an equal amount.
4
Identify the investigators' definition
The investigators (al-muḥaqqiqūn) define the plane angle as the state (hay'a) occurring at the meeting of two lines that surround a surface and meet at a point.
This is the core positive definition.
5
Distinguish plane from solid angle
The passage ends by describing the solid angle as a body surrounded by two surfaces meeting at a line, or the state at that meeting.
This completes the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يلي تلك النقطة من السطح على ما صرح من به قال إنها المنحدب أي موضع الانحداب من السطح الذي يحيط به خطان يلتقيان على نقطة . وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم أن قبولها القسمة بالذات بل بواسطة معروضها الذي هو السطح، ولو سلم فعندنا ما ينفي كونها من الكم ، وهو أنها تبطل بالتضعيف، ولا شيء من الكم كذلك. أما الكبرى فلأن التضعيف زيادة في الكم لا إبطال له . وأما الصغرى. فلأن الحادة بالتضعيف مرة أو مراراً إلى قائمة أو منفرجة، تنتهي وكل منهما يبطل بالتضعيف مرة: أما القائمة فلا لتقاء الخطين على استقامة بحيث يصيران خطاً واحداً . وأما المنفرجة فلتاديهما إلى ذلك، لأن تضعيف الكم عبارة عن زيادة مثله عليه. ولا يتصور ذلك إلا بزيادة كل ما هو أقل منه، فلا بد في تضعيف المنفرجة من زيادة القدر الذي يكون اتصال الخطين عنده على استقامة فتبطل المنفرجة بالضرورة، وحدوث الحادة في الجانب الآخر لا ينافي ذلك ، وأيضاً لا شك أن الزاوية جنس قريب للثلاثة، فإذا لم تكن القائمة من الكم لم يكن الأخريان منه والمحققون على أنها من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات فلذا فسروها بالهيئة الحاصلة عند ملتقى الخطين المحيطين بالسطح الملتقيين على نقطة، وما يقع في عبارات المهندسين من كونها سطحاً وقابلاً للتجزي والمساواة والمقاومة بالذات فميني على أنهم يريدون بالزاوية ذا الزاوية كما يريدون بالشكل المشكل فيقولون المثلث شكل تحيط به ثلاثة أضلاع وما ذكر اقليدس من أن الزاوية تماس الخطين فمعناه الهيئة الحاصلة عند تماسهما هذا هو الزاوية المسطحة . وأما المجسمة فهي جسم يحيط به سطحان يلتقيان بخط أو الهيئة الحاصلة عند ذلك
What follows that point of the surface according to what he clarified from it, he said that it is the convex, i.e., the place of convexity from the surface which is surrounded by two lines meeting at a point. And it was answered that we do not grant that it accepts division essentially, but rather by means of its substrate which is the surface; and if it were granted, we have that which negates its being from quantity, which is that it is annulled by doubling, and nothing of quantity is like that. As for the major [premise], because doubling is an increase in quantity, not an annulment of it. And as for the minor [premise], because the acute [angle] by doubling once or repeatedly becomes a right or obtuse [angle], and each of them is annulled by doubling once: as for the right, because the two lines meet in a straight line such that they become one line. And as for the obtuse, because they lead to that, because doubling of quantity means adding its like to it. That is only conceivable by adding all that is less than it, so in doubling the obtuse it is necessary to add the amount at which the two lines connect in a straight line, so the obtuse is necessarily annulled, and the occurrence of the acute on the other side does not contradict that. Also, there is no doubt that the angle is a proximate genus for the three, so if the right is not from quantity, the other two are not from it. And the investigators [hold] that it is among the qualities specific to quantities; therefore they defined it as the state (al-hay'a) occurring at the meeting point of the two lines surrounding the surface and meeting at a point. And what occurs in the expressions of the mathematicians about its being a surface and essentially receptive to division, equality, and resistance is based on that they intend by 'angle' the thing having the angle, just as they intend by 'figure' the figured thing, so they say: 'The triangle is a figure surrounded by three sides.' And what Euclid mentioned, that the angle is the contact of two lines, its meaning is the state occurring at their contact. This is the plane angle. As for the solid [angle], it is a body surrounded by two surfaces meeting at a line, or the state occurring at that.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of the nature of an angle. It begins by addressing a claim about the convex part of a surface, then presents an objection and answer. The main argument is that an angle is not a quantity (kamm) because it is annulled by doubling, whereas quantity is not annulled by doubling. The author demonstrates this for acute, right, and obtuse angles. Then it states that since the right angle is not a quantity, neither are the other two. The investigators (al-muhaqqiqun) define the angle as a quality (kayfiyya) specific to quantities, namely the state (hay'a) occurring at the meeting of two lines that surround a surface. The passage notes that mathematicians sometimes speak of the angle as a surface, but this is a metonymy: they mean the thing that has the angle. Finally, it distinguishes the plane angle from the solid angle, which is a body surrounded by two surfaces meeting at a line or the state at that meeting.

الكيف
القائمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An angle is a quantity because it can be divided.
Correction: Angle is divisible only through its substrate (the surface); in itself, it is a quality (state) that is annulled by doubling, unlike quantities.

Checkpoint

A) A surface enclosed by two lines meeting at a point.
B) The shape resulting from the meeting of two lines.
C) The state (hay'a) occurring at the meeting point of two lines surrounding a surface and meeting at a point.
D) A quantity divisible into parts.

Key Takeaway
An angle is not a quantity but a quality: a state (hay'a) at the meeting of two lines, proven by its annulment upon doubling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage defines an angle as a 'state' (hay’a) at the meeting of two lines. In your prayer, do you treat the moment of prostration as a state of encounter with Allah or merely as a quantitative act? What change can you make today to turn that moment into a genuine state of humility?
?
Just as doubling annuls an angle, sometimes our repeated actions—like seeking forgiveness without true remorse—annul the very spirit of repentance. Is there a habit you have doubled (repeated without heart) that has lost its meaning? What one step will you take to restore its quality?
?
The proof shows that an angle is not a quantity but a quality. In your relationship with the Qur’an, do you focus more on the number of pages you read (quantity) or the state of your heart when you recite (quality)? What practical adjustment will you make after this lesson to prioritize the quality of your engagement?

Dispositional Qualities and the Common Term
Defines dispositional qualities and discusses the common term as a bodily predisposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of dispositional qualities and the specific meaning of 'al-mushtarak' as a bodily predisposition.

Core Idea
Dispositional qualities are either readiness to be acted upon or readiness to resist.

This passage introduces the fourth division of qualities: dispositional qualities. It distinguishes between a readiness to be affected and a readiness to resist, and then discusses a special term 'al-mushtarak' which refers to a bodily predisposition that inclines the recipient. The author also addresses two aspects of this term using the example of wrestling.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two kinds of dispositional qualities
The passage presents two kinds: readiness to be affected (called la-quwwa) and readiness to resist (called quwwa). Find the definitions for each.
Note the examples: pliability/softness for la-quwwa, hardness/solidity for quwwa.
3
Understand the concept of al-mushtarak
Al-mushtarak is defined as a bodily predisposition toward an external matter. It is then addressed in two aspects: first, that wrestling involves psychic qualities and bodily hardness; second, that heat is a strong power on limbs yet is sensible.
The term 'al-mushtarak' here is not about linguistic homonymy but a specific bodily readiness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الرابع الكيفيات الاستعدادية وهي استعداد شديد على أن ينفعل كالممراضية واللين ويسمى اللاقوة أو على أن يقاوم ولا ينفعل كالمصحاحية والصلابة ويسمى القوة، فالمشترك كيفية بها يترجح القابل في أحد جانبي قبوله قبل أو على أن يفعل كالمصارعة، فالمشترك استعداد جسماني كامل نحو أمر من خارج ورد بوجهين الأول : أن المصارعة مثلاً تتعلق بعلم بالصناعة، وقدرة على الأفعال، وهما من الكيفيات النفسانية، وصلابة الأعضاء، وهي راجعة إلى الأول. الثاني : أن الحرارة قوة شديدة على الأطراف مع أنها من المحسوسات،
The fourth division: dispositional qualities. They are a strong readiness to be affected, like pliability and softness, and it is called la-quwwa (non-resistance), or to resist and not be affected, like hardness and solidity, and it is called quwwa (strength). The common term (al-mushtarak) is a quality by which the recipient is inclined toward one of the two sides of its receptivity, either before or toward acting, like wrestling. So the common term is a complete bodily predisposition toward an external matter, addressed in two aspects: First, that wrestling, for example, involves knowledge of the craft and ability to act, both of which are psychic qualities, and hardness of limbs, which reverts to the first. Second, that heat is a strong power on the limbs even though it is among the sensible qualities.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth division of qualities: dispositional qualities (al-kayfiyyat al-istidadiyya). It begins by stating that they are either a strong readiness to be acted upon (like softness and pliability), called 'la-quwwa' (non-resistance), or a readiness to resist and not be affected (like hardness and solidity), called 'quwwa' (strength). The author then introduces the term 'al-mushtarak' (the common term) in this context, defining it as a quality that inclines the recipient toward one of two sides of receptivity—either before acting or toward acting, using wrestling as an example. He further explains that 'al-mushtarak' is a complete bodily predisposition toward an external matter, and then addresses two aspects: First, wrestling involves knowledge (a psychic quality) and ability (also psychic), along with bodily hardness, which reverts to the first type. Second, heat is a strong power on the limbs even though it is a sensible quality. The passage thus clarifies the nature of dispositional qualities and the specific sense of 'al-mushtarak' as a bodily predisposition.

Key Terms

اللاقوة
القوة
المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'al-mushtarak' here refers to linguistic homonymy (a word with multiple meanings).
Correction: In this context, 'al-mushtarak' is a bodily predisposition, not a linguistic term.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two kinds of dispositional qualities?
A) Readiness to be affected and readiness to resist
B) Psychic qualities and bodily qualities
C) Sensible qualities and intelligible qualities
D) Heat and cold

Key Takeaway
Dispositional qualities are either readiness to be affected (la-quwwa) or readiness to resist (quwwa), and the term 'al-mushtarak' denotes a complete bodily predisposition toward an external matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your spiritual life, are you more often 'pliable' (open to divine direction) or 'hard' (resistant to change)? Which type of dispositional quality do you need to develop to draw closer to Allah?
?
The example of wrestling shows that both bodily and psychic readiness are required for action. Is your worship purely physical, or does it engage your heart and mind as well? What specific step can you take today to align both?
?
The common term (al-mushtarak) is a complete bodily predisposition toward an external matter. What external matter—such as prayer, charity, or seeking knowledge—are you merely 'aware of' versus fully predisposed toward in your body and soul? How can you move from passive inclination to active readiness?
