Rest, Motion, and Their Relation
Defines rest and answers the objection that motion is opposite to rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of rest (sukūn) as mere obtaining in a position and the resolution of the objection that motion is opposite to rest.

Core Idea
Rest is mere presence in a place; motion is not its absolute opposite.

This lesson introduces a key term in occasionalist physics: rest (sukūn). The author defines it as the simple obtaining of a body in a position, without any extra qualifier. Then he faces a critical objection: if motion and rest are opposites, how can motion be identical to or composed of rest? The answer requires a careful distinction between motion toward a position and motion away from it.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of rest
The passage opens by defining rest (sukūn) as 'mere obtaining in a position' (mujarrad al-ḥuṣūl fī al-ḥayyiz) without any condition that would distinguish it from parts of motion. Notice that this definition is neutral—rest is just being somewhere.
2
Follow the implication that every motion is rest
The author argues that if the first contingent (al-kawn al-awwal) in a new position is like the second contingent (staying), then entry into a position is also rest. Hence every motion, as entry into a new position, is a kind of rest. But not every rest is motion (e.g., staying put).
This is a logical consequence of the definition and the doctrine of occasional renewal (tajaddud).
3
Examine the objection and its answer
The objection states: motion is opposite to rest, so how can motion be the same as or composed of rest? The answer: opposition is not absolute. Motion from a position is opposed to rest in that same position. But motion to a position is not opposed to rest in that position—indeed, it is identical to the first contingent, which is like the second (which is rest).
The key distinction is between motion to a position and motion from a position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في السكون أن يكون عبارة من مجرد الحصول في الحيز من غير اعتبار قيد يميزه عن أجزاء الحركة، اللهم إلا أن يبني ذلك على أن الكون الأول في الحيز الثاني، يمثل الكون الثاني فيه، وهو سكون ،وفاقاً، فكذا الأول، ويكون هذا إلزاماً لمن يقال بتماثل الحصول الأول والثاني في الحيز الأول، فكذا في الحيز الثاني، فالتزم القاضي ذلك، وذهب إلى أن الكون الأول في الحيز الثاني، وهو الدخول فيه سكون، وبنى على ذلك أن كل حركة سكون من حيث إنها دخول في حيز وليس كل سكون حركة ، كالكون الثاني . فإن قيل: الحركة ضد السكون فكيف تكون نفسه أو مركبة منه . أجيب بأن التضاد ليس بين الحركة والسكون مطلقاً بل بين الحركة من الحيز والسكون فيه، وأما بين الحركة إلى الحيز والسكون فيه فلا تغاير عن التضاد لأنها عبارة عن الكون الأول فيه وهو تماثل الكون السكون الثاني الذي هو سكون بالاتفاق.
Concerning rest (as-sukūn): it is an expression of mere obtaining (al-ḥuṣūl) in a position (al-ḥayyiz) without consideration of a condition that distinguishes it from parts of motion (al-ḥaraka). Unless that is built upon the fact that the first contingent (al-kawn al-awwal) in the second position is like the second contingent therein, and it is rest, by agreement, so likewise the first. And this is a necessary implication against one who says the two obtainings (al-ḥuṣūlayn) are identical in the first position, so likewise in the second position. Al-Qāḍī adhered to that and held that the first contingent in the second position—i.e., entry into it—is rest, and built upon that that every motion is rest insofar as it is entry into a position, but not every rest is motion, like the second contingent. For it is said: Motion is opposite (ḍidd) to rest, so how can it be the same as it or composed of it? The answer: Opposition is not between motion and rest absolutely, but between motion from a position and rest in it. As for motion to a position and rest in it, there is no dissimilarity (lā taghāyur) from opposition [i.e., it is not opposed], because it is an expression of the first contingent therein, and it is identical to the second contingent, which is rest by agreement.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage defines rest (sukūn) and then addresses an objection. The author begins by stating that rest is merely the obtaining (ḥuṣūl) of a body in a position (ḥayyiz) without any additional condition that would distinguish it from parts of motion. This is because some earlier scholars (like al-Qāḍī) argued that the first contingent (kawn) entering a position is identical to the second contingent staying in that position, and since the second is rest, the first must also be rest. Thus every motion, as an entry into a new position, is a rest in that respect, but not every rest is motion (e.g., the second contingent is rest but not motion). Then an objection is raised: motion is opposite (ḍidd) to rest, so how can motion be the same as rest or composed of it? The answer explains that the opposition is not absolute; it holds only between motion from a position (leaving it) and rest in that same position. Motion to a position (entering) is not opposed to rest in that position—indeed, it is the same as the first contingent therein, which is identical to the second contingent (rest) by agreement. Therefore the objection fails.

Key Terms
سكون
حركة
حيز
كون
حصول
تضاد
إلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion and rest are absolute opposites in every respect.
Correction: Opposition is conditional: motion from a position is opposite to rest in that position, but motion to a position is not opposite to rest in that position.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of rest (sukūn)?
A) The opposite of motion
B) Mere obtaining in a position without a distinguishing condition
C) The first contingent in the second position
D) The collection of two obtainings

Key Takeaway
Rest is defined as mere presence in a position; the apparent opposition between motion and rest is resolved by attending to the direction of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Which direction truly defines your current efforts? Ask yourself: Am I merely fleeing evil, or am I actively entering into good? Write down one concrete 'entry' you will make today.

Al-Amidi's Objection on the Non-Identity of Two Obtainings
Al-Āmidī objects to the identity of two obtainings; response defends using criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Āmidī's objection that the two obtainings are not identical despite sharing specification, and the response based on criteria for motion and rest.

Core Idea
Sharing one attribute does not make two things identical; essential attributes differ.

Al-Āmidī challenges the claim that the two obtainings (first entering a position and second remaining) are identical because they both specify the body to a position. He argues that their essential attributes differ: the first is motion (exit from previous position), while the second is rest. This lesson presents his objection and the defense based on criteria for motion and rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow of objection and response.
2
Identify the core objection
Al-Āmidī argues that sharing one attribute (specification) does not entail identity. Find his key argument that the first obtaining is motion.
He uses the fact that the first obtaining is exit from the first position.
3
Understand the response criteria
The response defines motion as 'lack of being preceded by obtaining in that position' and rest as 'lack of connection with obtaining in another position'. See how these apply differently to the two obtainings.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the argument is a necessary implication against those who claim identity. Consider why the analogy works only if the two obtainings are identical.
The final sentence reaffirms that the first obtaining would have to be rest if the second is rest, but that is not the case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض الآمدي بمنع الحصولين واشتراكهما في كون كل منهما موجباً للاختصاص بذلك الحيز لا يوجب التماثل لأنا لا نسلم أنه أخص صفاتهما النفسية كيف والحصول الأول في الحيز الثاني حركة وفاقاً، لكونه خروجاً من الحيز الأول، فلو كان مماثلاً للحصول الثاني فيه لزم أن يكون هو أيضاً حركة، ولا قائل به . فإن أجيب بأن عدم المسبوقية بالحصول في ذلك الحيز معتبر في الحركة فيصدق على الحصول الأول دون الثاني . قلنا : فكذا عدم الاتصال بالحصول في حيز آخر معتبر في السكون فيصدق على الحصول الثاني دون الأول وحاصلة أن الكلام إلزام لمن يقول بتماثل الحصولين، وبأن كون الثاني سكوناً يستلزم كون الأول كذلك.
Al-Āmidī objected by denying the two obtainings, [saying]: Their sharing in each being a cause of specification (ikhtiṣāṣ) to that position does not necessitate identity (tamāthul), for we do not concede that it is the most specific of their essential attributes. How [can it be] when the first obtaining in the second position is motion by agreement, because it is exit from the first position? If it were identical to the second obtaining therein, it would necessarily also be motion, and no one says that. If it is answered that lack of being preceded by obtaining in that position is considered in motion, so it applies to the first obtaining but not the second, we say: Likewise, lack of connection with obtaining in another position is considered in rest, so it applies to the second obtaining but not the first. The conclusion is that the statement is a necessary implication against one who says the two obtainings are identical, and that the fact that the second is rest entails that the first is also rest.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage presents al-Āmidī's objection and the response. Al-Āmidī argues that the two obtainings (the first entering a position and the second remaining in it) are not identical merely because both specify the body to that position. Their essential attributes differ: the first obtaining is motion (exit from previous position), while the second is rest. If they were identical, the first would also be rest, which is absurd. The response argues that the criterion for motion is 'not being preceded by obtaining in that same position' (so the first obtaining meets this criterion, the second does not), and the criterion for rest is 'lack of connection with obtaining in another position' (so the second obtaining meets this, the first does not). Thus the two obtainings are different, and the original claim that the first obtaining is rest (by analogy with the second) is shown to be a necessary implication only against those who assert their identity. The passage ends by reaffirming that the argument is a refutation of the identity claim.

عدم الاتصال
إلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That sharing one attribute (like specification) makes two things identical.
Correction: Al-Āmidī shows that essential attributes (motion vs rest) differ, so identity does not follow.

Checkpoint
According to al-Āmidī's objection, why are the two obtainings not identical?
A) They occur at different times
B) The first is motion (exit) while the second is rest
C) They are in different positions
D) They have different causes

Key Takeaway
Two things can share one attribute without being identical; their essential attributes determine identity.

?
The first obtaining is motion because it leaves something behind; the second is rest because it stays. In your spiritual journey, are you in a season of motion (struggling to leave old habits) or rest (stabilizing and deepening)? Which one does your soul actually need right now, and why have you been resisting it?
?

Motion as a Collection of Rests: The Condition of Being Preceded
Analyzes the logical implications of defining motion as a collection of rests under a specific condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument regarding the definition of motion as a collection of rests and why a specific condition (lack of being preceded in that position) invalidates that definition.

Core Idea
Motion is a collection of rests means every part of motion is a rest, not that every rest is part of motion.

In kalam, motion is often analyzed in terms of atomic rests. One debated definition is that motion is a collection of rests (sakanāt). But what does that really mean? This passage from al-Mawāqif explores a key condition that challenges this definition and reveals a logical nuance about the relationship between parts and wholes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's words come through before interpreting.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the condition
The passage begins with a condition from al-Mawāqif: considering 'lack of being preceded by obtaining in that position' (not another position). Understand what this means: the motion is defined by not having been in that same position before.
This condition is key to the argument.
3
Understand the two interpretations
The passage presents two possible interpretations of what the condition implies. First: the rest (second obtaining) is not part of motion. Second: the first obtaining (starting point) is itself motion. Notice the different conclusions.
The first interpretation leads to an error; the second has some justification.
4
Grasp the logical error
The error is called 'misleading the reverse' (إيهام العكس). The statement 'motion is a collection of rests' means every part of motion is a rest, but it does NOT mean every rest is a part of motion. This is a crucial logical subtlety.
This distinction resolves the apparent invalidation.
5
Summarize the lesson
Take a moment to write down the key point: the condition does not actually refute the definition if we correctly understand the part-whole relationship. The definition only claims that parts of motion are rests, not that all rests are parts of motion.
Reflect on why this matters for kalam discussions of motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر في المواقف أنه إذا اعتبر في الحركة عدم المسبوقية بالحصول في ذلك الحيز لا المسبوقية بالحصول في حيز آخر بطل قولهم : إن الحركة مجموع سكنات فإن أراد أن السكون الذي هو الحصول الثاني لا يكون حينئذ جزءا للحركة، فلا يكون عبارة عن مجموع السكنات بل عن بعضها، فغلط من باب إيهام العكس لأن معنى قولهم هي مجموع سكنات أن كل جزء لها سكون، وهو لا يستلزم أن يكون كل سكون جزءاً لها. وإن أراد أن الحصول الأول في الحيز الأول يكون حينئذ حركة، مع أنه ليس مجموع سكنات فله وجه .
And it is mentioned in al-Mawāqif that if in motion one considers lack of being preceded by obtaining in that position, not lack of being preceded by obtaining in another position, then their statement that motion is a collection of rests (majmūʿ sakanāt) is invalidated. For if he meant that the rest which is the second obtaining is not then a part of motion, then it is not an expression of a collection of rests but rather of some of them. So the error is from the category of misleading the reverse, because the meaning of their statement 'it is a collection of rests' is that every part of it is a rest, and it does not entail that every rest is a part of it. And if he meant that the first obtaining in the first position is then motion, while it is not a collection of rests, then it has a point.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage cites al-Mawāqif to discuss the definition of motion as a collection of rests. The condition under consideration is 'lack of being preceded by obtaining in that same position' (i.e., motion is defined by not having been in that position before). If this condition is used, the claim that motion is a collection of rests fails. Why? Because the second obtaining (the rest in that position) would then not be a part of motion, so motion would not be a collection of all rests but only some of them. The author notes that this is an error of 'misleading the reverse': the statement 'motion is a collection of rests' means that every part of motion is a rest, but it does not mean that every rest is a part of motion. So even if some rests are not parts, that does not contradict the claim. However, if someone interprets the first obtaining in the first position (the starting point) as motion, even though it is not a collection of rests, that interpretation has some justification. Thus the passage clarifies the nuanced relationship between motion and rests.

جزء
المسبوقية
إيهام العكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'motion is a collection of rests' means every rest is a part of motion.
Correction: The passage clarifies it only means every part of motion is a rest, not the converse.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of the statement 'motion is a collection of rests'?
A) Every rest is a part of motion
B) Motion consists of the second obtaining only
C) Every part of motion is a rest
D) Motion is a single rest

Key Takeaway
The definition of motion as a collection of rests must be understood as 'every part of motion is a rest,' not 'every rest is part of motion'; the condition of not being preceded in the same position highlights this distinction.

In your own life, where might you be assuming the converse of Allah's commands—for example, thinking that because you sometimes pray, you are therefore always mindful of Him? What one action will you take today to ensure your actions are truly parts of a consistent character of faith?
?

How can this lesson's logical precision teach you to be more careful in your judgments of others and yourself? Will you commit to examining the 'conditions' before concluding about someone's faith?

Final Objection and Answer on Alternative Definition of Motion
Addresses objection and answer on alternative definition of motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the argument applies also when motion is defined as being preceded in another position is answered by clarifying that motion is the collection of two obtainings in two positions.

Core Idea
Motion as collection of rests means two obtainings in two positions, not just one.

This passage addresses a final objection that the previous argument holds even if we define motion as being preceded in another position rather than not being preceded in the same position. The answer clarifies the intended meaning of 'motion is a collection of rests' and reinterprets al-Āmidī's objection accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening objection: 'This applies also to the other assumption...' Understand the claim that the obtaining in this position is one, not a collection, regardless of how it is qualified.
2
Understand the answer's clarification
The answer states that the intended meaning is that motion is the collection of two obtainings in two positions, as made clear by the statement 'it is a collection of rests.' This refutes the idea that it is only the second obtaining.
The phrase 'collection of rests' is key.
3
See the reinterpretation of al-Āmidī
The author then reinterprets al-Āmidī's objection: if the first and second obtainings in one position were identical, then the second obtaining in the second position would be a part of motion like the first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: هذا وارد على التقدير الآخر أيضاً، وهو أن يعتبر في الحركة المسبوقية بالحصول في حيز آخر لأن الحصول في هذا الحيز سواء قيد المسبوقية بالحصول في حيز آخر أو بعدم المسبوقية بالحصول في ذلك الحيز أو لم يقيد بشيء أصلا فهو واحد لا مجموع . قلنا مرادهم أن الحركة مجموع الحصولين في الحيزين على ما يفصح عنه قولهم إنها مجموع سكنات لا مجرد الحصول في الحيز الثاني المقيد بالحصول في حيز سابق على ما يفهم من ظاهر العبارة، هذا لا يتأتى إلا على أن يشترط في الحركة الحصول في حيز سابق . وتوجيه اعتراض الآمدي حينئذ أنه لو تماثل الحصول الأول والثاني في حيز واحد لكان الحصول الثاني في الحيز الثاني جزءاً من الحركة كالأول.
For it is said: This applies also to the other assumption—that in motion one considers being preceded by obtaining in another position—because the obtaining in this position, whether it is qualified by being preceded by obtaining in another position or by not being preceded by obtaining in that position, or not qualified by anything at all, it is one, not a collection. We say: Their intended meaning is that motion is the collection of the two obtainings in the two positions, as is made clear by their statement that it is a collection of rests, not merely the obtaining in the second position qualified by obtaining in a previous position, as might be understood from the apparent wording. This is only realized if motion is conditioned on obtaining in a previous position. And the direction of al-Āmidī's objection then would be that if the first and second obtaining in one position were identical, then the second obtaining in the second position would be a part of motion like the first.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage deals with a final objection and its answer. The objection claims that the previous argument applies also if motion is defined as 'being preceded by obtaining in another position' (rather than not being preceded in the same position). The reason given is that the obtaining in this position, regardless of how it is qualified (whether by being preceded elsewhere, or by not being preceded here, or without qualification), is a single thing, not a collection. The answer clarifies that the intended meaning of the statement 'motion is a collection of rests' is that motion is the collection of two obtainings in two positions (the starting position and the ending position), not merely the obtaining in the second position conditioned by a previous obtaining. This interpretation only holds if motion requires obtaining in a previous position (i.e., the starting point). Then the author reinterprets al-Āmidī's objection in light of this: if the first and second obtainings in one position were identical (as some claim), then the second obtaining in the second position would be a part of motion just like the first. This shows the consistency of the argument.

Correction: The passage clarifies that 'motion is a collection of rests' refers to the collection of two obtainings in two positions (the starting and ending positions), not just the second obtaining.

A) The second obtaining only
B) The collection of two obtainings in two positions
C) The first obtaining only

Logical Analysis of Motion as Sum of Rests
Al-Taftazani analyzes the definition of motion as sum of rests and corrects a logical confusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical confusion about the definition of motion as a sum of rests and how Al-Taftazani clarifies the correct implication.

Core Idea
Motion as sum of rests: every part is a rest, but not every rest is part of motion.

Al-Taftazani examines a view from al-Mawaqif that defines motion as a sum of rests. He points out a common logical mistake: assuming that if motion is a sum of rests, then every rest must be a part of motion. Understanding the correct implication is key to avoiding conceptual errors in kalam discussions about motion and rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its literal English translation. Let the argument unfold.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The passage considers two possible meanings of 'if motion is considered without precedence...' and what follows. Notice the two 'if' clauses.
The first interpretation leads to a mistake; the second has a valid point.
3
Understand the logical distinction
Focus on the key phrase: 'every part of it is a rest, but it does not necessitate that every rest is a part of it.' This is the core of the correction.
This distinction reverses a common misunderstanding.
4
Recognize the mistake of reverse implication
The author calls the first mistake 'إيهام العكس' (suggesting the opposite). Appreciate why the first interpretation fails logically.
The mistake is to think that if motion is a sum of rests, then all rests are parts of motion.
5
Consider the alternative interpretation
The second interpretation, where the first attainment in the first place is motion itself, avoids the problem. Note that this reading is not ruled out.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المواقف أنه إذا اعتبر في الحركة عدم المسبوقية بالحصول في ذلك الحيز لا المسبوقية بالحصول في حيز آخر بطل قولهم : إن الحركة مجموع سكنات فإن أراد أن السكون الذي هو الحصول الثاني لا يكون حينئذ جزءا للحركة، فلا يكون عبارة عن مجموع السكنات بل عن بعضها، فغلط من باب إيهام العكس لأن معنى قولهم هي مجموع سكنات أن كل جزء لها سكون، وهو لا يستلزم أن يكون كل سكون جزءاً لها. وإن أراد أن الحصول الأول في الحيز الأول يكون حينئذ حركة، مع أنه ليس مجموع سكنات فله وجه.
In al-Mawaqif: if motion is considered without precedence of attainment in that place, not without precedence of attainment in another place, their statement that motion is a sum of rests becomes invalid. For if he means that rest which is the second attainment is then not part of motion, then motion is not an expression of the sum of rests but of some of them, so he is mistaken by way of suggesting the opposite, because the meaning of their statement 'it is a sum of rests' is that every part of it is a rest, but it does not necessitate that every rest is a part of it. And if he means that the first attainment in the first place is then motion, even though it is not a sum of rests, then that has a point.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage begins by quoting a view from al-Mawaqif regarding the definition of motion. If motion is defined as not being preceded by attainment in that same place, rather than not being preceded by attainment in another place, then the claim that motion is a sum of rests fails. The author explains the logical confusion: one might think that if motion is a sum of rests, then every rest is part of motion. But the correct implication is only that every part of motion is a rest, not the reverse. However, if one intends that the first attainment in the first place is motion by itself, that could be valid, even though it is not a sum of rests.

The Condition of Precedence in Motion and al-Amidi's Objection
Al-Taftazani addresses an objection about the definition of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani insists that motion must be conditioned by attainment in a preceding place to avoid the objection that motion would reduce to a single attainment.

Core Idea
Motion as a sum requires the condition of precedence.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if motion is defined as attainment in this place conditioned by precedence in another, then the attainment in this place remains a single entity. Al-Taftazani replies that motion is the sum of two distinct attainments, which only makes sense with the precedence condition. He then clarifies al-Amidi's objection that without this condition, the two attainments would collapse into one.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's claim that even with the condition of precedence, the attainment in this place is still one, not a sum.
Notice the phrase 'فهو واحد لا مجموع' – it is one, not a sum.
2
Examine the reply
Read al-Taftazani's reply: motion is the sum of two attainments in two places, as indicated by the statement 'it is a sum of rests.'
This only works if motion requires attainment in a preceding place.
3
Understand al-Amidi's objection
Read the final part about al-Amidi's objection: if the first and second attainments were analogous in one place, the second would be part of motion like the first, showing the necessity of the precedence condition.
This underscores why the condition is essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا وارد على التقدير الآخر أيضاً، وهو أن يعتبر في الحركة المسبوقية بالحصول في حيز آخر لأن الحصول في هذا الحيز سواء قيد المسبوقية بالحصول في حيز آخر أو بعدم المسبوقية بالحصول في ذلك الحيز أو لم يقيد بشيء أصلا فهو واحد لا مجموع . قلنا مرادهم أن الحركة مجموع الحصولين في الحيزين على ما يفصح عنه قولهم إنها مجموع سكنات لا مجرد الحصول في الحيز الثاني المقيد بالحصول في حيز سابق على ما يفهم من ظاهر العبارة ، هذا لا يتأتى إلا على أن يشترط في الحركة الحصول في حيز سابق . وتوجيه اعتراض الآمدي حينئذ أنه لو تماثل الحصول الأول والثاني في حيز واحد لكان الحصول الثاني في الحيز الثاني جزءاً من الحركة كالأول.
If it is said: This also applies to the other estimation, namely that motion is considered with precedence of attainment in another place, because attainment in this place, whether it is qualified by precedence of attainment in another place, or by non-precedence of attainment in that place, or not qualified at all, is one, not a sum. We reply: Their intention is that motion is the sum of the two attainments in the two places, as their statement 'it is a sum of rests' clarifies, not merely the attainment in the second place qualified by attainment in a preceding place, as understood from the apparent wording. This is only possible if motion requires attainment in a preceding place. Al-Amidi's objection then is that if the first and second attainments were analogous in one place, then the second attainment in the second place would be a part of motion like the first.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector says that the same problem arises on the alternative definition (motion with precedence in another place) because the attainment in this place is a single entity, not a sum. The reply clarifies that motion is the sum of two attainments in two places, not just the second attainment conditioned by a preceding one. This only works if motion is defined with the condition of attainment in a preceding place. Al-Amidi's objection is then explained: if the two attainments were similar in one place, then the second attainment would be a part of motion like the first.

Key Terms
مسبوقية

آمدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the condition of precedence is unnecessary and that motion can be understood without it.
Correction: The author shows that without the condition of precedence, motion cannot be understood as a sum of two distinct attainments; the objection demonstrates that without it, the two attainments would collapse into one.

Checkpoint

A) The second attainment must be conditioned by the first.
B) Motion requires attainment in a preceding place.
C) The two attainments must be analogous.
D) Motion is not a sum.

Key Takeaway
Motion as a sum of two attainments only holds if the second attainment is preceded by the first in a different place.

Motion as Continuous Attainments and the Definition of Rest
Al-Taftazani distinguishes between actual and imagined motion and discusses rest conditionally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Taftazani's distinction between actual and imagined motion, and how the definition of rest depends on whether duration is stipulated.

Core Idea
Motion is a series of attainments, not a continuous stretch; rest depends on whether we require duration.

This passage presents Al-Taftazani's verification on motion and rest. He begins by describing motion as continuous attainments without stability. He then distinguishes two senses of motion: the actual, realized motion, and the imagined extended one. This leads to a subtle discussion about whether motion can be considered a type of rest, depending on how we define rest. Let's unpack his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand key terms
Read the Arabic passage and translation side by side. Note the terms: حصول (attainment), استمرار (continuity), استقرار (stability), موهوم (imagined), سكون (rest), لبث (duration).
These terms are central to the argument.
2
Identify the two definitions of motion
Find where al-Taftazani distinguishes actual motion (attainment after attainment in another place) from imagined motion (successive attainments in consecutive places). Understand the difference.
Actual motion is discrete; imagined motion is conceived as a continuum.
3
Analyze the conditional definition of rest
Read the part beginning with 'ثم إن جعل السكون...' and see how the definition of rest changes if duration is stipulated or not. If no duration, motion is rest; if duration required, then rest is the second attainment or sum of two.
Note the hesitation (تردد) at the end.
4
Reflect on the philosophical significance
Consider why this distinction matters for kalam: it impacts how we understand change, time, and atomic theory. The 'attainments' view aligns with occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (والتحقيق) إن للمتحرك بين طرفي المسافة حصولات على الاستمرار دون الاستقرار، فإن أريد بالحركة ما هو المحقق منها، فهي الحصول بعد الحصول في حيز آخر ، وإن أريد الموهوم الممتد من المبدأ إلى المنتهى فهو الحصولات المتعاقبة في الأحياز المتتالية، ثم إن جعل السكون اسماً للحصول من غير اشتراط لبث، فالحركة سكون أو مجموع سكنات، وإن اشترط فلا وحينئذ فالسكون هو الحصول الثاني، أو مجموع الحصولين فيه ،تردد،
He said (and the verification): The moving body has continuous attainments between the endpoints of the distance without stability. If by 'motion' is meant what is realized from it, then it is attainment after attainment in another place. And if is meant the imagined extended from the beginning to the end, then it is the successive attainments in consecutive places. Then, if 'rest' is made a name for attainment without stipulating duration, then motion is rest or a sum of rests. And if duration is stipulated, then no. In that case rest is the second attainment, or the sum of the two attainments in it, there is hesitation.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This is the author's verification. He explains that the moving body has continuous attainments without stability. Two meanings of motion are distinguished: the actual (realized) motion is attainment after attainment in another place; the imagined motion extended from start to end is the series of successive attainments. Then he discusses rest: if rest is defined simply as attainment without requiring duration, then motion is rest or a sum of rests. If duration is required, then rest is either the second attainment or the sum of two attainments, but there is hesitation (taraddud) on this point.

The Unity of Rest and Opposition of Modes
Al-Taftazani explains the unity of rest's reality and the impossibility of combining opposed modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that rest has a single reality differentiated by aspects, and why opposed modes (akwān) cannot coexist in a substance.

Core Idea
Rest is one reality; its diversity comes from how we view it, not from its essence.

In this passage, al-Taftazani clarifies a subtle point about the nature of rest and the principle of opposition among modes (akwān). He first establishes that the reality of rest is uniform across all four modes, with differences arising only from our points of view. Then he explains why opposite modes—like conjunction and disjunction—cannot actually occur together in one substance, drawing on the impossibility of a substance being in two places at once. This reasoning also rules out counting contiguity as a mode.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with care
Read the Arabic and translation slowly, noticing the two distinct claims: the unity of rest's reality, and the argument against combining opposed modes.
2
Understand the unity of rest
Identify the claim that the reality of rest is one across motion, rest, conjunction, disjunction. Note that distinctions come from 'aspects' (ḥaythiyyāt).
3
Grasp the argument against combination
Follow the logical steps: two modes would require either one place (similar) or two places (opposed), but a substance cannot be in two places at once.
4
Consider the role of contiguity
Notice that contiguity (mumāssa) is excluded from modes, otherwise combination would be possible (as in a substance surrounded by six).
5
Reflect on the bigger picture
Ask: Why does this matter for kalam? It establishes that a substance cannot have contradictory attributes simultaneously, preserving rational consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الحق أن حقيقة السكون في الأربعة واحد. وإنما التمايز بالحيثيات أن الواحد بالشخص ربما يكون اجتماعاً وافتراقاً وحركة وسكوناً باعتبارات مختلفة، ومن أطلق القول بتضاد الأكوان أراد أن الأكوان المتميزة في الوجود يمتنع اجتماعها لأن الكونين يوجبان تخصيص الجوهر بحيز واحد فيتماثلان وبحيزين فيتضادان ضرورة امتناع حصول الجوهر في آن واحد في حيزين، وأيا ما كان فلا يجتمعان ومبنى ذلك على أن المماسة ليست من الأكوان وإلا فلا خفاء في اجتماعها كما في الجوهر المحفوف بستة جواهر ، فإن منعه على ما نقل عن البعض مكابرة.
Then the truth is that the reality of rest in the four is one. Distinction is only by aspects: the numerically one may be conjunction, disjunction, motion, and rest by different considerations. Whoever asserts the opposition of modes means that distinct modes in existence cannot combine because two modes necessitate allocating the substance to one place—so they are similar—or to two places—so they are opposed—due to the impossibility of the substance being in two places at one instant. And in any case, they do not combine. This is based on the fact that contiguity is not among the modes; otherwise, there is no doubt about their combination, as in a substance surrounded by six substances. If one denies this, as is reported from some, it is stubbornness.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage discusses the true nature of rest and the principle of opposition of modes (akwān). First, the reality of rest is one across the four modes (motion, rest, conjunction, disjunction). Distinctions arise only from different considerations (ḥaythiyyāt): the same entity can be called conjunction, disjunction, motion, or rest depending on context. Then the author explains the doctrine of opposition of modes: distinct modes cannot coexist in a single substance because two modes would require the substance to be allocated to one place (making them similar) or to two places (making them opposed), and it is impossible for a substance to be in two places at once. The basis of this is that contiguity (mumāssa) is not itself a mode, because if it were, modes could combine (e.g., a substance surrounded by six substances would have multiple contiguities). Denying this combination is stubborn.

Misconception: One might think that a single substance can simultaneously possess opposite modes (e.g., rest and motion) or that contiguity is a mode like the others.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that opposed modes cannot combine because they would require the substance to be in two places at once. Contiguity is not a mode; if it were, combination would be possible, as in a substance surrounded by six substances.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The reality of rest is one; opposed modes cannot coexist in a substance because that would require the substance to be in two places at once, and contiguity is not a mode.

Philosophical and Kalām Definitions of Motion
Al-Taftazani presents philosophical and kalām definitions of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two philosophical senses of motion and how the mutakallimūn redefined motion in terms of discrete attainments in places.

Core Idea
Motion is not a single concept; philosophers and theologians define it differently.

Al-Taftazani begins by distinguishing two philosophical meanings of 'motion': the continuous change of state and the imagined interval between endpoints. Then he shows how the mutakallimūn adopt each meaning to formulate their own definition. This passage introduces key terms that structure later debates in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage closely
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the shift from philosophical to kalām terminology.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the next lesson will continue.
2
Identify the two philosophical definitions
Find the two definitions of motion as described by philosophers: (1) continuous change at every instant, (2) an imagined extended continuum.
3
See how the mutakallimūn reinterpret each
Notice that the kalām scholars adopt the first philosophical definition to say motion is 'attainment in a place after attainment in another place' and the second to say 'successive attainments in contiguous places'.
This shift from continuous to discrete is crucial.
4
Recognize the derived terminology
Identify the terms kharūj (exit), dukhūl (entry), and sakan (rest) and understand how they relate to the preceding and subsequent places.
Some scholars call it 'rest' if they do not require duration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سيجيء في طريق الفلاسفة أنه قد يراد بالحركة كون المتحرك متوسطا بين المبدأ أو المنتهى بحيث تكون حاله في كل آن على خلاف ما قبله وما بعده ، وقد يراد بها الأمر الموهوم الممتد من المبدأ إلى المنتهي، والمتكلمون بالنظر إلى الأول قالوا إنها الحصول في الحيز بعد الحصول في حيز آخر، والناظر إلى الثاني إنها حصولات متعاقبة في أحياز متلاصقة، ويسمى بالإضافة إلى الحيز السابق خروجاً، وإلى اللاحق دخولاً ، ثم منهم من سمى مثل هذا الحصول سكوناً من غير أن يعتبر في مسماه اللبث، والحصول بعد
It will come in the way of the philosophers that by motion may be meant the moving body being intermediate between the beginning and the end, such that its state at every instant is different from what precedes and what follows. And it may be meant the imagined thing extended from the beginning to the end. The mutakallimūn, with regard to the first, said that it is attainment in a place after attainment in another place. And those who look to the second say that it is successive attainments in contiguous places. It is called, in relation to the preceding place, 'exit', and in relation to the subsequent place, 'entry'. Then among them there are those who call such attainment 'rest' without stipulating duration in its meaning, and attainment after...
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage introduces two definitions of motion: the philosophical definition (the body's state changing at every instant) and the imagined extended definition. Kalām scholars adopt the first definition: attainment after attainment in another place. Others focus on the second: successive attainments in contiguous places. This is also called exit (kharūj) and entry (dukhūl) relative to the preceding and subsequent places. Some scholars call such attainment 'rest' if duration is not stipulated. The text ends mid-sentence, indicating a continuation on the next page.

Philosophers see motion as continuous change, but kalām scholars define it as discrete successive attainments in places, giving rise to the terms exit, entry, and rest.

Motion and Rest: Two Meanings and Two Interpretations
Sanusi defines motion and rest using atomistic occurrences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the atomistic kalam definitions of motion and rest, including the two meanings of motion (actual vs. imaginary) and the two interpretations of rest (with or without stipulation of lingering).

Core Idea
Motion and rest are variations of the same reality: discrete occurrences in space.

This passage from Sanusi's Sharh al-Maqasid clarifies the foundational kalam terms 'motion' (haraka) and 'rest' (sukun). In the atomistic worldview, motion is not a continuous flow but a series of discrete 'presences' (husulat) in consecutive places. The author then examines two ways to define rest and how they relate to motion. Understanding these distinctions is essential for later discussions on time, change, and causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without deep analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the technical terms: حصول, حيز, متحرك, محقق, موهوم.
2
Identify the two definitions of motion
The passage presents two meanings of motion: actual (al-muḥaqqaq) and imaginary (al-mawhūm). Find how each is defined and note the difference between discrete occurrences and the imagined continuum.
Actual motion is occurrence after occurrence in another place; imaginary motion is the series of successive occurrences.
3
Analyze the two interpretations of rest
The passage then discusses rest (sukun). If rest means occurrence without lingering, motion becomes a kind of rest. If lingering is required, then rest is the second occurrence or the sum of two occurrences. Note the hesitation (taraddud) at the end.
This shows the scholastic debate on whether motion and rest are distinct or reducible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والتحقيق: إن للمتحرك حصولات على الاستمرار دون الاستقرار، فإن أريد بالحركة ما هو المحقق منها، فهي الحصول بعد الحصول في حيز آخر، وإن أريد الموهوم الممتد من المبدأ إلى المنتهى فهو الحصولات المتعاقبة في الأحياز المتتالية، ثم إن جعل السكون اسماً للحصول من غير اشتراط لبث، فالحركة سكون أو مجموع سكنات، وإن اشترط فلا وحينئذ فالسكون هو الحصول الثاني، أو مجموع الحصولين فيه تردد.
He said: And the verification: For the moving thing there are occurrences on continuity without rest. If by motion is meant what is actual of it, then it is occurrence after occurrence in another place. If by it is meant the imaginary thing extended from the beginning to the end, then it is the successive occurrences in consecutive places. Then if rest is made a term for occurrence without stipulating lingering, then motion is a rest or a sum of rests. If it is stipulated, then no, and in that case rest is the second occurrence, or the sum of the two occurrences; there is hesitation.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage defines motion and rest in the atomistic framework of kalam. It begins by stating that the moving thing has occurrences continuously without rest. Then it distinguishes two meanings of motion: the actual (al-muḥaqqaq), which is occurrence after occurrence in another place (i.e., discrete leaps), and the imaginary (al-mawhūm) continuum from start to end, which is the sequence of successive occurrences. Next it discusses the term 'rest' (sukūn). If rest is defined simply as occurrence (ḥuṣūl) without requiring lingering (lubth), then motion is itself a rest (since each occurrence is a presence) or a sum of rests (sakanāt). If lingering is required, then motion is not a rest; in that case sukun is either the second occurrence (the presence after the first in the same place) or the sum of both occurrences. The author notes there is hesitation (taraddud) among scholars as to which definition is correct.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
حصول
حيز
لبث
متحرك
محقق
موهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That motion and rest are independent, separate 'akwān' rather than variations of a single reality of presence.
Correction: In this atomistic framework, both motion and rest are explained through discrete occurrences (ḥuṣūlāt) in place. Rest is not a separate kind but a specific case of presence without change, and motion is a series of presences in different places. They are variations of the same reality.

Checkpoint
According to the first meaning of motion (actual motion), what is it?
A) The imaginary continuum from start to end
B) Occurrence after occurrence in another place
C) A sum of rests
D) The occurrence at the moment of origination

Key Takeaway
In kalam, motion and rest are both explained through discrete occurrences in place, with rest being a special case of motion when lingering is not required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The scholars hesitated over definitions of motion and rest. In which areas of your own convictions do you need to pause, reflect, and admit hesitation rather than cling to comfortable assumptions about how reality works?

The Unity of the Akwan and the Nature of Their Contradiction
States truth about the unity of akwan and the meaning of their alleged contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the four akwan (conjunction, separation, motion, rest) have a single reality and that their apparent contradiction is based on relational aspects, not ontological distinction.

Core Idea
The akwan are one in reality, diverse only in how they relate to places.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid addresses a key question: Are the akwan distinct entities that cannot coexist? The author explains that in truth, all four share one reality—occurrence in a place—and their differences are merely aspects. He then unpacks the meaning of their claimed contradiction, showing it depends on whether they necessitate one place or two.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice that the author starts with 'ثم الحق' (Then the truth is...), signaling a conclusive clarification.
2
Identify the core claim and reasoning
Find the statement that the reality of sukun is one, and how differentiation comes from aspects (حيثيات). Then follow the explanation of why distinct akwan cannot coexist: either they require one place (similar) or two places (impossible).
The text provides two cases: similar or contradictory.
3
Understand the role of contact (مماسة)
The author adds that this reasoning assumes contact is not among the akwan. Otherwise, a substance surrounded by six substances would have six coexisting akwan, which is clearly possible. Reflect on why this example is used.
Denying this possibility is called stubbornness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الحق أن حقيقة السكون في الأربعة واحد. وإنما التمايز بالحيثيات، أن الواحد بالشخص ربما يكون اجتماعاً وافتراقاً وحركة وسكوناً باعتبارات مختلفة. ومن أطلق القول بتضاد الأكوان أراد أن الأكوان المتميزة في الوجود يمتنع اجتماعها لأن الكونين يوجبان تخصيص الجوهر بحيز واحد فيتماثلان وبحيزين فيتضادان ضرورة امتناع حصول الجوهر في آن واحد في حيزين. وأيا ما كان فلا يجتمعان. ومبنى ذلك على أن المماسة ليست من الأكوان وإلا فلا خفاء في اجتماعها كما في الجوهر المحفوف بستة جواهر، فإن منعه على ما نقل عن البعض مكابرة.
Then the truth is that the reality of rest in the four [akwan] is one. Differentiation is only by aspects, that one individual thing may be conjunction, separation, motion, and rest according to different considerations. And whoever asserted the claim of the contradiction of the akwan meant that akwan that are distinct in existence cannot coexist, because the two akwan either necessitate assigning the substance to one place, in which case they are similar and cannot coexist, or to two places, in which case they are contradictory due to the impossibility of the substance's occurrence at one moment in two places. In any case they do not coexist. And this is built on the fact that contact is not among the akwan; otherwise there would be no obscurity in their coexistence, as in the substance surrounded by six substances. For to deny that, as reported from some, is stubbornness.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage clarifies the true nature of the four akwan (conjunction, separation, motion, rest). The author asserts that the reality of sukun – and by extension all akwan – is one: occurrence (ḥuṣūl) in a place. The differences are only by aspects (ḥaythiyyāt). An individual occurrence can be conjunction relative to one substance, separation relative to another, motion if preceded by a different place, and rest if preceded by the same place. Then he explains the claim of mutual contradiction (taḍād) among akwan. This claim means that two akwan that are distinct in existence cannot coexist in one substance simultaneously. The reason: if they require the substance to be in one place, they are similar (mutamāthilān) and cannot be together (the same effect cannot be realized twice); if they require two different places, they are contradictory because a substance cannot be in two places at once. The author adds that this logic assumes that contact (mumāssa) is not an akwan. If it were, then a single atom surrounded by six others would have six coexisting akwan, which is obvious and possible. Denying this possibility is mere stubbornness.

Key Terms
كون
أكوان
تضاد
حيثيات
اجتماع
افتراق
جوهر
مماسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four akwan are distinct, separate realities that cannot coexist.
Correction: They are one reality (occurrence in a place) considered from different relational aspects; their alleged contradiction is based on the impossibility of a substance being in two places at once.

Checkpoint
Why do two distinct akwan in the same substance cannot coexist?
A) Because they are always identical
B) Because they either demand one place (similar) or two places (impossible simultaneously)
C) Because akwan are imaginary and have no real existence
D) Because substances cannot have more than one akwan at a time

Key Takeaway
The four akwan share a single reality (occurrence in a place) and their 'contradiction' is only about the impossibility of a substance being in two places at once.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that akwan are one in reality but appear different due to aspects (ḥaythiyyāt). How might this realization remove false contradictions from your spiritual practice?
?
The author dismisses the denial of coexistence of akwan in a body surrounded by others as 'stubbornness' (mukābara). Is there a point in your own beliefs where you might be stubbornly rejecting a clear truth because it challenges a cherished assumption? What would it take for you to examine that assumption honestly today?

Definitions of Motion and Rest in Kalam
Sanusi presents two definitions of motion and two views on rest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of motion adopted by the mutakallimun and the two corresponding views on the definition of rest.

Core Idea
Motion is either a single leap or a series of rests.

The mutakallimun debated the nature of motion, building on two philosophical conceptions. This lesson unpacks their definitions and the associated implications for what counts as rest, setting the stage for later discussions on atomism and change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on motion definitions
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how the author first presents the philosophers' two possible meanings, then the mutakallimun's adoption of each.
The terms 'exit' (خروج) and 'entry' (دخول) are used relative to preceding and succeeding places.
2
Identify the two mutakallimun definitions
Find where the text states the two definitions: one based on 'occurrence in a place after occurrence in another place' and the other as 'successive occurrences in contiguous places'.
3
Understand the two views on rest
One group defines rest as any occurrence without requiring lingering, making motion a rest or sum of rests. The other requires lingering, defining rest only as occurrence in the same place after prior occurrence there.
The author notes the apparent wording suggests rest is the second occurrence, but the closer view is the sum of two occurrences.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these definitions affect whether motion, its parts, and the initial occurrence at origination count as rest.
This shows the nuanced discourse in kalam on fundamental concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سيجيء في طريق الفلاسفة أنه قد يراد بالحركة كون المتحرك متوسطا بين المبدأ أو المنتهى بحيث تكون حاله في كل آن على خلاف ما قبله وما بعده، وقد يراد بها الأمر الموهوم الممتد من المبدأ إلى المنتهي، والمتكلمون بالنظر إلى الأول قالوا إنها الحصول في الحيز بعد الحصول في حيز آخر، والناظر إلى الثاني إنها حصولات متعاقبة في أحياز متلاصقة، ويسمى بالإضافة إلى الحيز السابق خروجاً، وإلى اللاحق دخولاً، ثم منهم من سمى مثل هذا الحصول سكوناً من غير أن يعتبر في مسماه اللبث، والحصول بعد الحصول في حيز واحد، وكانت الحركة بالمعنى الأول سكوناً، وبالمعنى الثاني مجموع سكنات، وكان الحصول في أول زمان الحدوث سكوناً ومنهم من اعتبر ذلك، وفسر السكون بالحصول في حيز بعد الحصول فيه، فلم تكن الحركة ولا أجزاؤها، ولا الحصول في أن الحدوث سكوناً، ثم ظاهر العبارة أن السكون هو الحصول الثاني من الحصولين في حيز واحد، لكن الأقرب أن المراد أنه مجموع الحصولين كما قد يحمل قولهم الحركة حصول في الحيز بعد الحصول في حيز آخر على أنه مجموع الحصولين. قال ثم الحق يعني أن إطلاق أنواع على الأكوان الأربعة مجاز لأن حقيقة الكون، أعني الحصول في الحيز واحدة والأمور المميزة حيثيات وعوارض تختلف باختلاف الإضافات والاعتبارات لافصول منوعة بل ربما لا يوجب تعدد الأشخاص، فإن الكون المشخص قد يكون اجتماعاً بالنسبة إلى جوهر، وافتراقاً بالنسبة إلى آخر، وحركة أو سكوناً من جهة كونه مسبوقاً بحصول في حيز آخر أو في ذلك الحيز بل حركة وسكوناً إذا لم يشترط في السكون اللبث.
It will come in the way of the philosophers that motion may be intended as the moving thing's being intermediate between the beginning or the end, such that its state at every moment is contrary to what preceded and what follows, and it may be intended as the imaginary thing extended from the beginning to the end. And the mutakallimun, with regard to the first, said it is occurrence in a place after occurrence in another place, and those who looked to the second said it is successive occurrences in contiguous places, and it is called, in addition to the previous place, 'exit', and to the subsequent, 'entry'. Then among them are those who called such an occurrence a 'rest' without considering lingering in its meaning, and occurrence after occurrence in one place, and motion in the first sense was a rest, and in the second sense a sum of rests, and the occurrence in the first time of origination was a rest. And among them are those who considered that, and interpreted rest as occurrence in a place after occurrence therein, so neither motion nor its parts, nor the occurrence at the moment of origination, was a rest. Then the apparent wording is that rest is the second occurrence of the two occurrences in one place, but the closer view is that what is meant is the sum of the two occurrences, just as their statement 'motion is occurrence in a place after occurrence in another place' may be understood as the sum of the two occurrences. He said: Then the truth means that calling the four akwan 'types' is metaphorical, because the reality of akwan – I mean occurrence in a place – is one, and the distinguishing things are aspects and accidents that differ according to relations and considerations, not genuine differentiae. Indeed, it may not even require multiplicity of individuals. For the individual akwan may be conjunction relative to one substance, and separation relative to another, and motion or rest by virtue of being preceded by occurrence in another place or in that place. Rather, it may be both motion and rest if lingering is not stipulated in rest.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage expands on the definitions of motion and rest. It first references the philosophers' view, where motion is either an intermediate state (the moving thing being between start and end, each moment contrary to its neighbors) or an imaginary continuum. The mutakallimun adopt the first (discrete leaps) or second (successive occurrences) perspective. They call the movement relative to the previous place 'exit' (khurūj) and relative to the next 'entry' (dukhūl). The author then describes two groups regarding the definition of rest: one group calls any occurrence a 'rest' without requiring lingering, so motion in the first sense becomes a rest, and in the second sense a sum of rests, and the occurrence at the first moment of origination (ḥudūth) is a rest. The other group requires lingering and defines rest as occurrence in a place after a previous occurrence in that place, so motion, its parts, and the occurrence at origination are not rest. The author notes that the apparent wording suggests sukun is the second occurrence only, but the closer view is that it is the sum of two occurrences, analogous to how 'motion' is often taken to be the sum of two occurrences. Finally, he reemphasizes that calling akwān 'types' (anwāʿ) is metaphorical (majāz). The reality of an akwan (occurrence in place) is one; the distinguishing features are aspects (ḥaythiyyāt) and accidents (ʿawāriḍ) that vary by relations and considerations. They are not genuine differentiae (fuṣūl munawwiʿa). The same individual occurrence can be conjunction, separation, motion, or rest depending on what it is related to, and can even be both motion and rest if lingering is not stipulated in rest.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
حصول
حيز
خروج
دخول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is a continuous, smooth change of position.
Correction: Mutakallimun view motion as discrete leaps or successive rests, not continuous.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of motion for those mutakallimun who look to the second philosophical meaning?
A) Occurrence in a place after occurrence in another place
B) Successive occurrences in contiguous places
C) The imaginary continuum from start to end
D) A single rest

Key Takeaway
Mutakallimun defined motion either as a single leap or as a series of rests, and defined rest accordingly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the most significant spiritual changes in your life, do they feel more like a single, decisive leap or a series of small, subtle rests? Which description better captures your actual experience, and what does that reveal about your understanding of God's action in your life?
?
If every moment is a new creation and rest can be defined without lingering, what does that imply about your excuses to delay repentance or good deeds? Are you waiting for a 'continuous flow' of motivation, or will you take the discrete leap now?
?
The mutakallimun debated whether rest requires lingering—yet in your daily life, do you treat your spiritual state as static 'rest' or as a dynamic series of choices? How would your day change if you saw every moment as a new occurrence requiring a fresh intention?

Why Akwan Are Not Genuine Types
Sanusi argues that akwan are not real types but metaphorical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why calling the four akwan (conjunction, separation, motion, rest) 'types' is metaphorical, not literal.

Core Idea
The four akwan are distinguished by relations, not real differentiae.

After discussing motion and rest, the author addresses a common misconception: that the four akwan are distinct species. This lesson shows that their differences are merely relational, making the term 'types' a metaphor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on akwan
Read the Arabic carefully. Notice the claim that calling akwan 'types' is metaphorical (majāz).
The key terms: حيثيات (aspects) and عوارض (accidents).
2
Understand the reason
The reality of akwan (occurrence in place) is one; the distinguishing features are only aspects and accidents based on relations and considerations.
They are not genuine differentiae (فصول منوعة).
3
Example of one individual being multiple akwan
The same individual occurrence can be conjunction relative to one substance, separation relative to another, and motion or rest depending on precedence.
This shows it is not a fixed species.
4
Implication: no real multiplicity
The author even suggests that it may not require multiplicity of individuals, meaning one occurrence can have multiple labels simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سيجيء في طريق الفلاسفة أنه قد يراد بالحركة كون المتحرك متوسطا بين المبدأ أو المنتهى بحيث تكون حاله في كل آن على خلاف ما قبله وما بعده، وقد يراد بها الأمر الموهوم الممتد من المبدأ إلى المنتهي، والمتكلمون بالنظر إلى الأول قالوا إنها الحصول في الحيز بعد الحصول في حيز آخر، والناظر إلى الثاني إنها حصولات متعاقبة في أحياز متلاصقة، ويسمى بالإضافة إلى الحيز السابق خروجاً، وإلى اللاحق دخولاً، ثم منهم من سمى مثل هذا الحصول سكوناً من غير أن يعتبر في مسماه اللبث، والحصول بعد الحصول في حيز واحد، وكانت الحركة بالمعنى الأول سكوناً، وبالمعنى الثاني مجموع سكنات، وكان الحصول في أول زمان الحدوث سكوناً ومنهم من اعتبر ذلك، وفسر السكون بالحصول في حيز بعد الحصول فيه، فلم تكن الحركة ولا أجزاؤها، ولا الحصول في أن الحدوث سكوناً، ثم ظاهر العبارة أن السكون هو الحصول الثاني من الحصولين في حيز واحد، لكن الأقرب أن المراد أنه مجموع الحصولين كما قد يحمل قولهم الحركة حصول في الحيز بعد الحصول في حيز آخر على أنه مجموع الحصولين. قال ثم الحق يعني أن إطلاق أنواع على الأكوان الأربعة مجاز لأن حقيقة الكون، أعني الحصول في الحيز واحدة والأمور المميزة حيثيات وعوارض تختلف باختلاف الإضافات والاعتبارات لافصول منوعة بل ربما لا يوجب تعدد الأشخاص، فإن الكون المشخص قد يكون اجتماعاً بالنسبة إلى جوهر، وافتراقاً بالنسبة إلى آخر، وحركة أو سكوناً من جهة كونه مسبوقاً بحصول في حيز آخر أو في ذلك الحيز بل حركة وسكوناً إذا لم يشترط في السكون اللبث.
It will come in the way of the philosophers that motion may be intended as the moving thing's being intermediate between the beginning or the end, such that its state at every moment is contrary to what preceded and what follows, and it may be intended as the imaginary thing extended from the beginning to the end. And the mutakallimun, with regard to the first, said it is occurrence in a place after occurrence in another place, and those who looked to the second said it is successive occurrences in contiguous places, and it is called, in addition to the previous place, 'exit', and to the subsequent, 'entry'. Then among them are those who called such an occurrence a 'rest' without considering lingering in its meaning, and occurrence after occurrence in one place, and motion in the first sense was a rest, and in the second sense a sum of rests, and the occurrence in the first time of origination was a rest. And among them are those who considered that, and interpreted rest as occurrence in a place after occurrence therein, so neither motion nor its parts, nor the occurrence at the moment of origination, was a rest. Then the apparent wording is that rest is the second occurrence of the two occurrences in one place, but the closer view is that what is meant is the sum of the two occurrences, just as their statement 'motion is occurrence in a place after occurrence in another place' may be understood as the sum of the two occurrences. He said: Then the truth means that calling the four akwan 'types' is metaphorical, because the reality of akwan – I mean occurrence in a place – is one, and the distinguishing things are aspects and accidents that differ according to relations and considerations, not genuine differentiae. Indeed, it may not even require multiplicity of individuals. For the individual akwan may be conjunction relative to one substance, and separation relative to another, and motion or rest by virtue of being preceded by occurrence in another place or in that place. Rather, it may be both motion and rest if lingering is not stipulated in rest.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four akwan (conjunction, separation, motion, rest) are four distinct species with real differentiae.
Correction: They are distinguished only by relations and considerations; their core reality (occurrence in place) is one, so calling them 'types' is metaphorical.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is calling the four akwan 'types' metaphorical?
A) Because there are more than four types
B) Because the reality of akwan is one and the distinguishing features are aspects and accidents
C) Because the philosophers do not accept akwan
D) Because motion and rest are not real

Key Takeaway
The four akwan are not genuine species but relational descriptions of a single reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that the four akwan are not fixed categories but depend on relations and points of view. Where in your own understanding of Islam have you mistaken a relational attribute for an essential one? Will you examine that assumption today?
?
If the same reality can be described as both motion and rest depending on conditions, what does that teach you about the limitations of human language and categorization? When have you insisted on a label for something that could be seen differently?
?
Sanusi argues that calling akwan 'types' is only metaphorical. Are there theological labels you use to separate people or ideas that might be merely metaphorical? Would you be willing to drop a rigid category if it prevents you from seeing the truth?

Understanding the Contradiction of Akwan
Answers objection about verifiers' claim of absolute contradiction and begins atom-contact objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verifiers' claim that the four akwan are contradictory does not undermine their single reality, and how contradiction applies only to akwan distinct in existence.

Core Idea
Contradiction of akwan only applies to those distinct in existence, not to all aspects.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a critical objection: how can akwan be considered one thing when respected scholars like al-Qadi and his followers explicitly state that the four akwan are contradictory? The answer lies in distinguishing between akwan that are distinct existences and those that are not. This lesson unpacks that distinction and also introduces a second objection about the atom's six contacts, though the answer is cut short in the manuscript.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation without analysis. Let the structure of objection and answer become clear.
Notice that the passage contains two objections, but the second answer is incomplete.
2
Identify the first objection and answer
Locate the objection that verifiers like al-Qadi claimed absolute contradiction of the four akwan. Then find Sanusi's answer: they meant akwan distinct in existence, and contradiction is impossibility of coexistence.
Sanusi explains two cases: similarity (same place) and requiring different places.
3
Understand the reasoning behind contradiction
The two akwan are either similar (both assign substance to one place) — then they cannot coexist because each would be redundant; or each requires a different place — then they are truly contradictory.
This preserves the single reality of occurrence while acknowledging that distinct akwan cannot coexist.
4
Examine the second objection
The second objection asks about the atom surrounded by six others having six contacts. The passage begins an answer but breaks off. Consider what the answer might be: contact is not an akwan, so no contradiction arises.
The manuscript ends mid-sentence; the full answer may be on the next page.
5
Reflect on the key distinction
Why is it important that contradiction only applies to akwan distinct in existence? How does this resolve the apparent conflict between the verifiers' claim and the single reality of akwan?
This distinction allows akwan to have a unified meaning while still being exclusive in certain contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : كيف يصح ذلك والمحققون من المتكلمين كالقاضي وأشياعه قد أطلقوا القول بتضاد الأكوان الأربعة . قلنا مرادهم الأكوان المتمايزة في الوجود ومعنى التضاد مجرد امتناع الاجتماع ولو من جهة التماثل لأنهم احتجوا على ذلك بأن الكونين إن أوجبا تخصيص الجوهر بحيز واحد فهما متماثلان فلا يجتمعان كالحصول الأول والثاني في حيز واحد لأن كلاً منهما يسد مسد الآخر في تخصيص الجوهر بذلك الحيز، وإن أوجب كل منهما تخصيصه بحيز آخر فمتضادان ضرورة امتناع اجتماع حصول الجوهرين في آن واحد في حيزين. فإن قيل : أليس الجوهر الفرد المحفوف بستة جواهر على جهاته الست قد اجتمع فيه أكوان ستة هي مماساته لها، فإن منع ذلك ولم يجوز مماسة [؟]
For it is said: How can this be correct while the verifiers among the mutakallimun, like al-Qadi and his followers, have absolutely asserted the contradiction of the four akwan? We say: They meant the akwan that are distinct in existence, and the meaning of contradiction is merely the impossibility of coexistence, even if due to similarity. For they argued that if the two akwan necessitate the assignment of the substance to one place, they are similar and do not coexist, like the first and second occurrence in one place, because each one takes the place of the other in assigning the substance to that place. If each necessitates its assignment to another place, they are contradictory due to the impossibility of the coexistence of the substance's occurrence at one moment in two places. For it is said: Does not the individual atom surrounded by six substances on its six sides have six akwan coexisting in it, namely its contacts with them? If one denies that and does not permit contact [?]
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage presents two objections and answers. Objection 1: How can you claim that akwan have a single reality when authoritative scholars like al-Qadi and his school explicitly affirmed the mutual contradiction (taḍād) of the four akwan? Answer: The contradiction they meant applies only to akwan that are distinct in existence (mutamayyiza fī al-wujūd). Contradiction here simply means impossibility of coexistence, either because they are similar (like two occurrences in the same place, which cannot both be actual because each would be redundant) or because they are truly contradictory (requiring two different places, which is impossible). This does not apply to akwan that are not distinct existences, like the same occurrence viewed under different aspects. Objection 2: Consider a single indivisible atom (jawhar fard) surrounded on all six sides by six other atoms. It simultaneously has six contacts (mumāsāt) with them. Does this not imply six coexisting akwan (contact)? Answer: The text begins to answer but is cut off. The implied answer is that contact is not an akwan, so there is no contradiction. The author notes that denying such coexistence of contacts would be stubbornness (mukābara), as stated earlier. The incomplete ending suggests a continuation.

Key Terms
تضاد
كون
أكوان
جوهر
مماسة
محققون
قاضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verifiers' claim of contradiction applies to the single reality of akwan itself, making the concept self-contradictory.
Correction: The contradiction only applies to akwan that are distinct existences (e.g., two actual instances of being in a place). The single reality of akwan as a category remains intact.

Checkpoint
According to the answer given, what did al-Qadi and his followers mean by the 'contradiction' of the four akwan?
A) That the four akwan are inherently contradictory and cannot be found together in any sense
B) That the akwan that are distinct in existence cannot coexist, either due to similarity or requiring different places
C) That all akwan are metaphysically impossible
D) That motion and rest cannot both occur in the same substance

Key Takeaway
The verifiers' claim of contradiction among the four akwan applies only to those distinct in existence, not to the single reality of occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed that a general principle you hold in your faith cannot tolerate exceptions, only to later realize it had conditions? How does this lesson's precision about contradiction challenge you to examine your own assumptions about Allah's attributes? Will you seek refined understanding or remain content with oversimplification?
?
The verifiers refined their view to avoid error. Is there an area of your religious practice where you have clung to a vague or absolute statement about what is 'allowed' or 'forbidden' without considering deeper nuance? What would it cost you to investigate that nuance today?
?
The second objection hints at apparent complexity in God's creation. When you face apparent contradictions in life—between divine decree and human responsibility, or between mercy and justice—do you trust that there is a coherent resolution just as the mutakallimun trusted for the atom's contacts? Will you actively seek that coherence or passively accept confusion?

The Four Modes Are Metaphorical Types
The author explains that the four modes are metaphorical types and answers two objections about their opposition and contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the four modes (motion, rest, union, separation) are considered metaphorical types in kalām, and how the claim of their opposition is reconciled with that view.

Core Idea
The four modes are not real types; they are relational aspects of a single reality: occupying a place.

The author argues that the distinctions we draw between motion, rest, union, and separation are not essential but depend on how we consider the relation of a substance to a place. However, theologians also speak of these modes as being opposed to each other. This lesson explains that apparent contradiction by clarifying the meaning of 'opposition' in this context.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of motion and rest
Start with the opening lines where the author clarifies how motion and rest are understood in different senses, particularly in terms of occupation of a place.
These definitions set the stage for the main thesis.
2
Identify the main thesis
Notice the phrase 'ثم الحق' where the author states that calling the four modes 'types' is metaphorical. Understand the reasoning: the reality of a mode is one (occupying a place) and the distinctions are relational.
This is the core claim of the lesson.
3
Understand the first objection about opposition
The objection quotes al-Qāḍī and others who flatly state the mutual opposition (taḍād) of the four modes. Consider why this seems to contradict the metaphorical view.
The objection is based on a literal reading of 'opposition'.
4
Analyze the author's response
The author explains that 'opposition' here means merely impossibility of co-occurrence of distinct modes in the same substance at the same time with respect to the same relation. He provides the argument using two modes specifying the same or different places.
This response shows that opposition does not require real differentiate.
5
Examine the second objection about contact
The second objection presents the case of an atom contacting six surrounding atoms, implying six modes coexist. The author answers that those who affirm opposition do not consider contact (mumāssa) as one of the four modes.
This clarifies the scope of the four modes.
