الحصول في حيز واحد، وكانت الحركة بالمعنى الأول سكوناً، وبالمعنى الثاني مجموع سكنات، وكان الحصول في أول زمان الحدوث سكوناً ومنهم من اعتبر ذلك، وفسر السكون بالحصول في حيز بعد الحصول فيه، فلم تكن الحركة ولا أجزاؤها، ولا الحصول في أن الحدوث سكوناً، ثم ظاهر العبارة أن السكون هو الحصول الثاني من الحصولين في حيز واحد، لكن الأقرب أن المراد أنه مجموع الحصولين كما قد يحمل قولهم الحركة حصول في الحيز بعد الحصول في حيز آخر على أنه مجموع الحصولين. قال ثم الحق يعني أن إطلاق الأنواع على الأكوان الأربعة مجاز لأن حقيقة الكون، أعني الحصول في الحيز واحدة والأمور المميزة حيثيات وعوارض تختلف باختلاف الإضافات والاعتبارات لا فصول منوعة بل ربما لا يوجب تعدد الأشخاص، فإن الكون المشخص قد يكون اجتماعاً بالنسبة إلى جوهر، وافتراقاً بالنسبة إلى آخر، وحركة أو سكوناً من جهة كونه مسبوقاً بحصول في حيز آخر أو في ذلك الحيز بل حركة وسكوناً إذا لم يشترط في السكون اللبث. فإن قيل : كيف يصح ذلك والمحققون من المتكلمين كالقاضي وأشياعه قد أطلقوا القول بتضاد الأكوان الأربعة . قلنا مرادهم الأكوان المتمايزة في الوجود ومعنى التضاد مجرد امتناع الاجتماع ولو من جهة التماثل لأنهم احتجوا على ذلك بأن الكونين إن أوجبا تخصيص الجوهر بحيز واحد فهما متماثلان فلا يجتمعان كالحصول الأول والثاني في حيز واحد لأن كلاً منهما يسد مسد الآخر في تخصيص الجوهر بذلك الحيز، وإن أوجب كل منهما تخصيصه بحيز آخر فمتضادان ضرورة امتناع اجتماع حصول الجوهرين في آن واحد في حيزين . فإن قيل : أليس الجوهر الفرد المحفوف بستة جواهر على جهاته الست قد اجتمع فيه أكوان ستة هي مماساته لها، فإن من منع ذلك ولم يجوز مماسة جوهر لأكثر من جوهر تفادياً عن لزوم التجزي، فقد كابر متقضى العقل بل الحس، فإن تأليف الجسم من الجواهر عند من يقول بها لا يتصور بدون ذلك . قلنا : القائلون بتضاد الأكوان لا يجعلون المماسة منها أمراً اعتبارياً .
Occupying a single place, and motion in the first sense is rest, and in the second sense is a sum of rests, and occupying at the first moment of occurrence is rest. Some considered that, and interpreted rest as occupying a place after having occupied it, so motion and its parts and the occupation at the time of occurrence were not rest. Then the apparent meaning of the expression is that rest is the second of the two occupations in a single place, but the closer [view] is that what is meant is the sum of the two occupations, just as their statement 'motion is occupation of a place after occupation of another place' may be taken as meaning the sum of the two occupations. He said: Then the truth is that applying the names of types to the four modes (akwān) is metaphorical, because the reality of a mode, i.e., occupation of a place, is one, and the distinguishing matters are states and accidents that differ according to relations and considerations, not differentiating differentiae; rather, they perhaps do not necessitate multiplicity of individuals. For a particular mode may be union (ijtimāʿ) in relation to one substance, and separation (iftirāq) in relation to another, and motion or rest by virtue of being preceded by occupation of another place or of that same place—indeed motion and rest if rest is not conditioned by permanence. If it is said: How can this be correct when the verifiers among the theologians, such as al-Qāḍī and his followers, flatly stated the mutual opposition (taḍād) of the four modes? We say: What they mean is the modes distinct in existence, and the meaning of opposition is merely the impossibility of co-occurrence, even if from the aspect of similarity, because they argued for that by saying: If two modes both necessitate the specification of the substance to a single place, then they are similar and cannot coexist, like the first and second occupation of a single place, since each takes the place of the other in specifying the substance to that place; and if each necessitates its specification to a different place, then they are mutually opposed, necessarily because it is impossible for the two occupations of two substances to coexist in a single moment in two places. If it is said: Does not the individual atom surrounded by six atoms on its six sides have six modes coexisting in it, which are its contacts (mumāsāt) with them? For whoever denies that and does not permit contact of an atom with more than one atom, in order to avoid the implication of division, has opposed the dictate of reason, indeed of sense, for the composition of a body from atoms, according to those who hold it, cannot be conceived without that. We say: Those who affirm the opposition of the modes do not consider contact to be one of them in a real sense.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of the four 'modes' (أكوان) in kalām: motion, rest, union, and separation. The author first clarifies definitions of motion and rest, showing that they are relative. He then states the main point: calling these four 'types' (أنواع) is metaphorical because the essence of a mode is simply 'occupation of a place' (الحصول في الحيز), and the distinctions are relational accidents, not real differentiate. An objection is raised: how can this be when leading theologians like al-Qāḍī explicitly asserted the mutual opposition (تضاد) of the four modes? The answer clarifies that 'opposition' here means merely the impossibility of coexistence of distinct modes in the same substance at the same time with respect to the same relation. They argue by considering two modes: if both specify the substance to the same place, they are similar and cannot coexist (like two occupations of the same place); if to different places, they are opposed because a substance cannot occupy two places at once. A further objection is posed: an individual atom is in contact with six surrounding atoms, thus having six modes of contact simultaneously, which seems to contradict the claim that opposing modes cannot coexist. The response is that those who affirm opposition do not consider 'contact' (مماسة) as a true mode among them—it is not one of the four akwān that are subject to opposition. Thus the objection is based on a misunderstanding of what counts as a mode.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four modes (motion, rest, union, separation) are real, independent types with distinct essences.
Correction: The passage argues that they are metaphorical types; the essence of all modes is the same (occupying a place), and differences are relational.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is applying the names of types (أنواع) to the four modes (الأكوان الأربعة) considered metaphorical?
A) Because the modes are not actual occurrences but mental constructs.
B) Because the reality of a mode is simply occupation of a place, and the distinctions are relational.
C) Because the four modes are actually reducible to two: motion and rest.
D) Because the theologians disagreed on whether they are types.

Key Takeaway
The four modes are not truly distinct types but relational aspects of occupation of place; their 'opposition' merely means they cannot coexist in the same substance at the same time with respect to the same relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author teaches that the same 'occupation' can be seen as motion or rest depending on perspective. Think of a situation in your life where you have labeled your own actions as 'stagnation' or 'progress'—perhaps harshly. Might God judge that same action differently, as part of a deeper unity? Will you dare to stop calling yourself a 'failure' based on one angle, and ask God to show you the full reality?
?
The term 'opposition' is reduced to mere incompatibility in this passage. In your personal conflicts, do you treat others as inherently opposed to you, when really you are just occupying different 'places'? If you saw the 'other' as a different relation of the same substance, how would your heart change? Go and reconcile with one person this week, not by changing them, but by seeing the single reality between you.
?
The author insists that the four modes are not real types but relational aspects. What part of your identity have you treated as an unchangeable 'type'—your anger, your weakness, your loneliness—that is actually just a relation to circumstances? If you truly believed God could turn that 'mode' into its opposite (like motion into rest), what would you ask Him to transform today?

The Motion of Inner Parts and Two Disagreements
Sanusi states the truth about inner parts and examines two disagreements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the doctrinal truth about the motion of inner parts of a moving body and the two key disagreements about place and the mechanism of motion.

Core Idea
The disagreement is about real definitions, not just word choices.

This passage from Sanusi's Second Investigation presents a crucial claim: the inner parts of a moving body are indeed moving, while objects hidden on the ground or standing in the air are stationary by consensus. However, the matter is not straightforward—there are two underlying disagreements about the nature of place (ḥayyiz) and how motion occurs. Sanusi also clarifies that the opponents' position is not a mere terminological dispute but concerns the very realities these terms refer to.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the text. Pay attention to the structure: a truth statement followed by two points of disagreement.
The footnote defines 'rest' (sukūn) and explains its relation to equilibrium.
2
Identify the two disagreements
Find where Sanusi says 'الخلاف في الأول' and 'وفي الثاني'. These are the two key disagreements: one about the place of the inner part, the other about the definition of place and the mechanism of motion.
The first disagreement concerns the location (ḥayyiz) of the inner part; the second concerns whether place is the innate dimension or the inner part of the container.
3
Understand the clarification about terminology
Sanusi explicitly says that the opponent's position is not about renaming 'place' or 'motion', but about the underlying reality. This is a crucial point to avoid a superficial reading.
The phrase 'وليس مراد المخالف' introduces this clarification.
4
Note the two matters of difference
The passage ends by mentioning two matters where scholars differed on whether they are motion or rest. The first is the state of the inner parts; the second is not fully stated here but hinted at by the earlier example.
The footnote on rest provides additional background.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني : "الحق أن الباطن من أجزاء الجسم المتحرك متحرك، والمستتر على الأرض، أو الواقف في الجو عند تبدل الماء والهواء عليه ساكن لإطباق المقر والعرف على ذلك والخلاف في الأول عائد إلى الخلاف في حيز الجزء الباطن. وفي الثاني إلى الخلاف في أن الحيز هو البعد المفطور أو الباطن من الحاوي، وأن الحركة هل تحصل بزوال الحيز عن المتحيز حتى يمكن جهتي الحركة الواحدة في حالة واحدة أم لا بد أن يكون بزوال المتحيز عن حيزه حتى يمتنع ذلك . وليس مراد المخالف أني أجعل لفظ الحيز أو الحركة اسماً لهذا المعنى، بل إن حقيقة ما وضع الاسم في الأصل بإزائه هو مبدأ (۱) هذا فلا يكون نزاعاً في التسمية. اختلاف يشير إلى أمرين اختلفوا في كل منهما أنها حركة أو سكون(٢) . الأول : حال الأجزاء الباطنة من الجسم المتحرك . (۱) في (ب) بزيادة لفظ (مبدأ) . (۲) السكون ضد الحركة، وهو زوال الحركة عما من شأنه أن يتحرك، أو هو الحصول في المكان أكثر من زمان واحد، فإذا قر الشيء في المكان وانقطع عن الحركة وصفته بالسكون، وإذا كانت القوى المؤثرة فيه متضادة ومتعادلة وصفته بالتوازن لذلك قيل : إن في كل سكون توازناً كما أن في كل توازن سكوناً وثبوتاً واستقراراً.
The Second Investigation: "The truth is that the inner part of the parts of a moving body is moving, and that which is hidden on the ground, or standing in the air when water and air change upon it, is stationary, by the consensus of authoritative sources and custom. The disagreement regarding the first goes back to the disagreement concerning the place (hayyiz) of the inner part. And regarding the second, it goes back to the disagreement concerning whether place is the innate dimension or the inner part of the container, and whether motion occurs by the removal of place from the occupying object such that the two directions of a single motion are possible in one state, or it is necessary that it be by the removal of the occupying object from its place such that that is impossible. And the opponent does not mean that I make the word 'place' or 'motion' a name for this meaning, but rather the reality for which the name was originally placed is the principle (1) of this, so there is no dispute about terminology. Difference: It refers to two matters regarding which they differed as to whether each is motion or rest(2). The first: the state of the inner parts of a moving body. (1) In copy (B) with an addition of the word 'principle'. (2) Rest is the opposite of motion, and it is the removal of motion from that which is apt to move, or it is occurrence in a place for more than one time. So when a thing settles in a place and is cut off from motion, it is described as rest; and when the forces affecting it are antagonistic and balanced, it is described as equilibrium. Therefore it is said: in every rest there is equilibrium, just as in every equilibrium there is rest, stability, and settlement."
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement and classification. It opens with the Second Investigation (al-mabḥath al-thānī) and declares the truth: the inner parts of a moving body are moving, while a thing hidden on the ground or standing in the air when water and air change around it is stationary, by consensus. Then it identifies two disagreements underlying the topic. The first disagreement concerns the place (ḥayyiz) of the inner part. The second disagreement concerns the nature of place (whether it is the innate dimension or the inner part of the container) and the mechanism of motion (whether motion occurs by place leaving the occupying object, allowing two directions simultaneously, or by the occupying object leaving its place, preventing that). The author clarifies that the opponent's position is not merely a terminological dispute; the reality for which the term was originally set is the starting point, so it is not a mere naming conflict. Finally, the passage notes that there are two matters of difference regarding whether they are motion or rest, and the first is the state of the inner parts of a moving body. A footnote defines rest (sukūn) as the opposite of motion, explaining its meaning and relation to equilibrium.

The Correct Rulings for Two Disputed Cases of Motion and Rest
Taftazani states the correct rulings for two disputed cases of motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's resolution of two disputed cases concerning motion and rest, and the underlying disagreements about the nature of space (hayyiz) and motion.

Core Idea
Motion is objective, not relative; inner parts move with the whole, and stationary objects remain at rest despite changing surroundings.

In this passage, Taftazani addresses two specific cases where scholars disagreed whether something is moving or at rest. He clarifies that the inner parts of a moving body are themselves moving, and an object fixed on the ground is still at rest even when water or air flows around it. The disagreements, he explains, reduce to deeper questions about the nature of space and the definition of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its English translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the two cases mentioned.
2
Identify the two disputed cases
Find the two cases: (a) inner parts of a moving body, (b) stationary object with changing surroundings. Note Taftazani's ruling for each.
He says first is motion, second is rest.
3
Understand the underlying disputes
Taftazani explains that the disagreements reduce to disputes about the nature of space (hayyiz) and how motion occurs. Read his explanation carefully.
He mentions two sub-disputes: (1) what space is, (2) whether motion occurs by space leaving the occupant or vice versa.
4
Reflect on Taftazani's conclusion
Consider why he says this is not merely a terminological dispute but about reality. How do reason and custom testify to his rulings?
This reinforces the objective nature of motion and rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني : الحق أن الباطن من أجزاء الجسم المتحرك متحرك، والمستتر على الأرض، أو الواقف في الجو عند تبدل الماء والهواء عليه ساكن لإطباق المقر والعرف على ذلك والخلاف في الأول عائد إلى الخلاف في حيز الجزء الباطن. وفي الثاني إلى الخلاف في أن الحيز هو البعد المفطور أو الباطن من الحاوي، وأن الحركة هل تحصل بزوال الحيز عن المتحيز حتى يمكن جهتي الحركة الواحدة في حالة واحدة أم لا بد أن يكون بزوال المتحيز عن حيزه حتى يمتنع ذلك . وليس مراد المخالف أني أجعل لفظ الحيز أو الحركة اسماً لهذا المعنى، بل إن حقيقة ما وضع الاسم في الأصل بإزائه هو مبدأ هذا فلا يكون نزاعاً في التسمية. إشارة إلى أمرين اختلفوا في كل منهما أنها حركة أو سكون . الأول : حال الأجزاء الباطنة من الجسم المتحرك . الثاني : : حال الجسم المستقر المتبدل محاذياته بواسطة حركة بعض ما يحيط به من الأجسام كالحجر المستقر على الأرض في الماء الجاري، وكالطير الواقف في الجو عند هبوب الرياح والحق أن الأول حركة ، الثاني سكون بشهادة العقل والعرف .
The Second Investigation: The truth is that the inner part of the moving body is moving, and the one hidden on the ground, or the one standing in the air when water and air change upon it, is stationary, due to the agreement of reason and custom on that. And the disagreement regarding the first goes back to the disagreement regarding the space (hayyiz) of the inner part. And regarding the second, to the disagreement about whether space (al-hayyiz) is the innate dimension (al-bu'd al-maftur) or the inner part of the container (batin al-hawi), and about whether motion occurs by the removal of space from the occupant (matahayyiz) so that two directions of a single motion are possible in one state, or must be by the removal of the occupant from its space so that this is impossible. And it is not the intent of the opponent that I make the term 'space' or 'motion' a name for this meaning; rather, the reality of what the name was originally placed for is the origin of this, so it is not a dispute about terminology. An indication of two matters about which they disagreed as to each whether it is motion or rest. The first: the state of the inner parts of the moving body. The second: the state of the stationary body whose alignments change due to the motion of some of the bodies surrounding it, like the stone stationary on the ground in flowing water, and like the bird standing in the air when winds blow. And the truth is that the first is motion, the second is rest, by the testimony of reason and custom.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage begins the Second Investigation (al-mabḥath al-thānī) on the topic of motion and rest. Taftazani first states the correct view: the inner parts of a moving body are themselves moving, while an object that is stationary relative to the ground, but whose surroundings change—like a stone on the ground with flowing water or a bird in the wind—is actually at rest. He explains that the disagreement on the first case reduces to a more fundamental dispute about the nature of the space (hayyiz) of the inner part: is it the total space occupied by the whole body, or the specific space of that part? The disagreement on the second case reduces to two related disputes: (1) whether space is an innate dimension (al-bu'd al-maftur) that the object occupies, or the inner cavity of the container surrounding it; (2) whether motion occurs by the container's space leaving the occupant (allowing opposite directions in one motion) or by the occupant leaving its space (making that impossible). Taftazani clarifies that this is not a mere terminological dispute; the names 'space' and 'motion' were originally placed for specific realities, so the disagreement is about the realities themselves. He then identifies two specific cases of disagreement: (A) the inner parts of a moving body, and (B) a stationary object whose alignments change due to surrounding motion. He affirms with reason and custom that (A) is motion, (B) is rest.

الحيز
البعد المفطور
المتحيز
المحاذيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute is only about the meaning of words 'space' and 'motion'.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that the reality of what the names were originally placed for is the origin of the dispute, so it is about the realities, not just words.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the correct ruling regarding the inner parts of a moving body?
A) They are stationary.
B) They are moving.
C) It depends on the type of motion.
D) They are neither moving nor stationary.

Key Takeaway
The correct view is that the inner parts of a moving body are moving (not stationary), and an object at rest remains at rest even if its surroundings change, as supported by reason and custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the stone in the flowing water: it remains at rest even as everything around it moves. Where in your life do you allow the chaos of your surroundings to convince you that you are moving away from truth, when in reality your heart is still? What would it look like for you to trust that stillness this week?
?
The inner parts of a moving body are also moving. Do you ever assume that because your outer actions seem unchanged, your inner self is also stagnant? What hidden motions of your soul—toward pride, fear, or doubt—are you ignoring because you only look at the surface?
?
Taftazani insists that the dispute is not merely about words but about reality. When you confront a disagreement in your faith, do you easily dismiss it as 'just semantics'? How does this lesson challenge you to dig deeper into the realities behind theological disagreements rather than settling for surface-level labels?

The Case of Inner Parts: Arguments and Objections
First case arguments and objection-answer on inner parts motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam arguments for the motion of inner parts of a moving body, the objection based on leaving space, and the answer that redefines the space of the inner part as that of the whole body.

Core Idea
The motion of inner parts hinges on what we consider their space.

Taftazani presents two arguments for the motion of inner parts of a moving body, then gives the opponent's objection and an answer. This lesson unpacks these subtle points and shows how the debate reduces to a fundamental disagreement about the nature of space.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Notice the two arguments and the objection-answer pair.
The arguments use the concepts of separation (infikak) and containment within space.
2
Identify the arguments and objection
Find the two arguments for motion of inner parts and the opponent's objection. Understand the logic: if inner parts were stationary, the body would disintegrate; they are contained in outer parts which are in space, so they move with it. The opponent says inner parts have not left their immediate surrounding parts.
3
Reflect on the answer and its implication
The answer states the space of the whole is the space of the inner part. Ask yourself: what does this mean about the definition of 'space'? This will be the key to the broader dispute.
Taftazani later explains this reduces to disagreement about the space of the inner part.

وقد يستدل على الأول بأنه لو كان ساكناً مع حركة باقي الأجزاء لزم الانفكاك أي انفصال بعض الأجزاء عن البعض، وبأن الأجزاء الباطنة في الأجزاء الظاهرة، وهي في الحيّز فتكون الباطنة أيضاً فيه، وقد انتقل منه إلى آخر، وعلى الثاني بأنه لو كان متحركاً لزم التحرك في حالة واحدة إلى جهتين مختلفتين عند اختلاف جهات حركات الأجسام المحيطة به عليه، بأن يتحرك البعض عليه آخذاً من يمنته إلى يسرته والبعض بالعكس والكل ضعيف . احتج المخالف في الأول بأن الجزء الباطن لم يفارق حيزه الذي هو الأجزاء المحيطة به ولا حركة بدون مفارقة الحيز وأجيب بأن حيز الكل حيزه ، وقد فارقته وفي الثاني بأنه حصل في حيز هو ما يحيط به من الجواهر بعد الحصول في حيز آخر ، ولا معنى للحركة سوى هذا وبأنه قد تبدلت عليه محاذياته وهو نفس الحركة أو ملزومها . وأجيب بأن حيزه البعد المفطور وهـو بعـد حـاصل فيه، ولو سلم فالحصول في الحيز الثاني إنما يكون حركة إذا كان بزواله عن الأول دون العكس، وبأن تبدل المحاذيات إنما يستلزم الحركة إذا كان من جهة المتحيز بأن يزول من محاذاة إلى محاذاة، فظهر أن الخلاف في الأول عائد إلى الخلاف في حيز الجزء الباطن إنه حيز الكل أعني البعد المشغول به أو الجواهر المحيطة به أم ماله خاصة من البعد أو الأجزاء المحيطة به . وفي الثاني إلى الخلاف في أن الحيز هو البعد المفطور الذي لا يفارقه المستقر بتحرك الجواهر المحيطة، وتبدل المحاذيات بذلك أم الجواهر
And it may be argued for the first [case] that if it were stationary while the rest of the parts are moving, then separation (infikāk) would necessarily follow, i.e., the separation of some parts from others; and [also argued] that the inner parts are within the outer parts, and they [the outer] are in space (hayyiz), so the inner are also in it, and [the space] has moved from one to another. And for the second [case], that if it were moving, then movement in one state toward two different directions would necessarily follow when the directions of the motions of the surrounding bodies differ upon it—such that some [surrounding bodies] move upon it from its right to its left, and others the opposite. And all this is weak. The opponent argued in the first [case] that the inner part has not left its space, which is the parts surrounding it, and there is no motion without leaving space. And the answer was that the space of the whole is its space, and it has left it. And in the second [case], that it has come to be in a space that is the surrounding atoms after being in another space, and there is no meaning of motion other than this; and that its alignments (muḥādhayāt) have changed upon it, and that is motion itself or its necessary concomitant. And the answer was that its space is the innate dimension (al-bu'd al-maftur), and it is a dimension existing in it; and even if granted, the coming to be in the second space is only motion if it is by its removal from the first, not the reverse; and that the change of alignments only necessitates motion if it is from the side of the occupant (matahayyiz), by its removing from one alignment to another. Thus it appears that the disagreement in the first [case] goes back to the disagreement about the space of the inner part: whether it is the space of the whole, i.e., the dimension occupied by it, or the surrounding atoms, or what is particular to it of the dimension or the surrounding parts. And in the second [case], to the disagreement about whether space is the innate dimension from which the stationary object does not separate when the surrounding atoms move, and the change of alignments is by that, or the atoms.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage presents the detailed arguments and counterarguments for the two disputed cases of motion and rest. Taftazani first mentions two arguments for the first case (inner parts of a moving body): (1) if inner parts were stationary while the outer parts move, the body would disintegrate; (2) inner parts are contained within outer parts, which are in a space (hayyiz), so the inner parts share that space, and since the outer parts move, the inner must as well. For the second case (object stationary relative to ground but surroundings change), the argument is that if it were moving, it would have to move in two opposite directions simultaneously due to the differing directions of surrounding moving bodies—an impossibility. Taftazani calls these arguments weak. Then he gives the opponent's objections. For the first case: the inner part does not leave its immediate surrounding parts (its space), and motion requires leaving space. The answer: the space of the whole body includes that of the inner part, and the whole has left its original space, so the inner part moves with it. For the second case: the object is in a new space (surrounding atoms) after being in another, which is the very meaning of motion; also, its alignments change, which is motion itself or its necessary concomitant. Two answers are given: (a) its space is the innate dimension (al-bu'd al-maftur) that it always occupies, so it hasn't changed space; (b) even if it entered a new space, that is only motion if the object left its previous space, not if the surroundings change around it; similarly, change of alignments only indicates motion if the object itself moves from one alignment to another. Taftazani concludes that these disputes ultimately reduce to fundamental disagreements: for the first case, what is the space of the inner part—the whole body's dimension, the surrounding atoms, or its own particular dimension? For the second case, is space the innate dimension that the stationary object never leaves (thus no motion), or is it the surrounding atoms (implying motion)?

Key Terms
الانفكاك
الحيز
الأجزاء الباطنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the opponent's objection, why can the inner part not be considered moving?
A) It would cause the body to disintegrate.
B) It has not left its immediate surrounding parts, which the opponent considers its space.
C) It moves in two different directions simultaneously.
D) The outer parts are not moving.

Key Takeaway
The inner part's motion is accepted because its space is defined as the whole body's space, which moves with the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent argues that the inner part hasn't left its immediate surroundings, but the answer redefines its space as the whole. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes excuse your inner state as unchanged because your external circumstances haven't changed? But the lesson teaches that your inner self shares the space of your entire existence—your choices and actions. Are you truly moving with the whole of your life toward Allah, or are you stationary while your outer actions pretend otherwise? What concrete step will you take today to align your inner motion with your outer actions?
?
The initial arguments are described as weak. How often do you rely on weak justifications for your spiritual stagnation? This passage challenges you to seek firm grounds for your beliefs and actions. Reflect on one area where you have been using a weak argument to avoid change. What is the correct, strong argument that should move you, and will you act on it today?

The Case of Stationary Object and Changing Surroundings
Second case arguments, objections, answers, and concluding analysis on space definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument for the motion of an object stationary on a moving surface, the objections based on entering a new space and change of alignments, and the answers that distinguish between moving the object and its surroundings moving around it.

Core Idea
A stationary object does not move even if its surroundings change, because its space (innate dimension) remains fixed.

The second disputed case concerns an object that remains stationary relative to the ground while its surrounding bodies change positions. Taftazani presents arguments for motion and sophisticated responses, ultimately linking the debate to two different definitions of space. This lesson unpacks that exchange and the concluding reduction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second case
Read the Arabic from 'وفي الثاني' to the end. Notice the objection has two parts: entering a new space and change of alignments. Then read the two answers.
The concluding paragraph summarizes the two underlying disagreements.
2
Understand the objection
The opponent argues the object has entered a new space (the surrounding atoms) and its alignments have changed, which are the meaning of motion or its necessary concomitant.
3
Analyze the two answers
First answer: the object's space is the innate dimension (al-bu'd al-maftur) which it always occupies, so it has not changed space. Second answer: entering a new space is only motion if the object leaves its previous space; also, change of alignments only indicates motion if the object itself moves relative to the alignments.
The second answer is a two-part response.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
The concluding paragraph shows both disputes reduce to fundamental questions: for the second case, is space the innate dimension (no motion) or the surrounding atoms (motion)?
This connects the technical debate to broader definitions in kalam.

Motion and Space: Impossibility of Two Directions
The author critiques a philosophical definition of motion and its implications for direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the intellect rejects the possibility of a body moving in two opposite directions in a single state, and why this is not a mere terminological dispute.

Core Idea
Motion to two directions in one state is impossible; the dispute is about reality, not names.

This passage tackles a deep question: can a body move in two opposite directions at once? The author presents a philosophical definition of place and motion, then examines two interpretations. One allows such motion, but the author firmly rejects it, arguing that the intellect clearly sees it as impossible. He also warns against dismissing the issue as a mere verbal disagreement—it concerns the true nature of motion and place.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the technical terms: حيز (place), حركة (motion), محاذيات (alignments).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two interpretations of motion
The passage presents two ways motion could occur: either the body leaves its place and changes alignments, or the place itself is removed from the body. The first makes motion to two directions impossible, the second allows it.
3
Understand the criticism of Abu Ishaq
The author strongly disagrees with Abu Ishaq's conclusion that motion to two directions is possible. Note the phrase 'the intellect is certain that that is not motion.'
4
Reflect on the terminological dispute argument
The author refutes the claim from al-Mawaqif that this is just a naming issue. He argues that the definitions are about the actual quiddity (ماهية) and essential properties (ذاتيات), not arbitrary labels.
This shows the seriousness of the issue in kalam.
5
Connect to the learning objective
Now you should see why the impossibility of two-directional motion is a real, rational judgment, not a matter of convention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحيطة به على ما يناسب قول الفلاسفة من أنه السطح الباطن من الحاوي، وعلى هذا التقدير هل يتوقف حصول الحركة على أن يكون مفارقة الحيز وتبدل المحاذيات من جهة المتحيز البتة أم يحصل بأن يزول الحيز عنه، وعن محاذاته، وعلى الأول يمتنع حركة الجسم في حالة واحدة إلى جهتين مختلفتين وعلى الثاني لا يمتنع كما إذا تحرك بعض الجواهر المحيطة يمنة، والبعض يسرة على ما يلزمه الأستاذ أبو إسحق، وإن شدد غيره النكير عليه حقاً فإن العقل جازم بأن ذلك ليس بحركة وأن حركة الجسم في حالة واحدة لا تكون إلى جهتين، وما ذكر في المواقف من أن هذا نزاع في التسمية ليس على ما ينبغي لأن ما ذكره الأستاذ وغيره في بيان الحيز أو الحركة، أنه هذا أو ذاك ليس اصطلاحا منهم على أنا نجعله اسماً لذلك، وإلا لما كان لجلعه من المسائل العلمية والاستدلال عليه بالأدلة العقلية معنى بل تحقيقاً للماهية التي وضع لفظ الحيز أو الحركة وما يرادفه من جميع اللغات بإزائها، وإثبات ذاتياتها بعد تصورها بالحقيقة حين يحكم بأن هذا في حيز، وذاك في حيز (١) آخر وأن هذا متحرك وذاك ساكن
... [of that which surrounds it] according to what suits the statement of the philosophers that it is the inner surface of the container. And on this estimation, does the occurrence of motion depend on separation from place and change of alignments from the side of the located thing absolutely, or does it occur by the removal of place from it and from its alignments? On the first, motion of a body in one state to two different directions is impossible. On the second, it is not impossible, as when some of the surrounding substances move to the right and some to the left, according to what Master Abu Ishaq concluded, though others strongly and rightly rebuked him, for the intellect is certain that that is not motion and that the motion of a body in one state is not to two directions. And what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif that this is a dispute over naming is not as it should be, because what the Master and others mentioned in the explanation of place or motion—that it is this or that—is not a convention from them that we make it a name for that; otherwise, his making it a scientific question and arguing for it with rational proofs would have no meaning. Rather, it is an investigation of the quiddity for which the word 'place' or 'motion' and its synonyms in all languages are placed, and a demonstration of its essential properties after its conceptualization in reality when one judges that this is in a place and that is in another place (1) and that this is moving and that is at rest.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument about the definition of motion and place, specifically addressing the question of whether a body can move in two opposite directions simultaneously. The author first presents a philosophical view that defines place as the inner surface of the container, and then examines whether motion requires the body to leave its place and change alignments, or whether the place itself is removed from the body. On the first interpretation, motion to two directions in one state is impossible; on the second, it is not impossible, as illustrated by the example of surrounding substances moving in different directions, a position attributed to Master Abu Ishaq. However, the author strongly criticizes Abu Ishaq, stating that the intellect is certain that such a phenomenon is not true motion and that a body cannot move in two directions in a single state. The author then refutes the claim made in al-Mawaqif that this is merely a dispute over terminology. He argues that the definitions given by Abu Ishaq are not arbitrary conventions—otherwise, it would be meaningless to treat them as scientific questions requiring rational proof. Rather, the discussion is about the actual quiddity (mahiyya) of place and motion, and the essential properties (dhatiyyat) that must be established after proper conceptualization. The passage ends by affirming that when we judge that something is in a place and something else is in another place, and that one is moving and the other is at rest, these are real judgments about reality, not just verbal labels.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the dispute over whether a body can move in two directions is just a matter of agreeing on definitions, with no truth of the matter.
Correction: The author insists that the definitions are not arbitrary; they are investigations into the true nature (quiddity) of place and motion. The intellect firmly rejects the possibility of two-directional motion, so it is a real factual disagreement.

C) It is possible if the definition of motion is changed.
D) It is impossible only in a vacuum.

Key Takeaway
The intellect decisively rejects the possibility of a body moving in two opposite directions in one state, and this judgment is about the reality of motion, not mere terminology.

Classification of Spatial Location (al-Ayn)
Al-Taftazani classifies spatial location into real and figurative types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between real and figurative spatial location, its generic, specific, and individual types, and how it admits opposition and intensity.

Core Idea
Spatial location can be real or figurative depending on whether the container exceeds the contained.

This passage from al-Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid introduces the concept of al-ayn (spatial location). The author provides a clear classification that distinguishes between real and non-real location, further breaking it down into generic, specific, and individual categories. Additionally, he notes that spatial location can have opposites and degrees. Understanding this classification is essential for grasping later discussions in kalam about bodies and space.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the concise style and the logical flow from definition to subcategories.
The passage is short but packs multiple distinctions.
2
Identify the two main categories
Find the distinction between haqiqi (real) and ghair haqiqi (non-real). The criterion is whether the containing space (hayyiz) exceeds the object or not.
Example: water in a jug vs. Zayd in a house.
3
Examine the three subtypes
Note the classification into jinsi (generic), naw'i (specific), and shakhsi (individual). Understand each with the given examples.
Generic: being in a place; Specific: being in the air; Individual: being in this house.
4
Understand opposition and intensity
Observe that spatial location can have opposites (like above and below) and degrees (like the most above). Reflect on how these concepts apply.
This shows spatial location is not a simple binary but can be comparative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول الأين حقيقي إن لم يفضل الحيز على الشيء ككون الماء في الكوز وإلا فغير حقيقي ككون زيد في الدار أو في البلد أو في العالم، ويكون جنسياً ونوعياً وشخصياً ككون الشيء في المكان أو في الهواء، أو في هذه الدار، ويقبل التضاد كفوق وأسفل، والاشتداد كالأتم فوقية).
The first investigation: al-ayn (spatial location) is real if the space does not exceed the thing, like water being in a jug; otherwise it is non-real, like Zayd being in a house or in a city or in the world. And it is generic, specific, and individual, like a thing being in a place or in the air, or in this house. And it admits opposition, like above and below, and intensity, like the most above.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the concept of 'al-ayn' (spatial location). The author begins by distinguishing two main types: haqiqi (real) and ghair haqiqi (non-real). The criterion is whether the containing space (al-hayyiz) exceeds the contained object. If it does not exceed, as water exactly fills a jug, the location is real. If it exceeds, as Zayd in a house (where the house is larger than Zayd), the location is non-real. Next, the author classifies al-ayn into three specific types: jinsi (generic), naw'i (specific), and shakhsi (individual). For example, being in a place is generic, being in the air is specific, and being in this particular house is individual. Finally, al-ayn is said to accept tadad (opposition) like above and below, and ishtidad (intensity) like the superlative degree of 'above'.

Key Terms

الحيز
التضاد
الاشتداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spatial location is always real (the container exactly matches the contained).
Correction: Spatial location can be figurative (non-real) when the container is larger than the contained, as in Zayd being in a house.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of haqiqi (real) spatial location according to the passage?
A) Zayd in the house
B) Water in a jug
C) A bird in the sky
D) A fish in the sea

Key Takeaway
Spatial location is classified as real when the container exactly fits the contained, and non-real otherwise; it also has generic, specific, and individual subtypes, and admits opposition and intensity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author mentions degrees of 'above' (al-atam fawqiyya). In your relationship with Allah, are you content with the bare minimum of obedience, or are you striving for the highest nearness? What is one area where you can intensify your devotion to reach 'the most above'? Name it and set a specific goal.
?
Consider the opposition in spatial attributes (above/below). In your life, what false 'above' – like pride, status, or material pursuits – have you placed above your duty to Allah? How can you invert that order so that Allah is truly above all? Write down one practical step to humble yourself and exalt His command.

Classification of Location (al-Ayn)
Sanusi defines and classifies location (al-ayn) into real/unreal and by scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians classify location (al-ayn) into real vs unreal, by scope (generic, specific, individual), and its properties of opposition and intensity.

Core Idea
Location is not always what it seems: real location fits exactly, unreal location is loose.

This lesson explores the concept of location (al-ayn) as used in Islamic kalam. Al-Sanusi distinguishes between real and unreal location based on whether the container exceeds the contained, and further classifies location by scope and allows for opposites and degrees. Understanding these distinctions is essential for discussing motion and spatial relations in a theological context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's concise style and how each classification is introduced.
Arabic text may have minor OCR spacing issues; focus on the meaning.
2
Identify real vs unreal location
Look for the criterion: if the space (al-ḥayyiz) does not exceed the thing, it is real; otherwise, unreal. Examples: water in a jug vs Zayd in a house.
3
Understand the triple classification by scope
Location is divided into generic (jinsī), specific (nawʿī), and individual (shakhṣī). Examples: being in a place (generic), in the air (specific), in this house (individual).
This is a logical distinction, not a physical one.
4
Note opposition and intensity
Location can have opposites (e.g., above/below) and degrees (e.g., more above). This shows that location is not a simple concept.
Opposition and intensity are properties that apply to unreal locations especially.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول الأين حقيقي إن لم يفضل الحيز على الشيء ككون الماء في الكوز وإلا فغير حقيقي ككون زيد في الدار أو في البلد أو في العالم، ويكون جنسياًو ونوعياً وشخصياً ككون الشيء في المكان أو في الهواء، أو في هذه الدار، ويقبل التضاد كفوق وأسفل، والاشتداد كالأتم فوقية).
The first investigation: location (al-ayn) is real if the space does not exceed the thing, like water being in the jug; otherwise it is unreal, like Zayd being in the house or in the city or in the world. And it is generic, specific, and individual, like a thing being in a place or in the air or in this house. And it accepts contradiction like above and below, and intensification like the most above-ness.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage classifies the concept of location (al-ayn). It begins by distinguishing real location from unreal location. Real location is when the space (al-ḥayyiz) exactly contains the thing, like water in a jug — the jug's cavity perfectly matches the water. Unreal location is when the object is in a larger space, like Zayd being in a house, a city, or the world. Then it divides location into three types by scope: generic (jinsī), specific (nawʿī), and individual (shakhṣī). For example, being in a place is generic, being in the air is specific, and being in this particular house is individual. It also notes that location admits opposites (e.g., above and below) and degrees of intensity (e.g., being more above). The passage is a straightforward classification, preparing the ground for discussions of motion and spatial relations.

الحيز
حقيقي
غير حقيقي

شخصي
التضاد
الاشتداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all location statements refer to real, precise containment (real location).
Correction: This passage clarifies that many location statements are unreal because the container exceeds the contained, e.g., 'Zayd is in the house' is unreal location.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes real location from unreal location?
A) Real location involves physical contact; unreal location does not.
B) Real location occurs when the space does not exceed the thing; unreal location occurs when the space is larger.
C) Real location is only for liquids; unreal location is for solids.
D) Real location is generic; unreal location is specific.

Key Takeaway
Location in kalam is classified as real (space exactly contains object) or unreal (space larger), and further by scope (generic, specific, individual) with properties of opposition and intensity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you are 'in the world' — that is an unreal location. But where is your heart truly located? Is it firmly in the presence of the Real, or is it scattered across unreal spaces? What would it take for you to have a real location with your Creator?
?
The passage says location accepts intensification: you can be more or less above. Spiritually, are you content with a vague 'above-ness' or are you striving for the highest nearness to Allah? What is one step you will take today to move from unreal to real proximity?
?
You know that above and below are opposites. But in your life, where have you confused being 'in the world' (unreal) with being truly 'in the truth' (real)? Will you commit to examining one area today where your location is merely generic and make it personal and real?

Defining Motion as a Perfection in Potentiality
Author defines motion as a perfection from potentiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that motion is a perfection for the body from the aspect of potentiality, not from the aspect of actuality or substantial form.

Core Idea
Motion perfects the body in its potential, not its actual state.

Before diving into objections and nuances, it is essential to grasp the precise starting point: motion is considered a perfection of the body, but only from the angle of potentiality. This lesson unpacks that initial claim, setting the stage for the deeper discussion to follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage that states motion is a perfection from the aspect of potentiality, not from embodiment or shape. Note the emphasis on 'باعتبارها كان بالقوة'.
The definition is negative: it excludes actual perfections.
2
Identify the contrast with specific form
The author contrasts motion with 'الصورة النوعية' (specific form), which is a first perfection but from actuality. Notice how this clarifies what motion is not.
3
Reflect on the meaning of potentiality
Ask yourself: what does 'arriving at another place' (الحصول في المكان الآخر) have to do with potentiality? This is the key to the definition.
Motion is the body's potential to be somewhere else becoming actual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن من جهة أنه بالقوة لا جهة أنه بالفعل ولا من جهة أخرى، فإن الحركة لا تكون كمالاً للجسم في جسميته أو في شكله أو نحو ذلك، بل من الجهة التي هو باعتبارها كان بالقوة، أعني الحصول في المكان الآخر، واحترز بهذا التي ليست كذلك كالصورة النوعية فإنها كمال أول للمتحرك الذي لم يصل إلى المقصد، لكن لا من حيث هو بالقوة بل من حيث هو كمالاته بالفعل . واعترض أولاً بأن ماهية الحركة وإن لم تكن بديهية واضحة عند العقل، لكن لا خفاء في أن ما ذكر في هذا التعريف ليس بأوضح منها بل أخفى وثانياً : بأنه لا يصدق على الحركة المستديرة إذ لا منتهى لها بالفعل، فلا يتحقق كمال أول وثان وأجيب بأن هذا ليس تعريفاً للحركة يقصد بها تمييزها عما عداها أو تحصيل صورتها عند العقل، بل هو تلخيص وتبيين للمعنى المسمى بالحركة أينية كانت أو غير أينية فلا يضره كون تصوره أخفى من تصور ماهية الحركة، ولا كون الكمال الأول والثاني في بعض أقسام الحركة، أعني المستديرة بمجرد الفرض والاعتبار دون الفعل والحقيقة وذلك لأن كل نقطة تفرض فحال الجسم المتحرك على الاستدارة بالنسبة إليها من حيث طلبها توجه فيكون كمالاً أولاً ومن حيث الحصول عندها وصول فيكون كمالاً ثانياً . قال : وحاصل هذا المعنى : كيفية بها يكون للجسم توسط بين المبدأ والمنتهى مستمر لا يجتمع متقدمه مع متأخره وبها يحصل الجسم في حيز بعد ما كان في آخر، وحقيقته أمر واحد متصل في نفسه منقسم بحسب الفرض على قياس المسافة والزمان، وقد يقال الحركة لما يتوهم من كليته المتصلة الممتدة بين المبدأ والمنتهى ولا وجود لها في الأعيان لأنها قبل الوصول لم يتم، وعنده قد انقضت، وأما الأول فوجوده ضروري وعدم حصول المنقضى، واللاحق مع انتفاء الحاضر لأنه إن لم ينقسم لزم الجزء، وإن انقسم عاد الكلام لا يقتضي العدم مطلقاً كيف والمنقضى ما فات بعد الكون، واللاحق ما هو بصد الكون . يشير إلى أن ما ذكر بيان للمعنى المحقق الموجود من الحركة، فإن لفظ الحركة يطلق على معنيين أحدهما كيفية بها يكون للجسم توسط بين المبدأ والمنتهى بحيث لا يكون قبله ولا بعده وهي حالة مستمرة غير مستقرة أي يوجد المتحرك ما دام متحركاً، ولا متقدمه يجتمع مع متأخره، وبها يحصل الجسم في حيز بعد ما كان في حيز آخر ، وحقيقته كون في الوسط ينقسم إلى أكوان بحسب الفرض والتوهم وهو في نفسه واحد متصل على قياس المسافة والزمان فيما يفرض من حدود المسافة لئلا يلزم تركب الحركة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، وثانيهما الأمر المتصل المعقول للمتحرك من المبدأ إلى المنتهى . والحركة بهذا المعنى لا وجود لها في الأعيان لأن المتحرك مادام لم يصل إلى المنتهي لم توجد الحركة بتمامها فإذا انتهى، فقد انقطعت الحركة وبطلت بل في الأذهان لأن للمتحرك نسبة إلى المكان الذي تركه وإلى المكان الذي أدركه فإذا ارتسمت في الخيال صورة كونه في المكان الأول ثم ارتسمت قبل زوالها عن الخيال صورة كونه في المكان الثاني فقد اجتمعت الصورتان في الخيال، وحينئذ يشعر الذهن بالصورتين معاً على أنهما شيء واحد، وأما بالمعنى الأول فوجودها ضروري يشهد به الحس .
But from the aspect that it is in potentiality, not from the aspect that it is in actuality, nor from another aspect. For motion is not a perfection for the body in its embodiment or in its shape or the like, but from the aspect by which, in respect of it, it was in potentiality, namely, arriving at another place. And he guarded by this [that which is not like that], such as the specific form, for it is a first perfection for the moving thing that has not reached the destination, but not from where it is in potentiality but from where it is its perfections in actuality. First objection: that the essence of motion, even if it is not self-evident and clear to the intellect, yet there is no hiding that what is mentioned in this definition is not clearer than it, but rather more obscure. And second: that it does not apply to circular motion, since it has no end in actuality, so a first and second perfection are not realized. Answer: that this is not a definition of motion intended to distinguish it from others or to attain its form in the intellect, but rather it is a summary and clarification of the meaning called 'motion', whether it is in place or not. So it does not harm it that its concept is more obscure than the concept of the essence of motion, nor that the first and second perfection are in some types of motion, namely circular, merely by supposition and consideration, not in actuality and reality. That is because every point that is supposed: the state of the body moving in a circle relative to it, from the aspect of seeking it is a direction, so it is a first perfection; and from the aspect of arriving at it, it is attainment, so it is a second perfection. He said: The summary of this meaning: a modality by which the body has a continuous intermediate between the beginning and the end, such that its earlier does not coexist with its later, and by it the body comes to be in a place after it was in another. And its reality is a single thing continuous in itself, divided according to supposition, analogous to distance and time. And sometimes motion is said of what is imagined of its continuous whole extended between the beginning and the end, but it has no existence in external entities because before arrival it is not complete, and when it arrives it has passed. As for the first, its existence is necessary, and the non-attainment of the past and the subsequent with the negation of the present—because if it is not divided, then the part is necessary, and if it is divided, the argument returns—does not necessitate non-existence absolutely; how [could it]? For the past is what has passed after being, and the subsequent is what is about to be. He indicates that what has been mentioned is an explanation of the verified existing meaning of motion. For the word 'motion' is used in two senses: one, a modality by which the body has an intermediate between the beginning and the end such that it is not before it nor after it, and it is a continuous unstable state—i.e., the moving thing exists as long as it is moving, and its earlier does not coexist with its later, and by it the body comes to be in a place after it was in another place. And its reality is being in the middle, divided into [instantaneous] locations according to supposition and imagination, and it is in itself one continuous thing analogous to distance and time with respect to what is supposed of the limits of distance, so that motion is not composed of indivisible parts. The second: the continuous intelligible thing for the moving thing from the beginning to the end. Motion in this sense has no existence in external entities, for as long as the moving thing has not reached the end, motion in its entirety does not exist; when it ends, motion has ceased and become null. Rather, it is in minds, because the moving thing has a relation to the place it left and to the place it reached. When the image of its being in the first place is impressed in the imagination, and then before its disappearance from the imagination the image of its being in the second place is impressed, the two images come together in the imagination, and then the mind perceives the two images together as if they are one thing. As for the first sense, its existence is necessary, attested by sense perception.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is an extended discussion on the definition of motion (الحركة), addressing objections and clarifying two distinct senses of the term. The text begins in the middle of an explanation: motion is a perfection for the body not in its substantial form or shape, but from the aspect of potentiality—namely, arriving at another place. The author distinguishes this from the 'specific form' (الصورة النوعية) which is a first perfection but from actuality, not potentiality. Then two objections are raised: first, that the proposed definition is not clearer than motion itself; second, that it does not apply to circular motion, which has no actual endpoint. The answer is that this is not a strict logical definition but a clarification of the meaning, and circular motion can be analyzed by supposing points: each point sought is a first perfection, and arriving at it is a second perfection. Then the author summarizes the meaning as a continuous intermediate state with no coexistence of earlier and later, dividing conceptually like space and time. He then explicitly states that motion has two senses: (1) the continuous unstable state existing externally, where the moving thing is continuously in between—this is real and attested by sense; and (2) the conceptual whole from start to end, which does not exist externally because before completion it is incomplete and after completion it is gone, but it exists as a mental construct from successive images. The argument defends the reality of the first sense and the conceptual nature of the second.

الصورة النوعية

Correction: Motion is a perfection only from the aspect of potentiality, not from actual perfection of the body.

Checkpoint

?

Responding to Objections on the Definition of Motion
Author presents two objections and answers them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends his definition against two objections: obscurity and inapplicability to circular motion.

Core Idea
The definition is a clarification, not a logical definition; circular motion works by supposition.

Having laid out the initial definition, the author anticipates criticism: if motion itself is not self-evident, a definition using even more obscure terms is unhelpful; and circular motion seems to have no endpoint, challenging the first/second perfection framework. This lesson walks through the objections and the author's reply, which reframes the entire purpose of the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection section
Read the two objections carefully. The first complains about clarity, the second about circular motion.
Notice the structure: 'واعترض أولاً... وثانياً'.
2
Analyze the author's answer
The author responds that this is not a strict logical definition but a clarification. Then he explains how circular motion can be understood by supposing points as first and second perfections.
Key phrase: 'بمجرد الفرض والاعتبار'.
3
Apply the concept of supposition
Think about how every point on a circle can be seen as a destination sought (first perfection) and attained (second perfection), even though motion is continuous.
This shows the definition works even without actual endpoints.

But from the aspect that it is in potentiality, not from the aspect that it is in actuality, nor from another aspect. For motion is not a perfection for the body in its embodiment or in its shape or the like, but from the aspect by which, in respect of it, it was in potentiality, namely, arriving at another place. And he guarded by this [that which is not like that], such as the specific form, for it is a first perfection for the moving thing that has not reached the destination, but not from where it is in potentiality but from where it is its perfections in actuality. First objection: that the essence of motion, even if it is not self-evident and clear to the intellect, yet there is no hiding that what is mentioned in this definition is not clearer than it, but rather more obscure. And second: that it does not apply to circular motion, since it has no end in actuality, so a first and second perfection are not realized. Answer: that this is not a definition of motion intended to distinguish it from others or to attain its form in the intellect, but rather it is a summary and clarification of the meaning called 'motion', whether it is in place or not. So it does not harm it that its concept is more obscure than the concept of the essence of motion, nor that the first and second perfection are in some types of motion, namely circular, merely by supposition and consideration, not in actuality and reality. That is because every point that is supposed: the state of the body moving in a circle relative to it, from the aspect of seeking it is a direction, so it is a first perfection; and from the aspect of arriving at it, it is attainment, so it is a second perfection. He said: The summary of this meaning: a modality by which the body has a continuous intermediate between the beginning and the end, such that its earlier does not coexist with its later, and by it the body comes to be in a place after it was in another. And its reality is a single thing continuous in itself, divided according to supposition, analogous to distance and time. And sometimes motion is said of what is imagined of its continuous whole extended between the beginning and the end, but it has no existence in external entities because before arrival it is not complete, and when it arrives it has passed. As for the first, its existence is necessary, and the non-attainment of the past and the subsequent with the negation of the present—because if it is not divided, then the part is necessary, and if it is divided, the argument returns—does not necessitate non-existence absolutely; how [could it]? For the past is what has passed after being, and the subsequent is what is about to be. He indicates that what has been mentioned is an explanation of the verified existing meaning of motion. For the word 'motion' is used in two senses: one, a modality by which the body has an intermediate between the beginning and the end such that it is not before it nor after it, and it is a continuous unstable state—i.e., the moving thing exists as long as it is moving, and its earlier does not coexist with its later, and by it the body comes to be in a place after it was in another place. And its reality is being in the middle, divided into [instantaneous] locations according to supposition and imagination, and it is in itself one continuous thing analogous to distance and time with respect to what is supposed of the limits of distance, so that motion is not composed of indivisible parts. The second: the continuous intelligible thing for the moving thing from the beginning to the end. Motion in this sense has no existence in external entities, for as long as the moving thing has not reached the end, motion in its entirety does not exist; when it ends, motion has ceased and become null. Rather, it is in minds, because the moving thing has a relation to the place it left and to the place it reached. When the image of its being in the first place is impressed in the imagination, and then before its disappearance from the imagination the image of its being in the second place is impressed, the two images come together in the imagination, and then the mind perceives the two images together as if they are one thing. As for the first sense, its existence is necessary, attested by sense perception.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is an extended discussion on the definition of motion (الحركة), addressing objections and clarifying two distinct senses of the term. The text begins in the middle of an explanation: motion is a perfection for the body not in its substantial form or shape, but from the aspect of potentiality—namely, arriving at another place. The author distinguishes this from the 'specific form' (الصورة النوعية) which is a first perfection but from actuality, not potentiality. Then two objections are raised: first, that the proposed definition is not clearer than motion itself; second, that it does not apply to circular motion, which has no actual endpoint. The answer is that this is not a strict logical definition but a clarification of the meaning, and circular motion can be analyzed by supposing points: each point sought is a first perfection, and arriving at it is a second perfection. Then the author summarizes the meaning as a continuous intermediate state with no coexistence of earlier and later, dividing conceptually like space and time. He then explicitly states that motion has two senses: (1) the continuous unstable state existing externally, where the moving thing is continuously in between—this is real and attested by sense; and (2) the conceptual whole from start to end, which does not exist externally because before completion it is incomplete and after completion it is gone, but it exists as a mental construct from successive images. The argument defends the reality of the first sense and the conceptual nature of the second.

Misconception: A definition must be clearer than the defined term.
Correction: Here, the definition is a clarification, not a strict logical definition, so it need not be clearer.

Key Takeaway
The definition of motion is a clarification, not a strict definition, and works for circular motion through supposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this lesson's distinction between strict definition and clarification challenge your intellectual pride?
?

What might change if you approached your beliefs as a clarification rather than a strict definition?
?
The definition of motion works for circular motion only by supposition. Two Senses of Motion: Real and Conceptual
Author summarizes motion's reality and distinguishes two meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between motion as a real continuous process and motion as a conceptual whole, and why only the first exists externally.

Core Idea
Motion has two meanings: one real and continuous, the other mental and imagined.

After clarifying the definition and defending it, the author provides a condensed summary of motion's nature and then explicitly distinguishes two senses of the word 'motion'. This distinction is critical for understanding how motion is perceived by the senses and intellectually. The first sense is the actual, continuous state of being in between; the second is the complete path from start to end, which exists only in the mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the summary of the meaning
Read from 'قال : وحاصل هذا المعنى' to 'على قياس المسافة والزمان'. This is the author's own summary of the definition.
Key idea: motion is a continuous intermediate state.
2
Identify the two senses
Find where the author says 'فإن لفظ الحركة يطلق على معنيين'. Note the first sense (حالة مستمرة غير مستقرة) and the second sense (الأمر المتصل المعقول).
3
Understand why the second sense is not external
The author explains that the second sense (the whole from start to end) does not exist externally because before completion it is incomplete and after completion it is gone. It exists only in the imagination.
Compare with the first sense that is witnessed by the senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن من جهة أنه بالقوة لا جهة أنه بالفعل ولا من جهة أخرى، فإن الحركة لا تكون كمالاً للجسم في جسميته أو في شكله أو نحو ذلك، بل من الجهة التي هو باعتبارها كان بالقوة، أعني الحصول في المكان الآخر، واحترز بهذا التي ليست كذلك كالصورة النوعية فإنها كمال أول للمتحرك الذي لم يصل إلى المقصد، لكن لا من حيث هو بالقوة بل من حيث هو كمالاته بالفعل . واعترض أولاً بأن ماهية الحركة وإن لم تكن بديهية واضحة عند العقل، لكن لا خفاء في أن ما ذكر في هذا التعريف ليس بأوضح منها بل أخفى وثانياً : بأنه لا يصدق على الحركة المستديرة إذ لا منتهى لها بالفعل، فلا يتحقق كمال أول وثان وأجيب بأن هذا ليس تعريفاً للحركة يقصد بها تمييزها عما عداها أو تحصيل صورتها عند العقل، بل هو تلخيص وتبيين للمعنى المسمى بالحركة أينية كانت أو غير أينية فلا يضره كون تصوره أخفى من تصور ماهية الحركة، ولا كون الكمال الأول والثاني في بعض أقسام الحركة، أعني المستديرة بمجرد الفرض والاعتبار دون الفعل والحقيقة وذلك لأن كل نقطة تفرض فحال الجسم المتحرك على الاستدارة بالنسبة إليها من حيث طلبها توجه فيكون كمالاً أولاً ومن حيث الحصول عندها وصول فيكون كمالاً ثانياً . قال : وحاصل هذا المعنى : كيفية بها يكون للجسم توسط بين المبدأ والمنتهى مستمر لا يجتمع متقدمه مع متأخره وبها يحصل الجسم في حيز بعد ما كان في آخر، وحقيقته أمر واحد متصل في نفسه منقسم بحسب الفرض على قياس المسافة والزمان، وقد يقال الحركة لما يتوهم من كليته المتصلة الممتدة بين المبدأ والمنتهى ولا وجود لها في الأعيان لأنها قبل الوصول لم يتم، وعنده قد انقضت، وأما الأول فوجوده ضروري وعدم حصول المنقضى، واللاحق مع انتفاء الحاضر لأنه إن لم ينقسم لزم الجزء، وإن انقسم عاد الكلام لا يقتضي العدم مطلقاً كيف والمنقضى ما فات بعد الكون، واللاحق ما هو بصد الكون . يشير إلى أن ما ذكر بيان للمعنى المحقق الموجود من الحركة، فإن لفظ الحركة يطلق على معنيين أحدهما كيفية بها يكون للجسم توسط بين المبدأ والمنتهى بحيث لا يكون قبله ولا بعده وهي حالة مستمرة غير مستقرة أي يوجد المتحرك ما من حدود المسافة لئلا يلزم تركب الحركة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، وثانيهما الأمر المتصل المعقول للمتحرك من المبدأ إلى المنتهى . والحركة بهذا المعنى لا وجود لها في الأعيان لأن المتحرك مادام لم يصل إلى المنتهي لم توجد الحركة بتمامها فإذا انتهى، فقد انقطعت الحركة وبطلت بل في الأذهان لأن للمتحرك نسبة إلى المكان الذي تركه وإلى المكان الذي أدركه فإذا ارتسمت في الخيال صورة كونه في المكان الأول ثم ارتسمت قبل
