Definition and Classification of Rarefaction and Condensation
Al-Taftazani defines rarefaction and condensation and classifies them as motion in position and quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how rarefaction and condensation are defined as motions in position and quantity, and how they relate to concepts like tumor, obesity, and emaciation.

Core Idea
Rarefaction and condensation are motions of part arrangement, not simple growth.

In this passage, al-Taftazani unpacks two key terms in Aristotelian physics: rarefaction and condensation. He defines them as processes of dispersion and interpenetration of parts, and explains how they fit into the categories of motion. This lesson will help you see how these concepts are not merely about size change but about the configuration of parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the technical terms for dispersion (infishash) and interpenetration (indimaj).
These terms are foundational for understanding the rest.
2
Identify the definitions
Find how al-Taftazani defines rarefaction and condensation. Note that rarefaction involves distancing of parts allowing air to enter, while condensation involves approximation expelling air.
He also introduces subtypes: tumor, obesity, emaciation.
3
Classify the motion types
The passage explains that these motions belong to position (wad') because they concern the arrangement of parts relative to each other. But from the whole body's perspective, they are motion in quantity (kamm), and for individual parts, they are motion in place (ayni).
Compare with celestial sphere motion to solidify the concept.
4
Understand the significance
Ask yourself why this classification matters. It clarifies that rarefaction and condensation are not simple growth or shrinkage but involve change in the mutual positioning of parts.
This distinguishes them from generation and corruption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التخلخل والتكاثف للانفشاش (۱) والاندماج (۳) ، وهما مداخلة الهواء بتباعد الأجزاء وضده، وقد يكون ازدياد المقدار بورود الماء (۳) المادة على نسبة طبيعية، وهو الورم (٤) أو عليها، لكن لا في جميع الأقطار وهو السمن ويقابله الهزال) . يعني قد يراد بالتخلخل الانغشاش أي تباعد أجزاء الجسم بحيث تداخلها جسم غريب كالهواء، وبالتكاثف الانداماج أي تقارب الأجزاء بحيث يخرج ما بينها من الجسم القريب، وهما من قبل الوضع لرجوعهما إلى هيئة نسبة الأجزاء بعضها إلى البعض ثم لا يخفي أن هذا الانتقال بالنظر إلى الأجزاء حركة أينية، وأما بالنسبة إلى الكل فحركة في الكم على طريق النمو وإن لم يكن نمواً، وفي الوضع بحسب الداخل حيث تبدلت نسبة الأجزاء بعضها مع البعض، كما للفلك بحسب الخارج حيث) (٥) تبدلت نسبتها إلى الأمور الخارجة .
Rarefaction and condensation are for dispersion (infishash) and interpenetration (indimaj), and they are the interpenetration of air by the distancing of parts and its opposite. And increase in quantity may occur by the influx of water matter in a natural proportion, which is tumor (waram), or upon it, but not in all dimensions, which is obesity (saman) and its opposite is emaciation (huzal). It means that by rarefaction, dispersion is intended, i.e., the distancing of the parts of the body such that a foreign body like air interpenetrates them, and by condensation, interpenetration is intended, i.e., the approximation of the parts such that what is between them of the proximate body exits. And both are from the category of position (wad') because they revert to the configuration of the proportion of parts to each other. Then it is not hidden that this transition with respect to the parts is motion in place (ayni), but with respect to the whole, it is motion in quantity (kamm) in the manner of growth, even if it is not growth, and in position with respect to the interior since the proportion of parts to each other has changed, just as for the celestial sphere with respect to the exterior where their proportion to external things changes.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage defines two types of motion: takhalkhul (rarefaction) and takathuf (condensation). The author first states that rarefaction is for infishash (dispersion) and condensation for indimaj (interpenetration). He explains that these involve the distancing or approximation of parts, with air or another body entering or exiting the intervals. Then he notes that an increase in quantity may be due to the influx of matter in a natural proportion (which is tumor) or in a natural proportion but not in all dimensions (which is obesity), with emaciation as the opposite. The author clarifies that these are motions in wad' (position) because they concern the relative arrangement of parts. However, when considering the whole body, they are motions in kamm (quantity) similar to growth, though not true growth. He further distinguishes between motion relative to the parts (which is ayni, or local motion) and motion relative to the whole. He compares this to the celestial sphere's change in position relative to external things. The passage thus carefully classifies these motions within the Aristotelian categories.

حركة أينية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rarefaction and condensation are simply growth and shrinkage in size.
Correction: They are primarily changes in the arrangement of parts (position) and only secondarily affect the whole's quantity. True growth involves addition of substance, whereas rarefaction/condensation involve rearrangement and possible influx of matter.

Key Takeaway
Rarefaction and condensation are not merely changes in size, but motions that fundamentally involve the repositioning of a body's parts relative to each other.

The Many Forms of Increase and Decrease in Quantity
Al-Taftazani answers an objection about the limitation of motions in quantity and elaborates on growth, tumor, obesity, and emaciation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'motion in quantity' is not limited to simple increase and decrease; it includes distinct categories such as growth, tumor, and obesity, each with specific conditions.

Core Idea
Not every increase in size is growth—true growth is proportional and purposeful.

Al-Taftazani responds to an objection that motions in quantity seemed limitless. He agrees, but then refines the understanding: not all increases are the same. He distinguishes between growth, tumor, obesity, and their opposites, providing a detailed physical mechanism. This lesson explores these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening line where the questioner argues that on the first interpretation, motion in quantity is not limited to the four types. Notice that Al-Taftazani does not deny the lack of limitation.
The four types here refer to increase, decrease, rarefaction, and condensation.
2
Learn the distinctions among increases
The author distinguishes growth from tumor and obesity. Growth is a natural increase in all dimensions proportionally, with a specific time and natural end. Tumor lacks natural proportion; obesity is proportional but not in all dimensions and lacks a natural end.
Opposite of obesity is emaciation; opposite of growth is wilting.
3
Examine the detailed mechanism
Read the tahqiq section. It explains that growth occurs when new matter creates passages in the original parts and pushes them outward proportionally. Obesity occurs when new matter merely attaches without moving original parts.
Growth and wilting specifically involve motion of the original members themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فعلى الأول لا تنحصر الحركة في الكم في اعتبارات الأربعة . قلنا : لا كلام في عدم الانحصار وفي أن قولنا الانتقال من النقصان إلى الزيادة، لورود المادة نمو ليس على إطلاقه، وإلى هذا يشير قولنا وقد يكون ازدياد المقدار بورود المادة لا على تناسب طبيعي، وهو الورم أو على تناسب طبيعي لكن لا في جميع الأقطار وهو السمن، فإنه وإن كان ازدياد طبيعياً بانضياف مادة الغذاء إلى المغتذي كالنمو لكنه لا يكون في الطول على تلك النسبة ولا يختص بوقت معين ولا يكون له غاية ما يقصدها الطبع بخلاف النمو، ومقابل السمن هو الهزال (۱) ، فيكون انتقاضاً طبيعياً لكن لا في جميع الأقطار. وقد يقال له الذبول أيضاً، وتحقيق الكلام أنه إذا ورد على لجسم ما يزيد في مقداره فإذا أحدثت الزيادة منافذ في الأصل فدخلت فيها، واشتبهت بطبيعة الأصل واندفعت أجزاء الأصل إلى جميع الأقطار على نسبة واحدة في هو النمو وزواله بسبب انفصال تلك الأجزاء عن أجزاء الأصل هو الذبول ، وإذا لم يقو الغذاء على تفريق الأجزاء الأصلية والنفوذ فيها، بل انضم إليها من غير أن يتحرك الأعضاء الأصلية إلى الزيادة، وإن كان الجسم متحركا إلى الزيادة في الجملة، فذلك هو السمن وانتقاصه هو (ا) الهزال فالمخصوص باسم النمو () والذبول حركة الأعضاء الأصلية.
If it is said: Then on the first [interpretation], motion in quantity is not limited to the four considerations. We say: There is no dispute about the lack of limitation and that our statement 'transition from decrease to increase by the influx of matter is not growth absolutely', and to this our statement points: 'And the increase in quantity may be by the influx of matter not in a natural proportion, which is tumor, or in a natural proportion but not in all dimensions, which is obesity'. For it, even if it is a natural increase by the addition of food matter to the nourished like growth, yet it is not in length in that proportion, nor is it specific to a specific time, nor does it have an end intended by nature, unlike growth. The opposite of obesity is emaciation, so it is a natural decrease but not in all dimensions. And it may also be called wilting (dubul). And the detailed analysis is that when something comes to a body that increases its quantity, if the increase creates passages in the original and enters them, and becomes confused with the nature of the original, and the original parts are pushed to all dimensions in a single proportion, then it is growth, and its removal due to the separation of those parts from the original parts is wilting. And if the food is not strong enough to separate the original parts and penetrate them, but rather is attached to them without the original members moving to increase, even if the body is moving to increase in general, that is obesity, and its decrease is emaciation. So what is specifically called growth and wilting is the motion of the original members.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its detailed answer. The objection is: if rarefaction/condensation are motions in position, then motion in quantity would not be limited to the four standard types (increase, decrease, etc.). The author agrees there is no limitation, but then clarifies that not every increase is called 'growth' (numuww). Growth has specific conditions: it occurs in all dimensions proportionally, has a specific time period, and has a natural end. Other increases, like tumor (waram) and obesity (saman), lack these conditions. Obesity is an increase in natural proportion but not in all dimensions; its opposite is emaciation (huzal). The author then provides a detailed analysis (tahqiq) of the mechanisms. When new matter integrates with the body's original parts and pushes them outward proportionally, that is true growth; the reverse is wilting (dubul). When new matter merely attaches without penetrating or moving the original parts, that is obesity; its loss is emaciation. The passage emphasizes that growth and wilting specifically involve the motion of the original members themselves.

Motion in Quality (Istihalah): Examples and Deniers
Al-Taftazani introduces motion in quality (istihalah) with examples and refutes deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of motion in quality (istihalah), its examples, and the alternative explanations proposed by those who deny it.

Core Idea
Motion in quality is real gradual change, not just rearrangement of particles.

Al-Taftazani introduces the fourth category of motion—motion in quality, or istihalah. This refers to gradual qualitative changes like color or temperature. He provides clear examples and then critiques alternative atomistic explanations. Although his full refutation is cut short, the passage establishes the reality of continuous qualitative change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the examples of grapes and water.
2
Identify the definition of istihalah
Find how the author defines motion in quality and what it is called. Note the examples he gives.
3
List the deniers' views
The passage mentions three alternative explanations from those who deny gradual qualitative change. Write them down.
These views involve latent particles, incoming particles, or generation-corruption.
4
Understand the author's rejection
The author declares all these views false, though the full argument is cut off. Notice the strength of his dismissal.
The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the proof is in longer works.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الرابعة : الكيف كتسود العنب، وتسخن الماء مع الجزم بعدم الكون فيه أو الورود عليه . استحالة، يعني من المقولات التي يقع فيها الحركة الكيف (۳) ويسمى وذلك كانتقال العنب من البياض إلى السواد وانتقال الماء من البرودة إلى الحرارة شيئاً فشيئاً على التدريج وأنكر بعضهم ذلك، فمنهم من زعم أن في الماء مثلاً أجزاء نارية كامنة تبرز بالأسباب الخارجة فيحس بالحرارة، أنه ومنهم من زعم يرد عليه من الخارج أجزاء نارية ومنهم من زعم أن بعض أجزائه يصير ناراً بطريق الكون والفساد والكل فاسد بدلائل وأمارات، ربما تلحق الحكم بالضروريات على ما فصل في المطولات أدناها أن جبلا من
He said: The fourth: quality (kayf) like the blackening of grapes, and the heating of water with certainty that there is no [fire] substance in it or coming upon it. It is istihalah (transmutation). Meaning: among the categories in which motion occurs is quality, and it is called istihalah. This is like the transition of grapes from whiteness to blackness, and the transition of water from coldness to heat little by little gradually. And some denied that. Among them are those who claimed that in water, for example, there are latent fiery particles that emerge due to external causes and are sensed as heat. And among them are those who claimed that fiery particles come upon it from outside. And among them are those who claimed that some of its parts become fire by way of generation and corruption. And all are false by proofs and indications, perhaps including them among the necessary judgments, as detailed in the lengthy works. The least of them is that a mountain of...
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth category of motion: motion in quality (kayf), also called istihalah (transmutation). Examples are provided: the gradual blackening of grapes (change in color) and the heating of water (change in temperature). The author emphasizes that this change occurs gradually and that there is no pre-existing fire substance in the water nor does fire come from outside (denying atomistic or corpuscular explanations). He then notes that some scholars deny such motion and presents three alternative explanations: (1) latent fiery particles in water that emerge, (2) fiery particles entering from outside, or (3) some water parts turning into fire through generation and corruption. The author declares all these explanations false, referring to detailed proofs in longer works. The passage ends with the beginning of one such proof involving a mountain, but the text is cut off. The passage asserts that motion in quality is a real, continuous change, contrary to the deniers.

Changes in Quantity: Growth, Wilting, Fattening, Emaciation
Taftazani classifies four changes in quantity with definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani distinguishes four types of change in quantity—growth, wilting, fattening, and emaciation—based on the movement of original organs versus external addition.

Core Idea
True growth expands from within; fattening merely adds on from without.

In this passage, Taftazani delves into the physics of change in the category of quantity, distinguishing between processes that reshape a body from the inside and those that merely add external layers. He defines growth, wilting, fattening, and emaciation with precision, focusing on whether the original organs themselves move or not. This lesson unpacks these definitions and the underlying criterion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting how Taftazani uses technical terms.
The passage starts mid-sentence; accept it as is.
2
Identify the four terms
Find the definitions of النمو, الذبول, السمن, الهزال in the text.
Notice the contrast between growth/wilting and fattening/emaciation.
3
Understand the key distinction
Focus on the criterion: movement of original organs (الأعضاء الأصلية) versus mere addition.
This is the core of the classification.
4
Apply the concept
Think of real-world examples: a child growing vs. a person getting fat.
This solidifies the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نوعه، فذلك الأقطار. وقد يقال له الذبول أيضاً، وتحقيق الكلام أنه إذا ورد على لجسم ما يزيد في مقداره فإذا أحدثت الزيادة منافذ في الأصل فدخلت فيها، واشتبهت بطبيعة الأصل واندفعت أجزاء الأصل إلى جميع الأقطار على نسبة واحدة فهو النمو وزواله بسبب انفصال تلك الأجزاء عن أجزاء الأصل هو الذبول، وإذا لم يقو الغذاء على تفريق الأجزاء الأصلية والنفوذ فيها، بل انضم إليها من غير أن يتحرك الأعضاء الأصلية إلى الزيادة، وإن كان الجسم متحركا إلى الزيادة في الجملة، فذلك هو السمن وانتقاصه هو الهزال فالمخصوص باسم النمو والذبول حركة الأعضاء الأصلية.
Its type: that is the expansion in all directions. It is also called wilting. The verification of the discourse is that when something enters a body that increases its magnitude, and if the increase creates pores in the original and enters them, and becomes mixed with the nature of the original, and the parts of the original are pushed to all directions in a single proportion, that is growth. Its removal due to the separation of those parts from the parts of the original is wilting. If the nourishment is not strong enough to separate the original parts and penetrate them, but rather attaches to them without the original organs moving to increase, even if the body as a whole is moving towards increase, that is fattening. Its decrease is emaciation. So growth and wilting specifically are the movement of the original organs.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage classifies types of motion in the category of quantity (الكم). It begins by defining growth (النمو) as a process where additional material creates pores in the original substance, integrates with it, and pushes the original parts outward proportionally. Wilting (الذبول) is the reverse: the added parts separate. Fattening (السمن) occurs when nourishment attaches without penetrating the original parts, so the original organs do not expand; the body as a whole increases. Emaciation (الهزال) is the loss of such attached material. The author emphasizes that true growth and wilting involve movement of the original organs, unlike fattening and emaciation which are merely additive or subtractive.

Key Terms
النمو
الذبول
السمن
الهزال
الأقطار
الأعضاء الأصلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All increase in size is called growth (النمو).
Correction: Taftazani distinguishes growth, which involves internal expansion, from fattening, which is external addition.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, what distinguishes growth (النمو) from fattening (السمن)?
A) Growth involves a change in quality, while fattening involves a change in quantity.
B) Growth involves movement of the original organs, while fattening does not.
C) Growth is instantaneous, while fattening is gradual.
D) Growth is caused by external heat, while fattening is caused by moisture.

Key Takeaway
True growth and wilting involve expansion or contraction of the original substance, while fattening and emaciation are mere additive or subtractive changes.

Motion in Quality: Definition and Examples
Definition and examples of motion in quality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of motion in quality (حركة في الكيف) and the examples Taftazani gives.

Core Idea
Motion in quality is gradual change in a substance's attribute, not its location or substance.

Taftazani begins by defining a fourth kind of motion: motion in the category of quality. He gives two clear examples—grapes turning black and water heating—to illustrate gradual qualitative change. This sets the stage for the refutation of alternative theories that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once without analyzing. Let the wording settle.
The passage uses technical terms like 'الكيف' and 'الاستحالة'.
2
Identify the definition and examples
Taftazani explicitly says motion in quality is 'transmutation' (استحالة) and gives grape blackening and water heating as examples.
Notice the phrase 'شيئاً فشيئاً على التدريج' (little by little gradually).
3
Reflect on the significance
Why is it important to distinguish motion in quality from other types? Think about common assumptions about change.
This distinction prevents confusion with substantial change (kawn wa fasād).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: الرابعة: الكيف كتسود العنب، وتسخن الماء مع الجزم بعدم الكون فيه أو الورود عليه. استحالة، يعني من المقولات التي يقع فيها الحركة الكيف ويسمى وذلك كانتقال العنب من البياض إلى السواد وانتقال الماء من البرودة إلى الحرارة شيئاً فشيئاً على التدريج وأنكر بعضهم ذلك، فمنهم من زعم أن في الماء مثلاً أجزاء نارية كامنة تبرز بالأسباب الخارجة فيحس بالحرارة، ومنهم من زعم يرد عليه من الخارج أجزاء نارية ومنهم من زعم أن بعض أجزائه يصير ناراً بطريق الكون والفساد والكل فاسد بدلائل وأمارات، ربما تلحق الحكم بالضروريات على ما فصل في المطولات أدناها أن جبلا من كبريت يشتعل بقدر يسير من النار، فلو كان ذلك لظهور الأجزاء النارية الكامنة لكانت لكثرتها أولى بأن يشعلها ويحس بها، أو الواردة لكانت بقدر الوارد، وأن حرارة الماء الشديد السخونة، لو كانت بانقلاب بعض أجزائه نارا من غير استحالة لفارقته تلك النارية صاعدة بطبعها أو انطفأت ببرد الماء ورطوبته فلم يحس بها على أنك ستعرف في بحث الكون والفساد أن الماء لا يصير ناراً إلا بعد صيرورته هواء وحينئذ يتصعد بطريق البخار.
He said: The fourth: quality (al-kayf), like grapes becoming black, and water heating, while affirming that there is no becoming (kawn) in it nor arrival upon it. Transmutation (istihāla), meaning from the categories in which motion occurs: motion in quality (al-kayf). It is so called, like the transition of grapes from whiteness to blackness, and the transition of water from coldness to heat little by little gradually. Some denied that. Among them are those who claim that in water, for example, there are hidden fiery parts that emerge due to external causes, so heat is sensed. Others claim that fiery parts come upon it from outside. Others claim that some of its parts become fire through generation and corruption (kawn wa fasād). All are false, by proofs and indications that perhaps bring the judgment close to necessities, as detailed in lengthy works. The least of them is that a mountain of sulfur would ignite with a small amount of fire. If that were due to the emergence of hidden fiery parts, then because of their abundance it would be more apt to ignite them and be sensed; or if [the heat] were from parts arriving, it would be proportional to what arrives. And that the heat of very hot water, if it were by the conversion of some of its parts into fire without transmutation, that fieryness would separate from it rising by its nature, or be extinguished by the cold and moisture of the water, so it would not be sensed. Moreover, you will learn in the discussion of generation and corruption that water does not become fire except after becoming air, and then it rises by way of vapor.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage first defines motion in the category of quality (الكيف). The author gives examples: grapes turning black and water heating. He notes that some deny gradual change in quality. He then presents three false theories that attempt to explain how heat appears in water: (1) hidden fiery parts emerge, (2) external fiery parts enter, (3) some water parts become fire. All are refuted. The refutation uses a reductio: if hidden parts, then a sulfur mountain with many such parts would ignite with a tiny spark; if external, then heat would be proportional to the amount arriving; if conversion, the fire would either rise or be quenched. Additionally, water cannot become fire without first becoming air, a point proven in the discussion of generation and corruption.

Key Terms
الكيف
الاستحالة
التدريج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Taftazani mean by 'motion in quality'?
A) Change in substance
B) Gradual change in an attribute
C) Change in quantity
D) Change in location

Key Takeaway
Motion in quality is the gradual transformation of a thing's attribute without changing its substance or location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a personal quality you have been trying to change gradually—like patience, honesty, or humility. Have you been waiting for an external event to 'arrive' to fix you, or are you allowing the internal transmutation (istihala) to happen? What is holding you back from embracing that gradual change as a real transformation within yourself?
?
The passage says that motion in quality is not due to 'becoming' or 'arrival' of something from outside. Refutation of False Theories on Qualitative Change
Refutation of three false theories about heating water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's refutation of three false theories that attempt to explain qualitative change.

Core Idea
Gradual quality changes are not caused by hidden parts, external parts, or elemental transformation: they are truly transmutation.

After defining motion in quality, Taftazani addresses those who deny gradual qualitative change. He presents three alternative explanations—hidden fiery parts, external fiery parts, and conversion of water into fire—and refutes each with logical arguments and empirical observations. This lesson follows his critique and its implications for natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage listing the three false theories
Read the Arabic and translation to identify the three theories presented.
The theories are given in quick succession: hidden parts, external parts, conversion.
2
Understand each theory
Theory 1: Hidden fiery parts emerge. Theory 2: External fiery parts enter. Theory 3: Some water parts become fire.
These are attempts to explain how water becomes hot without true qualitative change.
3
Follow Taftazani's arguments against each
He uses logical and empirical arguments, e.g., the sulfur mountain reductio, and the behavior of fire in water.
Note the use of 'أدناها' (the least) showing the arguments are very strong.
4
Consider the conclusion
All three theories are false; qualitative change is genuine transmutation.
Taftazani ties this to broader discussions of generation and corruption.

Taftazani on Gradual Change: No Real Motion in Quality or Quantity
Taftazani argues that qualitative and quantitative change is not real motion, only instantaneous transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's argument that qualitative and quantitative changes are instantaneous and do not involve genuine motion, and how this differs from local motion.

Core Idea
Qualitative change is not motion—it is a series of instantaneous jumps between distinct states.

In this passage, Taftazani challenges a common assumption: that when something gradually changes in quality or quantity, it undergoes motion. He argues that such changes are not motion at all, because the intermediate states are actually distinct and each transition is instantaneous. To understand his reasoning, we need to examine the nature of the intermediate and why local motion is fundamentally different.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's concise style and logical flow.
Pay attention to the key terms like al-wasaṭ and daf‘ī.
2
Identify the main claim
Taftazani states that there is no real motion (ḥaraka) in qualitative or quantitative change. Find the sentence that expresses this claim.
Look for: 'لا حركة في نفس الأمر'
3
Understand the verification
Follow the author's argument about the intermediate (al-wasaṭ). He considers two possibilities: one intermediate or many. What does each imply?
If one, no motion; if many, impossible because it leads to indivisible parts (atomism).
4
Contrast with local motion
Taftazani distinguishes qualitative/quantitative change from local motion (al-ḥaraka al-ayniyya). How is the intermediate in local motion different?
In local motion, the intermediate is continuous and can be divided infinitely.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument matters for kalām cosmology. It defends the notion that only spatial translation is true motion, and that change in quality/quantity is immediate.
This relates to the debate on atomism and the nature of change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والحق أنهم لما وجدوا الجسم انتقل من كم أو كيف إلى آخر لا دفعة توهموا حركة ولا حركة في نفس الأمر لأن ما بين الطرفين من الكميات والكيفيات متمايزة بالفعل لا كأجزاء المسافة والانتقال إلى كل دفعي كالأرض تصير ماء ثم ناراً، وتحقيقه أن للوسط إن كان واحداً فلا حركة وإن كان كثيراً ضرورة كونه بين حاصرين فتكون الحركة من أجزاء لا تنقسم وهو محال لاستلزامه وجود الجوهر الفرد وكون البطء لتخلل السكنات بخلاف الحركة الأينية. فإن الوسط فيها واحد بالفعل يقبل بحسب الغرض انقسامات غير متناهية. قد سبقت إشارة إلى أن الحركة الوضعية عائدة إلى الحركة الأينية.
He said: And the truth is that when they found the body transitioning from one quantity or quality to another not instantaneously, they imagined motion, but there is no motion in reality. Because what is between the two extremes of quantities and qualities is actually distinct, not like parts of a distance. And the transition to each is instantaneous, like earth becoming water then fire. Its verification is that the intermediate (al-wasaṭ) if it is one, then there is no motion; and if it is many, necessarily being between two boundaries, then motion would consist of indivisible parts, which is impossible because it entails the existence of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) and that slowness is due to interposed rests. This is unlike local motion (al-ḥaraka al-ayniyya), for in it the intermediate is one in actuality, accepting in accordance with the purpose infinite divisions. Reference has already been made that positional motion (al-ḥaraka al-waḍ‘iyya) reduces to local motion.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage presents the author's own view on qualitative and quantitative change. He argues that people observed a body changing gradually and assumed motion, but in reality there is no motion (ḥaraka) in such changes. The intermediate states between the starting and ending qualities or quantities are actually distinct and actual (mutamayyiza bi al-fi‘l), unlike the continuous parts of a spatial distance. Each transition from one state to the next is instantaneous (daf‘ī), like earth becoming water then fire. The verification: if the intermediate (al-wasaṭ) is a single state, there is no motion; if there are many states, they must be finite because they are between two boundaries, so motion would consist of indivisible parts (ajzā’ lā tanqasim), which is impossible as it would imply the existence of the atom (al-jawhar al-fard) and that slowness is due to interposed rests (sakanāt). This is different from local motion (al-ḥaraka al-ayniyya), where the intermediate is a continuous spatial distance that can be divided infinitely. The author also notes that positional motion reduces to local motion.

Key Terms
الحركة
الدفعي
الوسط
الجوهر الفرد
السكنات
الحركة الأينية
الحركة الوضعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All apparent gradual changes involve real motion.
Correction: Taftazani argues that qualitative and quantitative changes are not real motion; they are instantaneous transitions between distinct states. Only local motion (spatial translation) is genuine motion.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, why is there no real motion in qualitative or quantitative change?
A) Because such changes occur instantaneously, not gradually.
B) Because the intermediate states are continuous like spatial distances.
C) Because quality and quantity are not real categories.
D) Because motion only exists in the mind.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani restricts genuine motion to spatial translation; qualitative and quantitative changes are instantaneous transitions between distinct states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Illusion of Motion in Quantity and Quality
The author identifies the source of the illusion that motion occurs in quantity and quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why gradual change in quantity and quality is commonly mistaken for motion and what true motion requires.

Core Idea
Gradual change is not motion unless it is non-instantaneous in essence and parts.

The author begins by addressing a common misconception: we observe gradual transitions in size, color, or other attributes and assume motion is occurring. But careful analysis shows these are instantaneous jumps between distinct states, not true gradual motion. This lesson unpacks the source of that illusion and sets the stage for the proof against motion in quantity and quality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage describing the illusion
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author identifies the source of the false belief.
The passage explains why we imagine motion where there is none.
2
Understand the definition of motion
The author states that motion is conceived as gradual change (tadrīj) that is not instantaneous in essence or parts. Highlight this definition.
This is key for the argument.
3
Identify the true nature of transitions
The changes in quantity and quality are actually a series of instantaneous transitions (dafʿāt). See how the author contrasts this with true gradualness.
The intermediate states are actually distinct.
4
Reflect on the example
Consider the example of earth turning to water, then air, then fire. Why is this not substantial motion?
The example illustrates the concept.

فههنا يريد نفي الحركة في الكم والكيف مع التنبيه على منشأ توهمهما، وذلك أنا نجد الجسم ينتقل على سبيل التدريج من كمية إلى كمية أخرى أزيد أو أنقص ومن كيفية إلى كيفية أخرى تضاد الأولى أو تماثلها من غير أن يظهر لنا تفاصيل ذلك وأزمنة وجود كل منها فنتوهم أن ذلك حركة إذ لا تعقل من الحركة إلا تغيراً على التدريج لكن لا حركة عند التحقيق لأن معنى التدريج المعتبر في الحركة أن لا يكون دفعة لا بحسب الذات، ولا بحسب الأجزاء والانتقال ههنا إنما هو دفعات يتوهم من اجتماعها التدريج لأن ما بين المبدأ والمنتهى من مراتب الكميات أو الكيفيات متمايزة بالفعل، ينتقل الجسم من كل منها إلى آخر دفعة كما في صيرورة الأرض ماء ثم هواء ثم ناراً مع الاتفاق ذلك ليس حركة جوهرية من الأرض إلى النار لظهور تفاصيل على أن مجموع المراتب وأزمنة وجوداتها ويدل على نفي الحركة في الأمور المتمايزة بالفعل سواء كانت كميات أو كيفيات أو جواهر أن الوسط بين المبدأ والمنتهى إن كان واحداً فظاهر أنه لا حركة، وإن كان كثيراً فتلك الكثرة سواء كان اختلافها بالنوع أو بالعدد أما أن تكون غير متناهية وهو محال ضرورة كونها محصورة بين حاصرين، وإما أن تكون متناهية وهو يستلزم تركب الحركة من أمور لا تقبل القسمة إذ لو انقسمت إلى أمور متغايرة ننقل الكلام إلى كل واحد منها، وهلم جرا، فيكون ما فرض متناهياً غير متناه هذا خلف وتركب الحركة مما لا يقبل الانقسام باطل لاستلزامه وجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وكون البطء لتخلل السكنات. أما الأول فلانطباق الحركة على ما فيه الحركة . أما الثاني فلأن السريع إذا تحرك جزءاً فالبطيء إن تحرك مثله دائماً لزم تساويهما أو أكثر لزم كونه أسرع أو أقل لزم انقسام ما لا ينقسم، فلم يبق إلا أن يكون له فيما بين أجزاء الحركة سكنات . وسيجيء بيان بطلان اللازمين وهذا بخلاف الحركة الأينية، فإن الوسط الذي بين المبدأ والمنتهى. أعني امتداد المسافة واحد بالفعل، يقبل بحسب الفرض انقسامات غير متناهية .

فإن قيل : يجوز أن يكون كل واحد من تلك الآحاد المتناهية قابلاً لانقسامات غير متناهية، فلا يلزم تركب الحركة مما لا تقبل الانقسام .

قلنا : هذا غير مقيد إذ التقدير أن الانتقال إلى كل من تلك الأحاد دفعى والحاصل أن امتناع تركب الحركة مما لا ينقسم يقتضي أن يكون امتدادها منطبقاً على أمر قابل لانقسامات غير متناهية على ما هو شأن لكم واحد كما في الحركة في الماء، أو الأجسام مختلفة كما في الحركة من الأرض إلى السماء لا على كم منفصل متناهي الآحاد سواء كان معروضه جوهراً، أو كماً متصلاً أو كيف أو غير ذلك، وبهذا يندفع ما يتوهم من أنه إذا جازت الحركة في المسافة لكونها معروضة لما يقبل الانقسام لا إلى نهاية، ففي الكم القابل لذلك بحسب ذاته أولى .
Here he intends to negate motion in quantity and quality while drawing attention to the source of their illusion. That is because we find the body gradually transitioning from one quantity to another greater or lesser, and from one quality to another contrary or similar, without the details and times of each appearing to us, so we imagine that this is motion, since motion is only conceived as gradual change. But in truth there is no motion, because the meaning of gradualness considered in motion is that it is not instantaneous neither in essence nor in parts, and the transition here is only instants of which gradualness is imagined, because the intermediate ranks of quantities or qualities between the beginning and end are actually distinct; the body transitions from each to the next instantaneously, as in the transformation of earth into water, then air, then fire—although this is not a substantial motion from earth to fire due to the appearance of details, but the totality of ranks and times of their existence [indicates...]. And it indicates the negation of motion in things that are actually distinct, whether quantities, qualities, or substances, that the middle between the beginning and end, if it is one, obviously there is no motion; if it is many, that multiplicity—whether its difference is by species or number—must either be infinite, which is impossible because it is bounded between two bounds, or finite, which entails composition of motion from indivisible parts, because if it were divided into different things, we would repeat the argument for each one, and so on, so what was assumed finite becomes infinite—this is a contradiction. And composition of motion from what does not admit division is false because it entails the existence of the indivisible part, and that slowness is due to the interpolation of rests. As for the first, [it is false] because of the application of motion to that in which motion occurs. As for the second, because if the quick moves a part, then if the slow always moves the like, they would be equal; if more, it would be faster; if less, it would entail division of what does not divide. So it remains only that it has rests between the parts of motion. And the proof of the falsity of both implications will come. This is unlike motion in place, for the middle between the beginning and end—i.e., the extension of distance—is one in actuality, admitting by assumption infinite divisions.

If it is said: It is possible that each of those finite units is divisible into infinite divisions, so the composition of motion from what does not admit division does not follow.

We reply: This is not valid, for the estimation is that the transition to each of those units is instantaneous. And the gist is that the impossibility of composition of motion from what does not divide requires that its extension corresponds to something susceptible to infinite divisions, as is the case for each one, like motion in water, or the bodies are different as in motion from earth to sky, not upon a discrete quantity with finite units, whether its substrate is substance, or continuous quantity, or quality, or other. And by this is refuted what is imagined, that if motion is permissible in distance because it is a substrate for what admits infinite division, then in quantity that admits that by itself, it is even more appropriate.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) refuting the notion of motion in the categories of quantity (al-kamm) and quality (al-kayf). The author begins by explaining the source of the illusion: we observe gradual transition in quantity and quality and mistakenly think it is motion, but true motion requires gradualness without instantaneousness. The transition here is actually a sequence of instantaneous changes (daf'at). The proof then argues that the intermediate stages between the beginning and end are actually distinct (mutamayyiza bi'l-fi'l). If the middle is one, there is no motion. If it is many, the multiplicity must be either infinite or finite. Infinite is impossible because it is bounded between two bounds. Finite entails motion composed of indivisible parts (ma la yaqbal al-qisma), because if we divide it, we repeat the argument, leading to an infinite regress. This composition is false because it implies the existence of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajzza) and that slowness is caused by rests (sakanat) between movements. He then refutes two implications: first, that the motion would apply to something that allows rest, and second, that if the quick moves a part, the slow must either match it (be equal), exceed it (be faster), or fall short (requiring division of the indivisible). The only remaining possibility is rests. He contrasts this with motion in place (al-haraka al-ayniyya), where the distance is a continuous extension admitting infinite divisions. An objection is raised: each finite unit might be infinitely divisible. The answer is that the transition to each unit is instantaneous, and the argument requires the overall extension to be continuous, not discrete. This refutes the mistaken notion that quantity, being intrinsically divisible, is even more fit for motion than distance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Gradual change in size or color is motion.
Correction: True motion requires non-instantaneous change in essence or parts; changes in quantity/quality are instantaneous jumps between actually distinct states.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of motion?
A) Any change over time.
B) Gradual change that is not instantaneous in essence or parts.
C) Change in location.
D) Sequential transitions.

Key Takeaway
True motion requires gradualness without instantaneousness in essence or parts; apparent gradual change in quantity/quality is a series of instantaneous jumps.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own spiritual growth, do you assume it is a steady, continuous motion toward Allah? What if each act of obedience or sin is an instantaneous jump between distinct states? How does that awareness change your responsibility for each moment?
?
The passage shows that our perception often masks the true discrete nature of change. In what area of your life have you been deceived by the illusion of gradualness, assuming you are gradually improving when in fact you are only making a series of unconnected choices? What will you do today to make each choice a deliberate jump toward righteousness?
?
Consider the transformation of earth into water, air, and fire: it appears continuous but is actually separate essences. Similarly, your character may seem to change gradually. Are you content with the speed of your change, or do you realize that each instant offers a chance for a radical transformation? Will you seize the next instant for a decisive turn?

The Proof: Motion Cannot Be Composed of Indivisible Parts
The author gives a proof that motion in actually distinct things (like quantity and quality) is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that motion in quantity and quality leads to absurdities such as composition from indivisible parts and infinite regress.

Core Idea
If motion occurred in actually distinct intermediates, it would require indivisible parts, which is impossible.

After exposing the illusion, the author provides a logical proof that motion cannot exist in quantity and quality because the intermediate states are actually distinct. He considers two cases: a single intermediate (no motion) or multiple intermediates. In the latter case, they must be finite (infinite is impossible), leading to motion composed of indivisible parts—an absurd consequence he proceeds to refute.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof from 'ويدل على نفي الحركة'
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It contains the core argument against motion in discrete categories.
2
Understand the two cases for the middle
The author says the middle between start and end is either one or many. Note the conclusion for each case.
If one, no motion. If many, a dilemma.
3
Grasp the infinite vs finite distinction
If the many are infinite, it's impossible because they are bounded. So they must be finite. But finite leads to composition from indivisibles through an infinite regress argument.
This is a classic kalam argument.
4
See the refutation of composition from indivisibles
The author then shows why composition from indivisibles is false: it implies the existence of an indivisible part (atom) and that slowness is due to rests. He refutes rests using the example of quick and slow motion.
The refutation uses thought experiment.

And composition of motion from what does not admit division is false because it entails the existence of the indivisible part, and that slowness is due to the interpolation of rests. As for the first, [it is false] because of the application of motion to that in which motion occurs. As for the second, because if the quick moves a part, then if the slow always moves the like, they would be equal; if more, it would be faster; if less, it would entail division of what does not divide. Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) refuting the notion of motion in the categories of quantity (al-kamm) and quality (al-kayf). The author begins by explaining the source of the illusion: we observe gradual transition in quantity and quality and mistakenly think it is motion, but true motion requires gradualness without instantaneousness. The transition here is actually a sequence of instantaneous changes (daf'at). The proof then argues that the intermediate stages between the beginning and end are actually distinct (mutamayyiza bi'l-fi'l). If the middle is one, there is no motion. If it is many, the multiplicity must be either infinite or finite. Infinite is impossible because it is bounded between two bounds. Finite entails motion composed of indivisible parts (ma la yaqbal al-qisma), because if we divide it, we repeat the argument, leading to an infinite regress. This composition is false because it implies the existence of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajzza) and that slowness is caused by rests (sakanat) between movements. He then refutes two implications: first, that the motion would apply to something that allows rest, and second, that if the quick moves a part, the slow must either match it (be equal), exceed it (be faster), or fall short (requiring division of the indivisible). The only remaining possibility is rests. He contrasts this with motion in place (al-haraka al-ayniyya), where the distance is a continuous extension admitting infinite divisions. An objection is raised: each finite unit might be infinitely divisible. The answer is that the transition to each unit is instantaneous, and the argument requires the overall extension to be continuous, not discrete. This refutes the mistaken notion that quantity, being intrinsically divisible, is even more fit for motion than distance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily acts of worship, do you perform them as isolated moments or as part of a continuous state of servitude? How does the impossibility of motion composed of indivisible parts challenge the idea that a few good deeds can define your spiritual trajectory?
?

?

Objection: Infinite Divisibility of Finite Units
The author answers an objection and distinguishes motion in place from motion in quantity/quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that each finite unit could be infinitely divisible and the author's response that the transition is instantaneous, along with the contrast with motion in place.

Core Idea
Even if units are finite, they could be infinitely divisible, but the motion is instantaneous per unit, requiring a continuous substrate like distance.

After the proof, an objector suggests that each finite unit between the start and end could itself be infinitely divisible, so motion could be continuous through further subdivisions. The author replies that the transition to each unit is instantaneous, so the motion as a whole remains discontinuous. He then contrasts this with motion in place, where the distance is a continuous extension admitting infinite divisions, making true motion possible there. This also refutes the naive notion that quantity, being intrinsically divisible, is even more fit for motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
The objection starts with 'فإن قيل' and the answer with 'قلنا'. Understand the objection clearly.
2
Analyze the response
The author says the transition to each finite unit is instantaneous (dafʿī). Why does that refute the objection?
Because instantaneous transition means no gradual motion within the unit.
3
Contrast with motion in place
Read the part about al-haraka al-ayniyya. Why is distance different?
Distance is a continuous single extension, not a collection of discrete states.
4
Refute the imagined priority of quantity
Finally, the author refutes the argument that if motion is allowed in distance, it should be more so in quantity. Understand why quantity does not have the same continuous unity.

فههنا يريد نفي الحركة في الكم والكيف مع التنبيه على منشأ توهمهما، وذلك أنا نجد الجسم ينتقل على سبيل التدريج من كمية إلى كمية أخرى أزيد أو أنقص ومن كيفية إلى كيفية أخرى تضاد الأولى أو تماثلها من غير أن يظهر لنا تفاصيل ذلك وأزمنة وجود كل منها فنتوهم أن ذلك حركة إذ لا تعقل من الحركة إلا تغيراً على التدريج لكن لا حركة عند التحقيق لأن معنى التدريج المعتبر في الحركة أن لا يكون دفعة لا بحسب الذات، ولا بحسب الأجزاء والانتقال ههنا إنما هو دفعات يتوهم من اجتماعها التدريج لأن ما بين المبدأ والمنتهى من مراتب الكميات أو الكيفيات متمايزة بالفعل، ينتقل الجسم من كل منها إلى آخر دفعة كما في صيرورة الأرض ماء ثم هواء ثم ناراً مع الاتفاق ذلك ليس حركة جوهرية من الأرض إلى النار لظهور تفاصيل على أن مجموع المراتب وأزمنة وجوداتها ويدل على نفي الحركة في الأمور المتمايزة بالفعل سواء كانت كميات أو كيفيات أو جواهر أن الوسط بين المبدأ والمنتهى إن كان واحداً فظاهر أنه لا حركة، وإن كان كثيراً فتلك الكثرة سواء كان اختلافها بالنوع أو بالعدد أما أن تكون غير متناهية وهو محال ضرورة كونها محصورة بين حاصرين، وإما أن تكون متناهية وهو يستلزم تركب الحركة من أمور لا تقبل القسمة إذ لو انقسمت إلى أمور متغايرة ننقل الكلام إلى كل واحد منها، وهلم جرا، فيكون ما فرض متناهياً غير متناه هذا خلف وتركب الحركة مما لا يقبل الانقسام باطل لاستلزامه وجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وكون البطء لتخلل السكنات. أما الأول فلانطباق الحركة على ما فيه الحركة . أما الثاني فلأن السريع إذا تحرك جزءاً فالبطيء إن تحرك مثله دائماً لزم تساويهما أو أكثر لزم كونه أسرع أو أقل لزم انقسام ما لا ينقسم، فلم يبق إلا أن يكون له فيما بين أجزاء الحركة سكنات . وسيجيء بيان بطلان اللازمين وهذا بخلاف الحركة الأينية، فإن الوسط الذي بين المبدأ والمنتهى. أعني امتداد المسافة واحد بالفعل، يقبل بحسب الفرض انقسامات غير متناهية .

فإن قيل : يجوز أن يكون كل واحد من تلك الآحاد المتناهية قابلاً لانقسامات غير متناهية، فلا يلزم تركب الحركة مما لا تقبل الانقسام .

قلنا : هذا غير مقيد إذ التقدير أن الانتقال إلى كل من تلك الأحاد دفعى والحاصل أن امتناع تركب الحركة مما لا ينقسم يقتضي أن يكون امتدادها منطبقاً على أمر قابل لانقسامات غير متناهية على ما هو شأن لكم واحد كما في الحركة في الماء، أو الأجسام مختلفة كما في الحركة من الأرض إلى السماء لا على كم منفصل متناهي الآحاد سواء كان معروضه جوهراً، أو كماً متصلاً أو كيف أو غير ذلك، وبهذا يندفع ما يتوهم من أنه إذا جازت الحركة في المسافة لكونها معروضة لما يقبل الانقسام لا إلى نهاية، ففي الكم القابل لذلك بحسب ذاته أولى .
Here he intends to negate motion in quantity and quality while drawing attention to the source of their illusion. That is because we find the body gradually transitioning from one quantity to another greater or lesser, and from one quality to another contrary or similar, without the details and times of each appearing to us, so we imagine that this is motion, since motion is only conceived as gradual change. But in truth there is no motion, because the meaning of gradualness considered in motion is that it is not instantaneous neither in essence nor in parts, and the transition here is only instants of which gradualness is imagined, because the intermediate ranks of quantities or qualities between the beginning and end are actually distinct; the body transitions from each to the next instantaneously, as in the transformation of earth into water, then air, then fire—although this is not a substantial motion from earth to fire due to the appearance of details, but the totality of ranks and times of their existence [indicates...]. And it indicates the negation of motion in things that are actually distinct, whether quantities, qualities, or substances, that the middle between the beginning and end, if it is one, obviously there is no motion; if it is many, that multiplicity—whether its difference is by species or number—must either be infinite, which is impossible because it is bounded between two bounds, or finite, which entails composition of motion from indivisible parts, because if it were divided into different things, we would repeat the argument for each one, and so on, so what was assumed finite becomes infinite—this is a contradiction. And composition of motion from what does not admit division is false because it entails the existence of the indivisible part, and that slowness is due to the interpolation of rests. As for the first, [it is false] because of the application of motion to that in which motion occurs. As for the second, because if the quick moves a part, then if the slow always moves the like, they would be equal; if more, it would be faster; if less, it would entail division of what does not divide. So it remains only that it has rests between the parts of motion. And the proof of the falsity of both implications will come. This is unlike motion in place, for the middle between the beginning and end—i.e., the extension of distance—is one in actuality, admitting by assumption infinite divisions.

If it is said: It is possible that each of those finite units is divisible into infinite divisions, so the composition of motion from what does not admit division does not follow.

We reply: This is not valid, for the estimation is that the transition to each of those units is instantaneous. And the gist is that the impossibility of composition of motion from what does not divide requires that its extension corresponds to something susceptible to infinite divisions, as is the case for each one, like motion in water, or the bodies are different as in motion from earth to sky, not upon a discrete quantity with finite units, whether its substrate is substance, or continuous quantity, or quality, or other. And by this is refuted what is imagined, that if motion is permissible in distance because it is a substrate for what admits infinite division, then in quantity that admits that by itself, it is even more appropriate.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) refuting the notion of motion in the categories of quantity (al-kamm) and quality (al-kayf). The author begins by explaining the source of the illusion: we observe gradual transition in quantity and quality and mistakenly think it is motion, but true motion requires gradualness without instantaneousness. The transition here is actually a sequence of instantaneous changes (daf'at). The proof then argues that the intermediate stages between the beginning and end are actually distinct (mutamayyiza bi'l-fi'l). If the middle is one, there is no motion. If it is many, the multiplicity must be either infinite or finite. Infinite is impossible because it is bounded between two bounds. Finite entails motion composed of indivisible parts (ma la yaqbal al-qisma), because if we divide it, we repeat the argument, leading to an infinite regress. This composition is false because it implies the existence of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajzza) and that slowness is caused by rests (sakanat) between movements. He then refutes two implications: first, that the motion would apply to something that allows rest, and second, that if the quick moves a part, the slow must either match it (be equal), exceed it (be faster), or fall short (requiring division of the indivisible). The only remaining possibility is rests. He contrasts this with motion in place (al-haraka al-ayniyya), where the distance is a continuous extension admitting infinite divisions. An objection is raised: each finite unit might be infinitely divisible. The answer is that the transition to each unit is instantaneous, and the argument requires the overall extension to be continuous, not discrete. This refutes the mistaken notion that quantity, being intrinsically divisible, is even more fit for motion than distance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If motion is possible in distance, it is even more possible in quantity because quantity is intrinsically divisible.
Correction: The divisibility of distance is as a continuous extension; quantity in this context is a series of discrete states, so it does not support gradual motion.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say motion in place is different?
A) Because place is not a quantity.
B) Because the distance is a continuous single extension that admits infinite divisions.
C) Because motion in place is slower.
D) Because place is a quality.

Key Takeaway
Motion requires a continuous substrate that admits infinite potential division, which quantity and quality as discrete states lack.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe motion in the world—like a leaf floating from a tree—do you ever consider that it might not be composed of tiny instantaneous jumps? How does this lesson challenge your intuitive understanding of movement, and are you willing to accept a counterintuitive truth for the sake of sound reasoning about Allah's creation?
?
The objection in this lesson seems plausible at first glance. Where in your own faith journey have you accepted a seemingly reasonable argument without examining its deeper implications? Take one such belief you hold and commit to analyzing it with the same rigor shown here.
?
This lesson distinguishes between continuous and discrete motions. Does your concept of Allah's attribute of 'acting' align more with continuous creation or discrete interventions? How does this affect your daily sense of reliance on Him? What change in your heart is needed to embrace the full depth of kalam arguments?
